First argument: specific place leads to preference without preferrer
Sharh al-Mawaqif argues that God cannot be in a specific place as it implies unjustified preference or need for an external specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that God cannot occupy a specific place because it would entail an unjustified preference or a dependence on an external factor.

Core Idea
A specific place for God implies either a groundless preference or a needy dependence.

The mutakallimun argued that since all empty spaces are identical, God's presence in one particular space would be arbitrary unless something external specifies it. This passage presents that argument and responds to three objections, reinforcing the doctrine of divine transcendence beyond spatial location.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Understand the core claim: that there is no difference between places, so assigning God to one is unjustified.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Notice that the argument presents a dilemma: either no external specifier (then it's a preference without a preferrer) or there is an external specifier (then God is needy).
3
Examine the objections and answers
The passage considers three objections: (1) a special relation of God's essence, (2) God's will as specifier, (3) God moving from place to place. See how each is refuted.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the argument ultimately shows God cannot be in any place, reinforcing His transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتكلمين هو الخلاء المتشابه (و) تساوي (نسبته) أي نسبة ذات الواجب ( إليها ) وحينئذ ( فيكون اختصاصه ببعضها دون بعض آخر منها ( ترجيحا بلا مرجح . إن لم يكن هناك مخصص من خارج ) أو يلزم الاحتياج)، أي احتياج الواجب (في تحيزه الذي لا تنفك ذاته عنه إلى الغير إن كان هناك مخصص خارجي . قوله فيكون اختصاصه ببعضها إلخ) قيل لم لا يجوز أن يكون لذاته تعالى نسبة مخصوصة إلى بعض الأحياز أو يكون المخصص هو الإرادة، وأجيب عن الأول بأنه لا مجال لاختلاف النسبة فيما يشابه المنسوب إليه، وعن الثاني بأن استناد التمكن إلى الإادة يوجب حدوثه، والمتمكن يستحيل وجوده بدون المكان كما سبق، والخصم أيضاً معترف به فإن قلت لم لا يجوز أن يكون قبل هذا المكان في مكان آخر إلى نهاية فلا يلزم الانفكاك عن مطلق المكان قلت : الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان لا يكون إلا بالحركة وهذا معلوم ضرورة والحركة حادثة قطعاً، فيلزم كونه تعالي محلاً للحوادث فيلزم حدوثه تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً.
The mutakallimun: it (the place) is similar empty space, and its relation is equal, i.e., the relation of the essence of the Necessary Being to it. Then, that He is particularized to some of them without others is a preference without a preferrer, if there is no external specifier, or else necessitates neediness, i.e., the neediness of the Necessary Being, in His occupying space which His essence is never free of, to another, if there is an external specifier. His statement: 'that He is particularized to some of them without others etc.' It is said: Why is it not permissible that His essence, may He be exalted, has a specific relation to some spaces, or that the specifier is the will? It is answered regarding the first: there is no room for difference in relation when there is similarity in what is related; and regarding the second: that the dependence of spatial occupancy on the will necessitates its temporal origination, and the occupant cannot exist without place as mentioned, and the opponent also admits that. If you say: why is it not permissible that He was in another place before this one, ad infinitum, so that separation from absolute place is not necessary? I say: transition from place to place only occurs through motion, and this is known necessarily, and motion is certainly temporally originated; so He would be a locus of temporal events, necessitating His temporality. Exalted is He above that, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the first argument that God cannot be in a specific place. The argument states: since empty spaces are all equal, God's presence in one particular space would be a groundless preference (ترجيح بلا مرجح) unless an external factor specifies it, which would make God needy. The commentator then addresses objections: first, that God's essence might have a special relation to some spaces—answered by noting relations are uniform across similar items; second, that the will of God could be the specifier—answered because the will would cause the spatial occupancy, making it temporally originated, and God would then be dependent on place. A further objection that God might move from place to place is refuted because motion is temporally originated and would make God a locus of accidents, implying temporality. The passage thus reinforces that God transcends place.

Key Terms
ترجيح بلا مرجح
مخصص
تمكن
حركة
حلول الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the specifier is God's own will, then the problem is avoided.
Correction: The argument shows that even if God's will is the specifier, the spatial occupancy would be temporally originated (since it depends on will), leading to God being dependent on place, which contradicts His necessity.

Checkpoint
What is the core problem if God is in a specific place and there is no external specifier?
A) God would be a body
B) It would be a preference without a preferrer
C) God would be divisible
D) It would necessitate God's nonexistence

Key Takeaway
God's transcendence means He cannot be limited to any specific place, as that would entail an arbitrary preference or dependence on something else.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you subconsciously place Him in a specific ‘direction’ like above the heavens? How does this argument reveal that even that mental image is a form of limiting Him? Will you actively train yourself to think of Him as beyond all place today?
?
If God's will could specify a place for Him, then His will would be temporally originated—meaning it would arise anew. Do you accept that God's will is eternal and unchanging? How does this passage force you to rely on reason to purify your belief about His attributes?
?
This argument shows that God cannot be even in ‘all places’ in a physical sense. How does this affect your understanding of His closeness to you? Can you worship Him with the certainty that He transcends space while still hearing your prayer? What will you change in your prayer to reflect this?

Second Argument: God Cannot Be in All Places
Sharh al-Mawaqif argues that God cannot be in all places as that would entail interpenetration of occupants and mixing with filth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Sharh al-Mawaqif argues that God being in all places would lead to the impossible consequence of interpenetration and association with impurities.

Core Idea
If God were everywhere, He would interpenetrate with bodies and mix with filth—both impossible.

This lesson continues our study of arguments for divine transcendence. Having rejected the possibility of God being in a specific place, the text now examines and refutes the idea that He could be in all places simultaneously. The argument uses simple logical necessity and theological reverence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the argument.
Notice that the passage mentions two separate consequences.
2
Identify the two implications
The passage says that if God were in all places, it would cause interpenetration of occupants and mixing with filth. Underline these two points.
The interpenetration point is elaborated more.
3
Understand the rebuttal of al-Abhari
The commentator refutes al-Abhari's view that interpenetration is only impossible if the two occupants are of the same genus. The text says it's absolutely impossible.
This shows the argument's strength.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this argument is important for establishing God's transcendence beyond physical space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني ) وهو أن يكون في جميع الأحياز ( فلأنه يلزم تداخل المتحيزين لأن بعض الأحياز مشغول بالأجسام ( وإنه ) أي تداخل المتحيزين مطلقاً ( محال بالضروة وأيضاً فيلزم ) على التقدير الثاني ( مخالطته لقاذورات العالم تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً. قوله : (أي تداخل المتحيزين مطلقاً) أشار بقوله مطلقاً إلى رد ما ذكره الأبهري من أن استحالة تداخل المتحيزين فيما إذا كانا من جنس واحد وهاهنا ليس كذلك.
And as for the second (possibility) – that He is in all spaces – it would necessitate the interpenetration of occupants, because some spaces are occupied by bodies. And that, i.e., interpenetration of occupants absolutely, is impossible by necessity. Also it would necessitate, on the second assumption, His mixing with the filths of the world – exalted is He above that, a great exaltation. His statement: 'i.e., interpenetration of occupants absolutely' – he indicated by his saying 'absolutely' a refutation of what al-Abhari mentioned, that the impossibility of interpenetration is only when they are of one genus, but here it is not so.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument that God cannot be in all places. If He occupied every location, He would necessarily interpenetrate with other occupants (bodies) because some spaces are already filled. Interpenetration of two occupants is absolutely impossible, as the commentator emphasizes by 'مطلقاً' (absolutely), rejecting al-Abhari's view that it is only impossible when the occupants are of the same genus. Furthermore, it would entail God mixing with impure substances of the world, which is beneath Him. Thus, both alternatives (specific place or all places) are impossible.

Key Terms
تداخل المتحيزين
مخالطة
جنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Interpenetration is only impossible if the two occupants are of the same genus.
Correction: The passage states interpenetration is impossible absolutely (مطلقاً), regardless of genus.

Checkpoint

B) Interpenetration of occupants and mixing with filth
C) God would be a body
D) God would have parts

Key Takeaway
God cannot be in all places because it would necessitate impossible interpenetration and association with impurities.

?

For example, do you sometimes think of God as everywhere physically while also knowing He is transcendent? This lesson demands consistency. Are you ready to uproot any hidden anthropomorphism in your conception of God, no matter how small?

The Fourth Argument: God Cannot Be Spatial
Sharh al-Mawaqif's fourth argument against God being spatial via substance category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument from Sharh al-Mawaqif that if God were spatial, He would be a substance, leading to an indivisible atom or a composite body, both impossible for God.

Core Idea
God cannot occupy space because that would make Him a substance, which leads to absurdity.

This lesson examines the fourth argument from Sharh al-Mawaqif, which uses the concepts of substance and accident to show that if God were spatial, He would have to be a substance. Then it shows that a substance must be either indivisible (an atom) or divisible (a body), both of which are incompatible with God's perfection and necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its background
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: if spatial → substance → either indivisible or divisible → both false.
This argument builds on the earlier conclusion that God cannot be an accident.
2
Understand the dilemma
Identify the two horns: (1) if indivisible, God would be an indivisible atom, which is the basest of beings; (2) if divisible, God would be a body, which involves composition and temporality.
Both outcomes are incompatible with God's necessary existence.
3
Consider the objection and its refutation
The objection: what if God is divisible hypothetically but not externally? The refutation: if the hypothesis matches reality, it implies composition; if not, it is irrelevant.
This closes the loophole that could have saved the spatial view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع لو كان ( متحيزاً لكان (جوهراً ) لاستحالة كون الواجب تعالى عرضاً وإذا كان جوهراً ( فأما أن لا ينقسم ) أصلا ( أو ينقسم وكلاهما باطل . أما الأول فلأنه يكون جزءاً لا يتجزأ وهو أحقر الأشياء تعالى الله عن ذلك. وأما الثاني فلأنه يكون جسماً وكل جسم مركب وقد مر أنه ( أي التركيب الخارجي ) ينافي الوجوب الذاتي، وأيضاً قد بينا أن كل جسم محدث فيلزم حدوث الواجب . قوله : ( لكان جوهر الاستحالة كون الواجب تعالى (عرضا المراد بالجوهر هاهنا هو المتحيز القائم بنفسه وبالعرض هو المتحيز القائم بغيره ومن البين أنه لا واسطة بينهما بعد تحقق التحيز وأنه يكفي في استلزام كون شيء أحقر الأشياء كونه متحيزاً قائماً بنفسه غير قابل للقسمة أصلاً غاية ما في الباب على تقدير أخذ الإمكان في مفهوم الجزء الغير المتجزئ أن يكون إطلاق الجزء بالمعنى المتعارف عليه مسامحة . قوله : ( فلأنه حينئذ يكون جزءاً لا يتجزأ) فإن قلت يجوز أن يكون يقبل الانقسام فرضاً لا خارجاً فلا يلزم كونه جوهراً فرداً ولا أن يكون جسماً ، قلت : إن طابق الفرض الواقع بأن يكون فيه شيء غير في الواقع يلزم أن لا يكون جزءاً لا يتجزأ، بل جسماً مركباً من شيئين أو أكثر، لانتفاء الواسطة على الفرض وإن لم يطابق فلا عبرة فلا ينافي كونه جزءاً لا يتجزئ وأحقر الأشياء كما حققناه في موقف الاعراض.
The fourth: If He were occupying space, He would be a substance, because it is impossible for the Necessary Being to be an accident. And if He is a substance, then either He is indivisible or divisible – both are false. As for the first, He would be an indivisible atom, and that is the basest of things – exalted is God above that. As for the second, He would be a body, and every body is composite, and it has passed that composition (i.e., external composition) is incompatible with necessary existence. Also we have shown that every body is temporally originated, so the temporality of the Necessary Being would follow. His statement: 'He would be a substance – the impossibility of the Necessary Being being an accident.' By 'substance' here is meant an occupying entity subsistent by itself, and by 'accident' an occupying entity subsistent by another. It is clear that there is no intermediate between them after establishing occupancy. And it suffices for something to be considered the basest of things that it is an occupant subsistent by itself not divisible at all. At best, on the assumption of taking possibility in the concept of the indivisible atom, the application of 'atom' in the conventional sense is a leniency. His statement: 'for then He would be an indivisible atom' – if you say: it is possible that He accepts division hypothetically but not externally, so He would not be an individual substance nor a body. I say: if the hypothesis corresponds to reality, namely that there is something else in reality, then it would not be an indivisible atom but a body composed of two or more things, because there is no intermediate in the hypothesis; if it does not correspond, it is disregarded, and it does not contradict His being an indivisible atom and the basest of things, as we verified in the locus of accidents.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument: if God occupied space, He would have to be a substance (جوهر), meaning an entity occupying space and subsisting by itself, because being an accident is impossible for God. As a substance, He is either indivisible (an atom) or divisible (a body). Both are absurd: an indivisible atom is the lowest of beings, while a body involves composition and temporal origination, contradicting necessary existence. The commentator clarifies that substance and accident are the only categories for an occupant, with no intermediate. He also addresses an objection that God might be divisible hypothetically but not externally: if the hypothesis corresponds to reality, it implies composition; if not, the hypothesis is irrelevant, and the indivisible atom remains base. Thus, God cannot be spatial.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
جزء لا يتجزأ
جسم
تركيب
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could be spatially extended but only hypothetically divisible, avoiding the dilemma.
Correction: Either the hypothesis corresponds to reality (then He is composite) or it does not (then the hypothesis is irrelevant and He remains an indivisible atom, which is base).

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, why is it impossible for God to be an indivisible atom?
A) Because atoms are temporal
B) Because an atom is the basest of things
C) Because atoms are composite
D) Because atoms are accidents

Key Takeaway
God cannot occupy space because that would force Him into being a substance, leading either to the baseness of an indivisible atom or the composition and temporality of a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is with me,' do you secretly imagine a spatial presence? This lesson proves any spatial concept of God collapses into absurdity. How will you actively purify your mind of spatial imagery when thinking of your Lord starting today?
?
The argument shows that treating God as an indivisible atom reduces Him to the basest of things. Yet how often do you reduce Allah to a mental image that fits your limited understanding? What concrete step will you take to remind yourself that He is beyond all categories?
?
If God cannot be contained by space, then He is closer to you than your jugular vein without being physically near. Does this truth change how you seek Him in your daily life? What will you do differently today to connect with a God who transcends location?

Refuting the Body Argument for Multiple Gods
Sharh al-Mawaqif argues then refutes the body argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if God were a body, each part would possess independent attributes leading to multiple gods, and why this argument is considered weak because a single attribute can attach to the whole.

Core Idea
A single attribute can belong to the whole, so multiple gods do not follow.

In this passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif, the author presents a common argument against God being a body: each part would need its own attributes, implying multiple gods. However, he then refutes this argument, showing its weakness. We'll explore both the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of argument and refutation.
2
Identify the argument
Notice how the arguer claims that if God were a body, each part would have distinct attributes, leading to multiple gods.
Key phrases: 'لو كان جسماً لقام بكل جزء منه علم وقدرة وحياة مغايرة'
3
Examine the counter-argument
See how the arguer is forced to say a human is multiple knowledges etc. to avoid the same problem.
This shows the argument is self-defeating.
4
Understand why the argument is weak
The commentator dismisses it because a single attribute can inhere in the whole as a whole, not requiring separate instances in each part.
Key phrase: 'لجواز قيام الصفة الواحدة بالمجموع من حيث هو مجموع'
5
Reflect on the concept
Consider how this argument relates to God's transcendence and the nature of divine attributes.
God is not a body, so attributes belong to Him as a whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقال ) في إبطال الثاني (لو كان ( الواجب ( جسماً لقام بكل جزء منه ( علم وقدرة ) وحياة مغايرة لما قام بالجزء الآخر ضرورة امتناع قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين، فيكون كل واحد من أجزائه مستقلاً بكل واحد من صفات الكمال ( فيلزم تعدد الآلهة وهذا المستدل يلتزم أن الإنسان الواحد علماء قادرون أحياء كيلا ينقض دليله بالإنسان الواحد لجريانه فيه، وهذا الاستدلال ضعيف جداً لجواز قيام الصفة الواحدة بالمجموع من حيث هو مجموع فلا يلزم ما ذكر من المحذور.
And it may be said, in refuting the second (possibility): if the Necessary Being were a body, there would inhere in each part of Him knowledge, power, and life distinct from what inheres in the other part, necessarily because the same accident cannot inhere in two loci. Thus each of His parts would be independent with each of the attributes of perfection, leading to multiplicity of gods. And this arguer commits himself that a single human being is knowledges, powers, lives – lest his proof be refuted by the human being because it would apply to him as well. But this argument is very weak because it is possible for a single attribute to attach to the whole as a whole, so the mentioned difficulty does not follow.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques an argument that if God were a body, each part would have its own knowledge, power, and life, since a single accident cannot inhere in two loci. This would lead to multiple gods. However, the argument is self-defeating because it would also apply to a human being, who would then be multiple beings. The arguer attempts to avoid this by positing that a single human has multiple 'knowledges' etc., but this is contrived. The commentator dismisses the argument as weak (ضعيف جداً) because a single attribute can inhere in the whole as a whole, not requiring separate instances in each part. Thus the multiplicity of gods does not follow.

محل
صفات الكمال
تعدد الآلهة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God had parts, each part must have its own separate attributes.
Correction: A single attribute can inhere in the whole as a whole, so separate attributes are not necessary.

Checkpoint

A) Because it implies God is composite
B) Because a single attribute can attach to the whole
C) Because bodies are temporal
D) Because it leads to multiplicity of gods

Key Takeaway
The argument from body for multiple gods is weak because attributes can attach to the whole as a whole, so no multiplicity follows.

The Argument from Equality: Does Spatiality Imply Similarity?
The commentator refutes the argument from equality against God being spatial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from equality against God being spatial is not decisive because it relies on an unproven assumption of similarity.

Core Idea
Equality in space does not prove similarity in essence.

This lesson examines a key argument used to reject spatiality for God, showing how it hinges on a hidden assumption. We'll see why equality in spatial occupation doesn't automatically imply essential similarity, and why the argument ultimately fails. Let's dive into the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow before interpreting.
2
Identify the main claim
The argument states: if God were spatial (mutehayyiz), He would be equal to all other spatial beings in essence, leading to either bodies being pre-eternal or God being temporal.
Notice the logical conditional: if spatial → equal → similar → contradiction.
3
Spot the hidden assumption
The argument assumes that all spatial beings are similar in essence (تماثل). The commentator points out that this assumption is not proven, and equality in space does not automatically give similarity.
The basis of similarity among bodies is composition from similar atoms, not spatial occupancy.
4
Understand the refutation
The commentator gives two refutations: 1) equality in space ≠ similarity; 2) even if essence were shared, pre-eternity and temporality are external properties that need not be shared. Additionally, proving that spatiality entails composition from homogeneous atoms is extremely difficult.
The phrase 'دونه خرط القتاد' means it's extremely difficult.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقال : ( في نفي المكان عنه تعالى ( لو كان متحيزاً لكان مساوياً لسائر المتحيزات ) في الماهية ( فيلزم ) حينئذ ( إما قدم الأجسام أو حدوثه، لأن المتماثلات تتوافق في الأحكام (وهو) أي هذا الاستدلال ( بناء على تماثل الأجسام بل على تماثل المتحيزات بالذات، قوله : ( بناء على تماثل (الأجسام فإن قلت لا يلزم من المساواة في الحصول في الحيز الاتحاد في الماهية حتى يثبت التماثل، قلت : مبنى تماثل الأجسام تركبها من الجواهر المتماثلة لا تساويها في الحصول في الحيز، نعم يتجه أنه لا يلزم من الاتحاد في الماهية الاتحاد في القدم والحدوث فإنهما من اللوازم الخارجية كما سيصرح به الشارح فيما سيأتي . قوله : (بل على تماثل (المتحيزات وجه الإضراب أن هذا هو المناسب لقوله لكان مساوياً لسائر المتحيزات ، وأما البناء على تماثل الأجسام فقط ففيه أن تماثلها سواء بناء على ما ذكرته لا يستلزم تماثل المتجيزات فلا يلزم الموافقة في الأحكام فلا يصح قوله فيلزم حينئذ إما قدم الأجسام أو حدوها؛ اللهم إلا أن يقال عدم كونه تعالى جزاً لا يتجزئ ثابت بالاتفاق فلو كان متحيزاً لكان جسماً مساوياً لسائر الأجسام المتحيزة في التركب من الجواهر المتماثلة، فيلزم حينئذ أحد الأمرين المذكورين وأنت خبير بأن إثبات استلزام التحيز للتركب من الجواهر المتجانسة دونه خرط القتاد .
And it may be said, in negating place from Him, exalted: if He were spatial, He would be equal to all other spatial beings in essence, then it would follow either the pre-eternity of bodies or His temporality, because similar things agree in their properties. And this argument is based on the similarity of bodies, rather on the similarity of spatial beings in essence. His statement: 'based on the similarity of bodies' – if you say: equality in occupying space does not necessitate unity of essence so as to establish similarity. I say: the basis of similarity of bodies is their composition from similar atoms, not their equality in spatial occupation. Yes, it is clear that unity of essence does not necessitate unity in pre-eternity and temporality, for these are external concomitants, as the commentator will explain later. His statement: 'rather on the similarity of spatial beings' – the purpose of the correction is that this is appropriate to his saying 'equal to all spatial beings.' As for building only on the similarity of bodies, even if based on what you mentioned, it does not necessitate the similarity of spatial beings, so agreement in properties does not follow, so his statement 'then follows either the pre-eternity of bodies or their temporality' is invalid. Unless it is said that it is agreed that He is not an indivisible atom, so if He were spatial He would be a body equal to other spatial bodies in composition from similar atoms, then one of the two mentioned things would follow. But you are aware that establishing that spatiality necessitates composition from homogeneous atoms is extremely difficult.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents the argument from equality: if God were spatial, He would share the same essence as all spatial beings, thus either bodies would be pre-eternal or God would be temporal, since similar things have the same properties. The commentator notes that this argument rests on the assumption that all spatial beings are similar (تماثل), which is not proven. The basis of similarity among bodies is their composition from similar atoms, not just spatial occupancy. Even if bodies are similar, spatial beings in general may not be similar. He points out that even if essence is shared, pre-eternity and temporality are external concomitants that need not be shared. The commentator further clarifies that if one insists that God is not an indivisible atom, then being spatial would make Him a body composed of similar atoms, leading to the contradiction, but proving that spatiality entails composition from homogeneous atoms is extremely difficult (دونه خرط القتاد). Thus the argument is not decisive.

Key Terms
تماثل
مساواة
جوهر فرد
تركيب
لازم خارجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equality in occupying space implies similarity in essence.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that similarity of bodies is based on composition from similar atoms, not spatial equality.

Checkpoint

A) Equality in spatial occupancy
B) Composition from similar atoms
C) Being temporal
D) Being composite

Key Takeaway
The argument from equality fails because equality in space does not entail essential similarity, and even if it did, properties like pre-eternity and temporality are not necessarily shared.

The Argument from Composition and Its Refutation
Argument that spatiality implies composition, refuted by principle that sharing accidents does not compose essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that spatiality would imply composition in God and why the commentator refutes it by distinguishing between sharing accidents and composition.

Core Idea
Sharing attributes does not necessitate composition of essence.

The commentator presents a skeptical argument: if God were spatial, He would share spatiality with bodies, but differ in other ways, leading to composition. However, this argument is swiftly refuted by recalling a principle established earlier: sharing accidents does not necessitate composition. This lesson examines the logical structure of this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the formal structure: a conditional statement leading to a conclusion, followed by a refutation.
Notice the phrase 'ربما يقال' which introduces a potential objection.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The argument assumes that if God is spatial, He equals bodies in spatiality but must differ in something else; from this, composition follows. Find these parts.
The conclusion is 'يلزم التركيب'.
3
Analyze the refutation
The commentator refers to an earlier point: sharing accidents does not necessitate composition. Understand why this refutes the argument.
The key phrase is 'الاشتراك والتساوي في العوارض لا يستلزم التركيب'.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why this principle is important for divine simplicity. What would happen if sharing any attribute implied composition?
This principle safeguards the oneness of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقال لو كان متحيزاً لساوى الأجسام في التحيز ولا بد من أن يخالفها بغيره فيلزم التركيب) في ذاته، (وقد علمت في صدر الكتاب (ما فيه وهو أن الاشتراك والتساوي في العوارض لا يستلزم التركيب
And it may be said: if He were spatial, He would equal bodies in spatiality, and necessarily differ from them in something else, thus composition in His essence would follow. And you have known from the beginning of the book what is in it – namely that sharing and equality in accidents does not necessitate composition.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This very brief passage presents an argument that if God were spatial, He would share spatiality with bodies but must differ in something else, resulting in a composite essence. However, the commentator dismisses this by referring to an earlier point established in the book: sharing accidents does not necessitate composition of the essence. Thus the argument is not valid.

Correction: Sharing accidents (like spatiality) does not imply composition of essence, as the commentator states.

Opponent's Rational Proof for Spatiality and Its Refutation
Opponent's first rational proof for spatiality and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that reason necessarily demands a spatial location for every existent is rejected by Ash'ari theologians, and how they refute it by distinguishing rational necessity from estimative judgment.

Core Idea
Reason does not force spatiality; the estimative faculty does, and it cannot dictate the nature of non-sensibles.

In this passage, the opponent argues that it is a rational necessity that every existent occupies space or is in a spatial being. The theologian responds by denying this necessity, showing that it stems from the estimative faculty which only applies to material things. By examining examples like the universal man and our knowledge, we can conceive non-spatial existents, and the consensus of scholars further undermines the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Understand the argument that reason necessarily judges every existent as either occupying space or inhering in an occupant.
2
Identify the refutation
Notice that the answer denies this as rational necessity and attributes it to the estimative faculty (wahm), which is not accepted for non-sensible things.
3
Examine the counterexamples
Look at the examples of the universal man and our knowledge: both are existent yet not spatial.
This helps conceive a non-spatial existent.
4
Consider the consensus argument
The text notes that if the judgment were necessary, the great multitude (except Hanbalis and Karramiyya) could not disagree; their disagreement shows it is not necessary.
This is a dialectical proof.
5
Internalize the key distinction
Differentiate between rational necessity (universal and incontrovertible) and estimative judgment (limited to sensible objects).
This distinction is crucial in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم على إثبات الجهة والمكان (بوجوه) خمسة. ( الأول ضرورة العقل ) أي بديهته ) تجزم بأن كل موجود فهو متحيز أو حال فيه ليكون مختصاً بجهة ومكان إما أصالة أو تبعاً، والجواب منع الضرورة العقلية وإنما ذلك حكم الوهم ( بضرورته ) وإنه غير مقبول ) فيما ليس بمحسوس، ( وربما يستعان في تصوره أي تصور موجود لا حيز له أصلا ) بالإنسان الكلي ) المشترك بين أفراده، ( وعلمنا به فإنهما موجودان وليسا متحيزين قطعاً. أما الأول فلانه لو كان متحيزاً أو قوله: (والجواب منع الضرورة العقلية كيف ولو كان ضرورياً لامتنع إطباق الجمع العظيم، وهم ما سوى الحنابلة والكرامية على خلافه .
The opponent argued to establish direction and place with five proofs. First: the necessity of reason, i.e., its intuition, judges that every existent is either an occupant or inhering in an occupant, so as to be specific to a direction and place, either essentially or derivatively. The answer: denial of the rational necessity; rather that is a judgment of the estimative faculty by its necessity, and it is not accepted concerning what is not sensible. And perhaps one may seek help in conceiving an existent with no space at all by the universal man common to his individuals, and our knowledge of it; for both are existent and definitely not spatial. As for the first, if it were spatial or... His statement: 'The answer: denial of the rational necessity' – how so? For if it were necessary, it would be impossible for the great multitude to unanimously agree, while they – except the Hanbalis and Karramiyya – are upon the contrary.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's first argument: reason necessarily judges that every existent is spatial (either an occupant or inhering in one). The answer denies this necessity, attributing it instead to the estimative faculty (الوهم), which only applies to sensible objects. To help conceive a non-spatial existent, one may consider the universal man (الإنسان الكلي) and our knowledge of it, which are existent yet not spatial. The commentator adds that if the judgment were truly necessary, it would be impossible for such a large group (the majority of Muslims) to disagree; only the Hanbalis and Karramiyya hold that God is in a direction, while others deny it. Thus, the claim of necessity is false.

Key Terms
ضرورة العقل
وهم
الإنسان الكلي
حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something exists, it must be in a place; reason compels this.
Correction: Reason does not compel spatiality for all existents; only the estimative faculty does, and it is limited to sensible objects. Non-sensible realities like mental concepts and knowledge are existent yet non-spatial.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator argue against the claim that reason necessarily judges every existent as spatial?
A) By proving that God is a body
B) By saying the judgment is from the estimative faculty and rejected for non-sensibles
C) By denying that anything exists without space
D) By asserting that reason is unreliable

Key Takeaway
The claim of rational necessity for spatiality is false; it is an estimative judgment that does not apply to non-sensible realities, as shown by counterexamples and scholarly consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have never questioned why you instinctively think of God as being 'somewhere' above the heavens. This lesson reveals that instinct is not rational necessity but the estimative faculty imposing a spatial framework. In what areas of your life do you confuse familiarity with rational necessity? ?

The Similarity Objection to God's Location
The author presents and refutes the argument that God's being located would make Him similar to other bodies, implying either pre-eternity or temporality of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against God's being located, which argues from similarity of located things, and why it fails due to an unproven assumption.

Core Idea
Objections based on similarity assume what needs to be proven.

This lesson examines a second objection to the Ash'ari position that God is not in a place. The objection claims that if God were located, He would be similar to other bodies, forcing an unacceptable conclusion about the pre-eternity or temporality of all bodies. The refutation, however, reveals that the argument hinges on an unproven assumption of essential similarity among located things.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Carefully read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice how the objection is structured as a conditional statement: if located, then equal in quiddity, then similar judgments, leading to a dilemma.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بناء على تماثل الأجسام بل على تماثل المتحيزات بالذات' which hints at the refutation.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the objection into its premises and conclusion. What is the key premise about similarity?
3
Understand the refutation
The text states that the reasoning is 'based on the similarity of bodies' and even more specifically 'on the similarity of located things in essence.' This points to a hidden assumption: that all located things share an essential nature.
If that assumption is not granted, the argument collapses.
4
Reflect on significance
Consider why this matters for the doctrine of divine transcendence. The objection tries to force a choice between pre-eternity and temporality, but both are incompatible with God's uniqueness.
The refutation shows that such objections rely on unwarranted comparisons between God and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقال في نفي المكان عنه تعالى: لو كان متحيزاً لكان مساوياً لسائر المتحيزات في الماهية فيلزم حينئذ إما قدم الأجسام أو حدوثه، لأن المتماثلات تتوافق في الأحكام، وهو هذا الاستدلال بناء على تماثل الأجسام بل على تماثل المتحيزات بالذات.
And it may be said, in negating place from Him exalted: If He were located, He would be equal to other located things in quiddity, and then it would necessitate either the pre-eternity of bodies or their temporal origination, because similars agree in judgments. This reasoning is based on the similarity of bodies, rather on the similarity of located things in essence.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its refutation. The objection argues: if God were located (mutahayyiz), He would be equal in quiddity to other located things. Since similars agree in judgments, either all bodies are pre-eternal (if God is pre-eternal) or all are temporally originated (if God is originated), but both are problematic. The refutation, noted by the commentator, is that this reasoning depends on the similarity of bodies—specifically, on the similarity of located things in essence. If that similarity is not granted, the conclusion does not follow. The phrase 'rather on the similarity of located things in essence' emphasizes that the argument requires a stronger claim than mere bodily similarity.

Key Terms
متحيز
الماهية
تماثل
قدم
حدوث
أجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God were located, He would necessarily be similar to other bodies in all respects, including essential properties.
Correction: The refutation shows that this assumes all located things share the same essence, which is not granted. God's uniqueness precludes such unqualified comparison.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the second objection as identified in the refutation?
A) The necessity of God being in a place.
B) The similarity of located things in quiddity.
C) The pre-eternity of bodies.
D) The temporal origination of bodies.

Key Takeaway
The objection from similarity fails because it assumes, without proof, that all located things share an identical essence, which the Ash'ari tradition rejects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that because someone shares one characteristic with you, they must be like you in all important ways? How can this lesson on the danger of assuming essential similarity help you avoid judging others unfairly? Will you commit to examining your assumptions before making such judgments?
?
The objector's argument seemed logical but rested on an unproven premise. What unexamined premises might you be holding about God that affect how you understand His transcendence? Are you ready to subject those premises to scrutiny?
?
If the failure of this argument reminds you that human reasoning can be flawed, how does that affect your reliance on rational proofs in matters of faith? Will you humbly accept that your reasoning may need correction, and seek knowledge from reliable sources?

Third Objection: Location Imposes Composition and Its Refutation
The author presents and refutes a third objection against divine location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God's location would necessitate composition in His essence is invalid based on the principle that sharing accidents does not necessitate composition.

Core Idea
Sharing accidents does not force composition in essence.

The third objection argues that if God were located, He would be equal to bodies in location and would require something else to differ, leading to composition. This lesson examines the objection and its refutation, revealing a key principle about accidental attributes not necessitating essential composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the logical structure: if located -> equal in location -> need difference -> composition.
2
Understand the implied syllogism
Identify the premises and conclusion of the objection: (1) God would be located, (2) location makes Him equal to bodies, (3) He must differ by something else, (4) therefore composition in essence.
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation references an earlier principle: sharing equality in accidents does not necessitate composition. Consider how this undermines the objection.
The objection assumes that sharing location forces composition, but that assumption is false.
4
Apply the principle
Reflect on why accidents (like location) do not affect essential composition. This safeguards God's transcendence.
This connects to the broader theme of divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقال: لو كان متحيزاً لساوى الأجسام في التحيز ولا بد من أن يخالفها بغيره فيلزم التركيب في ذاته، وقد علمت في صدر الكتاب ما فيه وهو أن الاشتراك والتساوي في العوارض لا يستلزم التركيب.
And it may be said: If He were located, He would equal bodies in location, and it is necessary that He differ from them by something else, so composition in His essence would follow. You have already learned earlier in the book what is in it, namely that sharing and equality in accidents does not necessitate composition.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection and its refutation. The objection argues: if God were located, He would be equal to bodies in location (a common attribute), so He must differ by something else, which would make His essence composite. The refutation references an earlier discussion in the book: sharing and equality in accidents does not necessitate composition in essence. Thus the objection is invalid because the difference required could be in essential attributes that do not entail composition, or the equality in a mere accident does not force composition.

Key Terms
متحيز
تركيب
عوارض
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing any attribute with creation forces composition in essence.
Correction: The author clarifies that sharing accidental attributes does not necessitate composition, only sharing essential attributes might.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the third objection fail?
A) Because God cannot be located.
B) Because equality in accidents does not necessitate composition.
C) Because bodies are not similar to God.
D) Because composition is impossible for any being.

Key Takeaway
The third objection fails because the principle that shared accidents require composition is false; accidental attributes do not affect essential composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

Will you let this truth sink into your heart so that you trust Him more completely?

The First Argument: Rational Necessity for Every Existent to be Located
The opponent argues from rational necessity that every existent is either a body or a state in a body; the author denies this and provides counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument from rational necessity that every existent must be located or inhering, and the author's refutation that distinguishes rational judgment from imagination, using the universal man and knowledge as examples.

Core Idea
Rational necessity in non-sensible matters is actually imagination.

The opponent argues that reason dictates every existent must occupy space or be in something. The author denies this, calling it a judgment of imagination. To help conceive a non-located being, he offers the universal man and our knowledge of it as examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Let the flow of the argument settle in.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ضرورة العقل' — it means 'rational necessity'.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that reason necessarily judges every existent to be either a body (mutahayyiz) or a state in a body. This is the foundation for arguing that God must have a direction or place.
3
Understand the author's denial
The author denies that this is a rational necessity. Instead, it is a judgment of the imagination (wahm) which is not valid for non-sensible realities.
The key distinction: imagination vs. reason.
4
Examine the counterexamples
The universal man (insan kulli) and our knowledge of the universal are both existent yet not located. The author explains why they cannot be located: the universal cannot have specific magnitude or position.
Notice the subtlety: the universal man has organs but taken universally, not as particular.
5
Grasp the caution about 'aid' vs. 'proof'
The author says these examples are only aids to conception, not proofs, because the existence of the natural universal is disputed. Yet they still help in understanding the possibility of a non-located existent.
This shows the author's methodological care.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم على إثبات الجهة والمكان بوجوه خمسة. الأول: ضرورة العقل أي بديهته تجزم بأن كل موجود فهو متحيز أو حال فيه ليكون مختصاً بجهة ومكان إما أصالة أو تبعاً. والجواب: منع الضرورة العقلية، وإنما ذلك حكم الوهم بضرورته، وإنه غير مقبول فيما ليس بمحسوس. وربما يستعان في تصوره أي تصور موجود لا حيز له أصلا بالإنسان الكلي المشترك بين أفراده، وعلمنا به، فإنهما موجودان وليسا متحيزين قطعاً. أما الأول: فلأنه لو كان متحيزاً أو حالاً فيه لاختص بمقدار معين ووضع مخصوص، فلا يطابق أفراداً متباينة المقادير والأوضاع، فلا يكون مشتركاً بينها. وأما الثاني: فلأن العلم بالماهية الكلية لا يختص بمقدار ووضع مخصوصين، وإلا لم يكن علماً بتلك الماهية. فإن قلت: الإنسان المشترك لا بد أن يكون له أعضاء مخصوصة من عين ويد وظهر وبطن وغيرها على أوضاع مختلفة ومقادير متناسبة وأبعاد متفاوتة، ولا شك في أنه من حيث هو يكون متحيزاً. قلت: هذا إنما يلزم إذا لم توجد تلك الأعضاء من حيث أنها كلية مشتركة، ولا شبهة أنها في الإنسان الكلي مأخوذة كذلك. وإنما قال: وربما يستعان في تصوره ولم يقل: وربما يستدل عليه؛ لأن الاستدلال به موقوف على وجود الكلي الطبيعي ووجود العلم به في الخارج مع أنه مختلف فيه، بخلاف الاستعانة المذكورة فإنها تتم مع ذلك الاختلاف.
The opponent argued for establishing direction and place by five arguments. The first: the necessity of reason, i.e., its self-evidence, judges that every existent is either a body or a state in a body, so that it is specific to a direction and place either primarily or secondarily. The answer: denial of rational necessity; rather that is a judgment of the imagination with its necessity, and it is not accepted for what is not sensible. And perhaps one may seek aid in conceiving it, i.e., conceiving an existent with no location at all, by the universal man common among his individuals, and our knowledge of him, for they are both existent and definitely not located. As for the first: if it were located or a state in it, it would be specific to a determinate magnitude and particular position, so it would not correspond to individuals of different magnitudes and positions, so it would not be common among them. As for the second: because knowledge of the universal quiddity is not specific to a determinate magnitude and particular position; otherwise it would not be knowledge of that quiddity. If you say: The universal man must have specific organs—eye, hand, back, belly, etc.—in different positions and proportionate magnitudes and varying dimensions, and there is no doubt that in terms of itself it is located. I say: This only follows if those organs are not taken as universal common; there is no doubt that they are taken as such in the universal man. And he said 'perhaps one may seek aid in conceiving it' and did not say 'perhaps one may argue from it' because arguing from it depends on the existence of the natural universal and the existence of knowledge of it in the external world, which is disputed, unlike the mentioned seeking aid which is complete despite that dispute.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's first argument and the author's detailed refutation. The opponent claims a rational necessity that every existent is either a body (mutahayyiz) or a state in a body, thus God must be located. The author denies this necessity, stating it is actually a judgment of the imagination (wahm) that does not apply to non-sensibles. To aid in conceiving a non-located existent, he provides two examples: the universal man (al-insan al-kulli) and our knowledge of it. Both are existent yet not located. The universal man is universal, so it cannot have a specific magnitude or position; otherwise it wouldn't be common to all individuals. Knowledge is also not specific to magnitude or position. A potential objection that the universal man has organs is dismissed because those organs are taken universally, not as particular. The author notes that he speaks of 'aid in conceiving' rather than 'proof' because the existence of the natural universal is disputed, but the concept still helps in understanding the possibility of a non-located existent.

Key Terms
جهة
مکان
ضرورة العقل
وهم
إنسان كلي
ماهية كلية
استعانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every existent must be located or inhering because reason demands it.
Correction: That is a judgment of imagination, not rational necessity; non-sensible beings need not be located.

Checkpoint
Why does the author use the phrase 'seek aid in conceiving' rather than 'prove' when referring to the universal man and knowledge?
A) Because they are not actual existents.
B) Because the existence of the natural universal is disputed.
C) Because they are not located.
D) Because they are not real.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's claim of rational necessity is actually a product of imagination, and the universal man and knowledge serve as conceptual aids, not proofs.

?

The Opponent's Argument from Connection and Separation
The opponent poses a dichotomy and the author denies its applicability to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's second argument that God must be located because any two existents either connect or separate, and the author's refutation that this dichotomy is an imaginary judgment that does not apply to non-sensibles.

Core Idea
Imaginary dichotomies do not apply to the Divine.

The opponent presents a dichotomy: any two existents either connect or separate. If God connects to the world, He is located; if He is separate, He is also located. The author rejects the dichotomy as an imaginary judgment, which may resemble a self-evident truth but is not valid for non-sensibles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the opponent's claim and the author's concise response.
The passage is short but packed with technical terms.
2
Identify the dichotomy and its consequence
The opponent says every two existents either connect or separate. If God connects to the world, He is located; if separate, He is also located. This forces location on God.
3
Understand the refutation
The author denies the dichotomy, classifying it as an imaginary judgment (hukm wahmi) that does not apply to non-sensibles. Imaginary judgments often resemble self-evident truths, so they are misleading.
Imaginary judgments are based on sensory illusions, not rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: كل موجودين فأما أن يتصلا أو ينفصلا. فهو أي الواجب تعالى إن كان متصلا بالعالم فمتحيز، وإن كان منفصلاً عنه فكذلك. والجواب: منع الحصر وهو من الطراز الأول أي من الأحكام الوهمية، وقد عرفت أن أحكامه لا تقبل في غير المحسوسات لكنها قد تشبه بالأوليات فتحسب أنها منها. الثالث: أنه إما داخل العالم أو خارج العالم أو لا داخله ولا خارجه، والثالث خروج عن المعقول وعما تقتضيه بداهة العقل، والأولان فيهما المطلوب. قوله: (فإما أن يتصلا أو ينفصلا) قيل لو زيد عليه قوله أولا هذا ولا ذاك وهو خروج عن المعقول كما فعله في الثالث لكان أحسن والأمر فيه سهل. قوله: (الثالث أنه إما داخل العالم إلخ) الفرق بين الدليلين باعتبار كل من الشقين في أن الاتصال هو المماسة سواء كان من داخل أو خارج، ولو اشترط فيه كونها من خارج فالفرق أظهر والانفصال عدم الاتصال عما من شأنه ذلك، وأما الخروج فهو يتناول المماسة من خارج والانفصال. قوله: (والأولان فيهما المطلوب) أما الأول فلأن العالم في جهة فما هو فيه يكون في تلك الجهة، وأما الثاني فلأنه إذا كان خارجاً عنه يكون في إحدى الجهات الست منه فلا عبرة بقول...
The second: Every two existents either connect or separate. He, the Necessary Exalted, if He is connected to the world, then He is located; and if He is separate from it, likewise. The answer: denial of the dichotomy, and it is of the first kind, i.e., imaginary judgments, and you already know that its judgments are not accepted for non-sensibles, but they may resemble self-evident truths and so be thought to be from them. The third: He is either inside the world, or outside the world, or neither inside nor outside; the third is contrary to reason and to what rational intuition requires, and the first two contain what is sought. His saying: (they either connect or separate) it was said: if one added to it 'or neither this nor that' which is contrary to reason, as was done in the third, it would be better, but the matter is easy. His saying: (the third that He is either inside the world etc.) the difference between the two arguments in terms of each of the two sides is that connection is contact whether from inside or outside; if contact is restricted to being from outside, the difference is clearer; separation is the absence of connection with that which is supposed to have connection. As for 'being outside,' it includes contact from outside and separation. His saying: (the first two contain what is sought) As for the first, because the world is in a direction, so what is inside it is in that direction. As for the second, because if He is outside it, He is in one of the six directions from it, so no attention is paid to the saying...
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage covers the opponent's second and third arguments and the author's responses. The second argument posits a dichotomy: any two existents either connect or separate; if God connects to the world, He is located; if He is separate, He is also located, so He must be located. The answer denies the dichotomy, calling it imaginary (wahmi) and not acceptable for non-sensibles, even though it may be mistaken for a first principle. The third argument presents a trichotomy: inside, outside, or neither; the third is absurd, so God must be inside or outside, hence located. The response is not directly given as a denial; instead, the commentary (sharh) analyzes the difference between the two arguments, explaining that 'connection' means contact (mumassa) whether from inside or outside, and 'separation' is the absence of connection. The commentator notes that the second argument could be improved by adding the third option (neither) as in the third argument. The discussion ends with the explanation that the first two options (inside/outside) indeed imply directionality, but the reader is left to infer that the refutation is that these are again imaginary judgments or that the absolute dichotomy is not necessary.

Key Terms
اتصال
انفصال
وهم
أوليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dichotomy of connection and separation is a self-evident rational truth.
Correction: The author shows it is an imaginary judgment derived from sensory experience, not a universally valid rational principle.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the dichotomy of connection and separation rejected?
A) Because it is contradictory.
B) Because it is an imaginary judgment that does not apply to non-sensibles.
C) Because it assumes God is located.
D) Because it is based on sense perception.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's dichotomy of connection and separation is an imaginary judgment, not a rational necessity, so it cannot be applied to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's relationship to the world, do you ever catch yourself using mental categories like 'near' or 'far'? How does this lesson challenge that habit, and what will you do to guard against imposing such limits on your conception of Allah?
?
Imagine you are in a discussion with someone who insists that God must either be 'connected to' or 'separate from' creation because that is obvious. Having learned that such judgments are imaginary, what will you say to correct them? Will you have the courage to challenge what feels obvious?
?
The author warns that imaginary judgments 'may resemble self-evident truths and so be thought to be from them.' What 'obvious' ideas about God do you hold that might actually be rooted in sensory experience rather than revelation? Take one such idea and commit to re-examining it today.

The Opponent's Argument from Inside, Outside, or Neither
The opponent poses a trichotomy and the commentator analyzes its spatial implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's third argument that God must be either inside or outside the world, and the commentator's analysis showing that both options imply direction and location.

Core Idea
The trichotomy of inside/outside/neither forces location unless the third is allowed.

The opponent presents a trichotomy: God is either inside the world, outside it, or neither. The third is deemed absurd, so God must be inside or outside—both imply location. The commentator analyzes the meanings of connection, separation, and outside, showing that 'outside' can include contact from outside, blurring the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three options and the commentator's discussion of the differences between the second and third arguments.
The passage includes the opponent's trichotomy and the commentator's analysis.
2
Identify the trichotomy
The opponent says God is either inside, outside, or neither. The third is considered contrary to reason, so the first two must hold.
3
Understand the implication of inside/outside
If God is inside the world, He is in the direction of the world; if outside, He is in one of the six directions. Both imply location.
The commentator explains that 'outside' includes contact from outside, which is a form of connection.
4
Reflect on the refutation
The passage does not explicitly provide the author's refutation, but the commentator's analysis suggests that the opponent's trichotomy relies on spatial concepts that are not applicable to God.
The refutation might be similar to the second argument: these are imaginary judgments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The trichotomy of inside/outside/neither is exhaustive and rationally necessary for all entities.
Correction: The commentator shows that these spatial categories are not applicable to a non-spatial being like God.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the difference between the second and third arguments?
A) The second argument is weaker.
B) Connection and contact are the same.
C) 'Outside' includes contact from outside and separation.
D) Both arguments are based on sense perception.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's trichotomy forces spatial location on God, but the categories used are not applicable to the Divine.

Refuting the Dichotomy of Connection and Separation
Sanusi presents the second proof and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for God's spatiality and its refutation through the denial of exhaustive division based on the nature of imaginary judgments.

Core Idea
Not every logical dichotomy applies to the Divine.

This lesson examines a classic argument that postulates God must be spatial because any two existents are either connected or separated. The answer, however, reveals a crucial limitation: imaginary judgments, which underpin this dichotomy, do not hold for the non-sensible. Understanding this refutation is essential for maintaining divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a proof is presented, then a response.
Pay attention to the terms 'اتصال' (connection) and 'انفصال' (separation).
2
Identify the proof's logic
The proof argues: any two existents are either connected or separated. If God is connected to the world, He is spatial; if separated, likewise. Identify the implied conclusion.
The proof attempts to force a spatial location on God.
3
Understand the response: denial of exhaustive division
The response says 'منع الحصر' (denial of exhaustive division) and classifies the dichotomy as an imaginary judgment. Grasp why this division is not exhaustive.
Imaginary judgments only apply to sensibles, not to God.
4
Reflect on imaginary judgments
The passage notes that such judgments can be confused with primary axioms. Consider why this confusion arises and how to avoid it in theological reasoning.
This connects to broader principles of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني كل موجودين فأما أن يتصلا أو ينفصلا فهو ) أي الواجب تعالى إن كان متصلا بالعالم ،فمتحيز، وإن كان منفصلاً عنه ( فكذلك، والجواب منع الحصر وهو من الطراز الأول ( أي من الأحكام الوهمية، وقد عرفت أن أحكامه لا تقبل في غير المحسوسات لكنها قد تشبته بالأوليات فتحسب أنها منها .
The second: Every two existents are either connected or separated. So the Necessary, exalted, if He is connected to the world, then He is spatial; and if He is separated from it, then likewise. The answer: the division is not exhaustive, and it is of the first type (i.e., imaginary judgments; you have known that its judgments are not acceptable for non-sensibles, but they may be confused with primary axioms so they are thought to be from them.)
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This is a proof (the second) that God is spatial. It argues that any two existents are either connected or separated; if God is connected to the world, He is in contact and thus spatial; if separated, He is also in a location relative to the world. The answer refutes the dichotomy: the division is not exhaustive—it is an imaginary judgment. Such judgments only apply to sensible things, but here they are mistakenly taken as self-evident.

Key Terms
اتصال
انفصال
الحصر
الأحكام الوهمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is not connected to the world, He must be separated from it, implying a spatial relationship.
Correction: The dichotomy of connection and separation is not exhaustive; it is an imaginary judgment that only applies to sensible objects, not to God.

Checkpoint
What is the main response to the second proof?
A) God is both connected and separated.
B) The division into connection and separation is not exhaustive.
C) God is neither connected nor separated.
D) The proof is based on scripture.

Key Takeaway
The mind's imaginary dichotomies should not be applied to the non-sensible Divine.

Difference Between the Second and Third Proofs
Clarifies the distinction between connection/separation and inside/outside as two different proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct conceptual bases of the second and third proofs for divine transcendence, specifically the difference between connection/separation and inside/outside.

Core Idea
Connection is contact; being outside is about spatial location.

This passage from the commentary clarifies a subtle but important distinction between two proofs for God's transcendence. The second proof deals with whether God is connected or separated from the world, while the third proof considers whether He is inside, outside, or neither. Understanding this difference prevents conflating two separate arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the technical terms: اتصال, انفصال, مماسة, خروج.
2
Understand connection and separation
The passage defines connection (اتصال) as contact (مماسة) and separation (انفصال) as absence of contact. Note that connection is not restricted to outside.
This is the basis of the second proof.
3
Distinguish 'outside' (خروج) from connection
The passage explains that 'outside' includes both contact from outside and separation. It then shows why both 'inside' and 'outside' options imply directionality, which is problematic for divine transcendence.
The third proof uses a different conceptual framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الثالث أنه إما داخل العالم إلخ الفرق بين الدليلين باعتبار كل من الشقين في أن الاتصال هو المماسة سواء كان من داخل أو خارج، ولو اشترط فيه كونها من خارج فالفرق أظهر والانفصال عدم الاتصال عما من شأنه ذلك، وأما الخروج فهو يتناول المماسة من خارج والانفصال . قوله : ( والأولان فيهما المطلوب أما الأول فلان العالم في جهة فما هو فيه يكون في تلك الجهة، وأما الثاني فلانه إذا كان خارجاً عنه يكون في إحدى الجهات الست منه فلا عبرة بقول
His saying: 'The third: He is either inside the world...' The difference between the two proofs considering each of the two sides is that connection is contact (mumassa) whether from inside or outside; if it is conditioned to be from outside, the difference is clearer. Separation is absence of contact from what normally has that. As for 'khuruj' (being outside), it encompasses contact from outside and separation. His saying: 'And in both the first two is the desired (conclusion)': as for the first, because the world is in a direction, so what is in it is in that direction; as for the second, because if He is outside it, He would be in one of the six directions from it. So no heed to the saying.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage clarifies the difference between the second proof (connection/separation) and the third proof (inside/outside/neither). Connection (ittisal) means contact (mumassa), whether from inside or outside. Separation (infisal) is the absence of such contact. Being outside (khuruj) is broader: it includes both contact from outside and separation. The commentator also explains why both the first two options of the third proof imply directionality: if inside, the world is in a direction, so what is inside it is in that direction; if outside, it would be in one of the six cardinal directions.

Key Terms
اتصال
انفصال
مماسة
خروج
جهات ست

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might conflate the two proofs as the same argument.
Correction: The second proof is about contact (connection/separation), while the third is about spatial location (inside/outside). They are distinct conceptual frameworks.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the second and third proofs?
A) The second is about inside/outside, the third about connection.
B) The second is about connection/separation (contact), the third about inside/outside (encompassment).
C) They are the same proof restated.
D) The second uses scripture, the third uses reason.

Key Takeaway
The second proof concerns connection/separation (contact), while the third concerns inside/outside (spatial location), and both challenge God's transcendence by implying direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Third Proof: Inside, Outside, or Neither
Presents the third proof and its refutation via the imagination/reason distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for God's spatiality and how Islamic theology refutes it by distinguishing the rationally impossible from the merely unimaginable.

Core Idea
The third option is a 'surd' for imagination, not for reason.

This lesson presents a famous trilemma: God is either inside the world, outside, or neither. The proof argues that the third is irrational, forcing spatiality. But the answer reveals a key distinction: what is unimaginable for creatures need not be impossible for the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the dense Arabic and its literal translation. Notice the logical structure: trilemma, rejection of third, conclusion, answer.
2
Identify the trilemma
Break apart the three options: inside, outside, neither. Understand why the proof says the third is 'against reason'.
3
Examine the answer
The answer denies the impossibility of the third. It says it is only unimaginable, not irrational. Reflect on the difference between rational impossibility and imaginative failure.
This is a key kalam move.
4
Apply the distinction
Consider how this distinction protects divine transcendence. God is not constrained by spatial categories, yet this is not a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالث) أنه إما داخل العالم أو خارج العالم أولا داخله ولا خارجه والثالث خروج عن المعقول ( وعما تقتضيه بداهة العقل والأولان فيهما المطلوب) وهو أنه متحيز وفي جهة ) والجواب أنه لا داخل ولا خارج وهذا خروج عن الموهوم دون المعقول .
The third: He is either inside the world or outside the world or neither inside nor outside. The third is a departure from reason and from what the natural intellect requires. In both the first two is the desired (conclusion), namely that He is spatial and in a direction. The answer: He is neither inside nor outside, and this is a departure from the imaginary, not from reason.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This is the third proof: a trilemma—God is either inside the world, outside it, or neither. The third is deemed impossible by reason, so one of the first two must hold, implying spatiality. The answer rejects the impossibility: 'neither inside nor outside' is not rationally impossible; it only violates the imagination. The conclusion is not forced.

Key Terms
داخل
خارج
المعقول
الموهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'neither inside nor outside' is a logical contradiction or impossible for God.
Correction: The passage clarifies it is only unimaginable for contingent objects, not rationally impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does the third option (neither inside nor outside) not force spatiality?
A) Because it is logically impossible.
B) Because it is only unimaginable, not irrational.
C) Because God is both inside and outside.
D) Because the trilemma is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The third proof is refuted by showing that 'neither inside nor outside' is rationally possible, only unimaginable, preserving God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel distant from Allah in prayer, do you unconsciously imagine Him as being 'far away' in a spatial sense? How can the distinction between imagination and reason transform your awareness of His closeness?
?
You have never seen Allah, yet your imagination may try to place Him somewhere. What practical step can you take today to train your heart to accept His transcendence beyond all spatial categories?
?
The objector demanded that God fit into the category of 'inside' or 'outside'. In your own life, where do you insist that Allah must fit into your limited understanding? Will you let Him be beyond your imagination, or will you repent from trying to box Him in?
