Refuting Apparent Meanings of Scripture
Refutes arguments from Quranic and hadith expressions that imply spatiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars refute the claim that Quranic verses and hadith imply God has a spatial location, and why raising hands to heaven does not mean God is in heaven.

Core Idea
Apparent meanings of scripture do not imply spatiality; they are metaphorical or indicate direction.

This lesson examines the fifth proof, which uses scriptural expressions that seem to describe God in spatial terms. The author explains each term (istawa, maji'a, etc.) and shows that they are not to be taken literally. He also addresses the common practice of raising hands to heaven in prayer, clarifying that heaven is a qibla, not a place of residence for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the author's style of listing terms and then providing the answer.
The passage includes a marginal note about 'صاحب الكواشف' – consider it an aside.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms istiwa, maji', nuzul, 'indiyya, su'ud, 'uruj, and dunuww. How does the author show their literal meanings suggest spatiality?
3
Understand the answer for each term
The author's response is that these terms are interpreted figuratively. What principle allows this? How does he handle 'man fi al-sama''?
The underlying principle is that God transcends spatial attributes; thus, these expressions are metaphorical.
4
Analyze the hand-raising argument
Why does raising hands to heaven not imply God is there? What analogy does the author use with the Ka'ba?
Heaven is the qibla of supplication, just as the Ka'ba is the qibla of prayer.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this argument preserves divine transcendence while acknowledging the practices of the faithful. How does this fit into the broader discussion of God's attributes?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس الاستدلال بالظواهر الموهمة بالتجسم من الآيات والأحاديث نحو قوله تعالى : الرحمن على العرش استوى ﴾ [طه:٥] ، وجاء ربك والملك صفاً صفاً ﴾ [الفجر: ٢٢]، صاحب الكواشف، ليت شعري ما المحذور في اختيار أنه خارج ونمنع ما ذكر فيه وكأنه توهم أن الخروج مجرد سلب الدخول، ولا شك أن المتبادر أخص من ذلك السلب، وهو ما يكون في حيز مباين عن العالم وهذا المعنى الأخص هو مراد المستدل فلذلك لا نختار . قوله : (من الآيات والأحاديث نحو قوله إلخ) الاستواء يشعر بالتحيز، يقال: استوى فلان على دابته أي استقر والمجيء الإتيان والنزول الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان والعندية مشعرة بالتحيز والجهة والصعود والعروج الحركة إلى جهة العلو فكونهما إليه تعالى يشعر بكونه في تلك الجهة أيضاً تعالى عن ذلك ومن في السماء يكون متحيزاً فيه والدنو مشعر بالتحيز لكن الاستدلال به مبني على رجوع ضميرنا إلى الله تعالى والرجوع إلى جبريل عليه السلام مذكور أيضاً في كتب التفاسير، وقد يستدل على التحيز لشيوع رفع الأيدي إلى السماء عند الدعاء فإنه طريقة متوارثة السلف والجواب أن ذلك الرفع ليس لأن المدعو في السماء تعالى عن ذلك بل لأن السماء قبلة الدعاء، كما أن الكعبة شرفها الله تعالى قبلة الصلوات فكما أن الله عز وجل يخصص بعض الأمكنة ببعض العبادات، كذلك له تعالى تخصيص بعض الجهات بالتقرب إليه بالدعاء .
The fifth: arguing by apparent meanings suggesting corporeality from verses and hadith, such as His saying: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' [20:5], and 'And your Lord comes, and the angels rank upon rank' [89:22]. The author of al-Kawashif: I wonder what is the objection to choosing that He is outside; we deny what is mentioned in it, as if he imagined that 'khuruj' (being outside) is merely the negation of inside; but undoubtedly the immediate understanding is more specific than that negation, namely what is in a space separate from the world. This more specific meaning is what the arguer intends, so we do not choose it. His saying: (from verses and hadith such as...) Al-Istiwa' (establishment) suggests spatiality; it is said: so-and-so established himself on his mount, i.e., settled. Al-Maji' (coming) is arrival; al-nuzul (descending) is moving from place to place; al-'indiyya (withness) suggests spatiality and direction; al-su'ud and al-'uruj (ascending and rising) are movement to the direction of height; so their being attributed to Him suggests He is in that direction—exalted is He above that. And 'man fi al-sama'' (who is in the heaven) is spatial therein. Al-dunuww (nearness) suggests spatiality, but arguing by it is based on the pronoun referring to God; the reference to Gabriel, peace be upon him, is also mentioned in commentary books. One may also argue for spatiality from the common practice of raising hands to heaven in supplication, for it is a method inherited from the predecessors. The answer is that this raising is not because the supplicated is in heaven—exalted is He—but because heaven is the qibla (direction) of supplication, just as the Ka'ba, may God honor it, is the qibla of prayers. So just as God, mighty and majestic, designates some places for some acts of worship, likewise He designates some directions for drawing near to Him in supplication.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This is the fifth proof, which uses scriptural expressions that seem to imply God is spatial (tajsim). It cites verses like 'Istawa 'ala al-'arsh' and 'Jaa'a rabbuka' (your Lord comes). The author also mentions a note about someone (صاحب الكواشف) who suggests 'outside' is not problematic. Then the main answer: these expressions are understood figuratively. For each term (istiwa', maji', nuzul, etc.), they are explained in a way that suggests spatiality if taken literally, but they are metaphorical. The special case of raising hands to heaven in prayer: this does not mean God is in heaven; rather, heaven is the qibla of supplication, just as the Ka'ba is the qibla of prayer. Thus, no spatial location is implied.

Key Terms
الظواهر الموهمة
التجسم
الاستواء
المجيء
قبلة الدعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Raising hands to heaven in supplication indicates that God is located in heaven.
Correction: The author explains that heaven is the qibla (direction) of supplication, not a place where God resides, just as the Ka'ba is the direction for prayer without God being inside it.

Checkpoint
How does the answer explain raising hands to heaven in prayer?
A) It is a sign that God is in heaven.
B) Heaven is the qibla of supplication, not God's location.
C) It is a prohibited innovation.
D) It is a symbol of God's omnipresence.

Key Takeaway
Apparent meanings of scripture do not compel belief in God's spatiality; they are understood figuratively, and actions like raising hands to heaven are matters of direction, not location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What other aspects of your faith might be based on outward appearances rather than the truth of God's transcendence?

Apparent Meanings Suggesting Corporealism
Listing verses and hadiths that seem to imply corporealism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural proofs that seem to imply God occupies space or has a body, and why they pose a challenge to the doctrine of divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Scripture contains expressions that seem to suggest God has a body; these must be reconciled with rational certainties.

The opponents of the Ash'ari doctrine of divine transcendence cite numerous verses and hadiths that appear to describe God in spatial terms—sitting on a throne, coming, descending, being in heaven. In this lesson, we examine these texts and understand why they are considered speculative (ẓannī) evidence that cannot overturn the certain rational proofs of God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of verses and hadiths
Read through the verses and hadiths cited. Notice they all describe God in terms of location, movement, or direction.
These are the main texts used by those who claim God has a body.
2
Identify the pattern
Each text implies either a place (e.g., 'over the Throne', 'in the heaven'), a movement (coming, descending, ascending), or a spatial relationship (nearness, withness).
3
Understand the problem
If taken literally, these texts contradict the rational certainties that God is not in a place or direction. This creates a conflict between scripture and reason.
This conflict must be resolved without denying either source.
4
See the objection
The question to the slave girl 'Where is Allah?' and the prophet's affirmation seem to imply direction. This is a key point for the opponents.
The objection is that these texts must be taken literally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس الاستدلال بالظواهر الموهمة بالتجسم من الآيات والأحاديث نحو قوله تعالى : { الرحمن على العرش استوى } [طه:٥] ، { وجاء ربك والملك صفاً صفاً } [الفجر: ٢٢]، { فإن استكبروا فالذين عند ربك } [فصلت : ۳۸ ] ، { و إليه يصعد الكلم الطيب } [فاطر: ۱۰] ، { تعرج الملائكة والروح إليه } [المعارج : ٤]، { وهل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم في ظلل من الغمام } [البقرة : ٢١٠] ، { أمنتم من في السماء أن يخسف بكم الأرض } [الملك:16؟]، { ثم دنى فتدلى فكان قاب قوسين أو أدنى } [النجم: 8-9]، وحديث النزول وهو أنه تعالى ينزل إلى السماء الدنيا في كل ليلة وفي رواية في كل ليلة جمعة فيقول هل من تائب فأتوب عليه هل من مستغفر فأغفر له ) وقوله عليه السلام للجارية الخرساء أين الله فأشارت إلى السماء (فقرر ولم ينكر وقال إنها مؤمنة ) فالسؤال والتقرير المذكوران يشعران بالجهة والمكان والجواب أنها ظواهر ظنية لا تعارض اليقينيات الدالة على نفي المكان والجهة كيف ومهما تعارض دليلان وجب العمل بهما ما أمكن فتؤول الظواهر إما اجمالاً ويفوض تفصيله إلى الله كما هو رأى من يقف على الله وعليه أكثر السلف كما روي عن أحمد الاستواء معلوم والكيفية مجهولة والبحث عنها بدعة، وإما تفصيلاً كما هو رأى طائفة فنقول الاستواء الاستيلاء نحو قوله ( قد استوى عمرو على العراق ( من غير سيف ودم مهراق ( والعندية بمعنى والإكرام ) كما يقال فلان قريب من الملك وجاء ربك أى أمره. وإليه يصعد الكلم الطيب أي يرتضيه فإن الكلم عرض يمتنع عليه الانتقال ومن في السماء أي حكمه أو سلطانه أو ملك من ملائكته موكل بالعذاب للمستحقين وعليه فقس سائر الآيات والأحاديث فالعروج إليه هو العروج إلى موضع يتقرب إليه بالطاعات فيه وإتيانه في ظل إتيان عذابه والدنو هو قرب الرسول إليه بالطاعة والتقدير بقاب قوسین تصوير للمعقول بالمحسوس والنزول محمول على اللطف والرحمة وترك ما يستدعيه عظم الشأن وعلو الرتبة على سبيل التمثيل، وخص بالليل لأنه مظنة الخلوات وأنواع الخضوع والعبادات . والسؤال بأين استكشاف عما ظن أنها معتقدة له من الأينية في الإلهية، فلما أشارت إلى السماء علم أنها ليست وثنية وحمل إشارتها على أنها أرادت الاصطفاء.
The fifth (argument) is the inference from the apparent meanings (al-ẓawāhir) that suggest corporealism (al-tajassum) from the verses and hadiths, such as His statement: 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' [20:5], 'And your Lord comes and the angels in rows' [89:22], 'But if they are arrogant, then those who are with your Lord' [41:38], 'And to Him ascends good speech' [35:10], 'The angels and the Spirit ascend to Him' [70:4], 'Do they await but that Allah should come to them in canopies of clouds?' [2:210], 'Do you feel secure that He who is in the heaven will not cause the earth to swallow you?' [67:16], 'Then He approached and came closer and was at a distance of two bow-lengths or nearer' [53:8-9], and the hadith of descent: that He descends to the lowest heaven every night, and in a narration every Friday night, saying: 'Is there any repenter that I may forgive him? Is there any seeker of forgiveness that I may forgive him?' And his statement, peace be upon him, to the mute slave girl: 'Where is Allah?' and she pointed to the sky, so he affirmed it and did not deny and said: 'She is a believer.' So the question and the affirmation mentioned suggest direction and place. The answer: These are speculative apparent meanings (ẓawāhir ẓanniyya) that do not contradict the certainties (al-yaqīniyyāt) that negate place and direction. How? And whenever two proofs conflict, it is obligatory to act on both as much as possible. So the apparent meanings are interpreted either summarily (ijmālan), delegating the details to Allah, which is the view of those who stop at Allah, and this is the way of most of the Salaf, as narrated from Ahmad: 'The establishment (al-istiwā') is known, the modality (al-kayfiyya) is unknown, and asking about it is an innovation.' Or in detail (tafṣīlan), as is the view of a group. So we say: al-istiwā' means conquest and domination (al-istīlā'), like the saying 'Amr has established himself over Iraq' without sword or bloodshed. And 'and with Him' (al-'indiyya) means honor and veneration, as one says 'So-and-so is close to the king.' And 'your Lord comes' means His command. And 'to Him ascends good speech' means He approves it, for speech is an accident that cannot undergo transition. And 'He who is in the heaven' means His command, His dominion, or an angel from His angels entrusted with punishment for those who deserve it. And upon this, analogize all other verses and hadiths. So 'ascension to Him' is ascension to a place where one draws near to Him through acts of obedience in it. And 'His coming in canopies' is the coming of His punishment. And 'the nearness' is the closeness of the Messenger to Him through obedience, and the estimation of two bow-lengths is a depiction of the intelligible by the sensible. And 'descent' is interpreted as kindness and mercy and the abandonment of what the greatness of status and loftiness of rank would require, by way of representation, and it is specified at night because it is a time of seclusion and types of humility and worship. And the question 'Where?' was an investigation into what he thought she believed of 'where-ness' in divinity. When she pointed to the sky, he knew she was not an idolater, and he interpreted her pointing as meaning she intended the attribute of being chosen (al-iṣṭifā').
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument for God's corporeality based on scriptural apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) that seem to imply direction or place, followed by the general answer and detailed interpretation. The argument lists numerous verses and hadiths that are tashbīhī (anthropomorphic) in appearance. The answer begins by stating that these are speculative (ẓanniyya) and do not contradict the certainties (yaqīniyyāt) that negate place and direction. When two proofs conflict, one must reconcile them, so the apparent meanings are interpreted. Two methods are given: the Salaf method of tafwīḍ (consigning the details to Allah) and the method of detailed ta'wīl (interpretation). Then each ambiguous expression is given a figurative meaning: istiwā' as istīlā' (domination), 'coming' as command, 'ascending' as approval, 'in the heaven' as command or angel, etc. The hadith of descent is interpreted as descending mercy, and the slave girl hadith as her referring to divine selection, not location.

Which verse is cited that mentions 'two bow-lengths or nearer'?
A) 20:5

Key Takeaway
The scriptural texts that seem to imply corporealism are numerous and constitute a challenge to transcendence, requiring careful interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne,' does your heart feel a need to imagine a physical throne or location for Allah? How do you train yourself to submit to His transcendence without visualization?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) did not correct the slave girl's pointing to the sky, but neither did he affirm that Allah is literally there. Does this ambiguity unsettle you? Are you willing to remain in the tension between the text and the intellect, trusting that both are true?
?
If a fellow Muslim argued that these verses prove Allah has a body because they seem clear, would you have the confidence to explain why they are only 'apparent'? What is the state of your own heart—do you truly believe Allah is beyond all physical categories, or do you unconsciously cling to a more tangible image of Him?

Interpreting Apparent Meanings: Summarily or in Detail
Answering the fifth argument with two methods of interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two methods of interpreting anthropomorphic expressions in scripture: the Salaf method of tafwīḍ (consigning detail to Allah) and the method of detailed ta'wīl (figurative interpretation).

Core Idea
Scriptural apparent meanings are reconciled with reason either by affirming the meaning while delegating the modality, or by providing a figurative interpretation.

In this lesson, we explore the answer to the problem raised by anthropomorphic verses and hadiths. The author explains that such texts are speculative and cannot override certain rational proofs. He then presents two valid approaches: the way of the Salaf, who affirm the meaning without asking how, and the way of detailed interpretation, which assigns a figurative meaning to each expression.

Guided Path
1
Read the general principle
The author states that these texts are speculative (ẓanniyya) and cannot contradict the certainties (yaqīniyyāt) that negate place and direction.
This is the foundational rule for handling apparent meanings.
2
Understand the two methods
Two methods are given: (1) tafwīḍ: consign the detailed meaning to Allah, as done by most Salaf; (2) ta'wīl: provide a figurative interpretation, as done by some later scholars.
Imam Ahmad's statement is cited for the first method.
3
Examine examples of tafwīḍ
The author quotes the statement: 'The establishment is known, the modality is unknown, and asking about it is an innovation.'
This illustrates the Salaf approach.
4
Examine examples of ta'wīl
For each expression, a figurative interpretation is given: istiwā' as istīlā', 'coming' as command, 'ascension' as approval, etc.
These are detailed, context-sensitive interpretations.
5
Understand the slave girl hadith explanation
The question 'Where?' was to test her belief, and her pointing to sky was interpreted as referring to divine selection, not location.
This resolves the apparent implication of place.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Interpreting figurative meanings is a form of denial or rejection of scripture.
Correction: Ta'wīl is a valid method of reconciling reason and revelation, used by many scholars, and does not deny the truth of scripture.

Checkpoint
What is the first method of interpretation mentioned?
A) Literal acceptance
B) Tafwīḍ (summarily delegating detail to Allah)
C) Rejection of the text
D) Allegorical interpretation only

Key Takeaway
The apparent meanings of scripture are not literal; they are interpreted in a way that upholds divine transcendence, either by tafwīḍ or ta'wīl.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse describing Allah's 'hand' or 'coming', do you feel a tension between what you read and what you know intellectually about Allah's transcendence? Which of the two methods in this lesson—tafwīḍ or ta'wīl—do you find yourself actually using, and are you content with that? If not, what would it take for you to submit to the method of the Salaf and trust that Allah knows the modality better than you do?
?
This lesson shows that the Salaf (like Imam Ahmad) affirmed the meaning but stopped at the modality. Have you ever felt the urge to 'understand' the 'how' of Allah's attributes? How does that urge conflict with the principle of tanzīh? Write down one attribute you often think about and commit today to saying 'Allahu a'lam' instead of trying to imagine it.
?
The slave girl pointed to the sky when asked about Allah’s location, and the Prophet accepted that as a sign of her belief in divine selection, not location. In your own life, when you hear or read something about Allah that confronts your assumptions, do you immediately defend your position or do you pause to consider whether your interpretation might be influenced by your own cultural or mental imagery? What one assumption about Allah are you willing to hand over to the method taught here?

The Problem of Apparent Direction in Scripture
The author lists problematic texts and states the principle of reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scriptural texts that seem to attribute direction or place to Allah are considered presumptive and must be interpreted in harmony with certain rational proofs.

Core Idea
Presumptive meanings cannot override certainties.

This passage begins by listing verses and hadiths that appear to place Allah in a direction or location. The author then presents the foundational principle: such texts are ẓawāhir ẓanniyya (presumptive apparent meanings) and cannot contradict the qaṭʿiyyāt (certainties) that negate place and direction from Allah. This sets the stage for how these texts must be reconciled.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of problematic texts
Notice the verses and hadiths cited: they include words like 'with your Lord', 'ascends to Him', 'comes in clouds', the hadith of descent, and the slave girl pointing to the sky. Observe how they suggest direction or place.
These are the texts that the opponent might use.
2
Identify the central claim
The author states that these texts ‘indicate direction and place’. Understand that this is the problem to be resolved.
3
Grasp the answer and principle
The answer is that these are presumptive (ẓannī) and do not contradict certain (qaṭʿī) proofs negating direction. The principle: when two proofs conflict, act on both as much as possible.
This principle will be applied in later lessons.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الأول : أنه تعالى ليس في جهة ولا في مكان
فإن استكبروا فالذين عند ربك ) [فصلت : ۳۸ ]، و إليه يصعد الكلم الطيب ﴾ [فاطر: ۱۰]، تعرج الملائكة والروح إليه ﴾ [المعارج : ٤]، وهل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم في ظلل من الغمام أمنتم من في السماء أن يخسف بكم الأرض ثم دنى فتدلى فكان قاب قوسين أو أدنى ﴾ [البقرة : ٢١٠]، وحديث النزول وهو أنه تعالى ينزل إلى السماء الدنيا في كل ليلة وفي رواية في كل ليلة جمعة فيقول هل من تائب فأتوب عليه هل من مستغفر فأغفر له ) وقوله عليه السلام للجارية الخرساء أين الله فأشارت إلى السماء (فقرر ولم ينكر وقال إنها مؤمنة ) فالسؤال والتقرير المذكوران يشعران بالجهة ) والمكان ) والجواب أنها ظواهر ظنية لا تعارض اليقينيات ( الدالة على نفي المكان والجهة كيف ) ومهما تعارض دليلان وجب العمل بهما ما أمكن فتؤول الظواهر إما اجمالاً ويفوض تفصيله إلى الله كما هو رأى من يقف على الله وعليه أكثر السلف كما روي عن أحمد الاستواء معلوم والكيفية مجهولة والبحث عنها بدعة، وإما تفصيلاً كما هو رأى طائفة فنقول الاستواء الاستيلاء نحو) قوله ( قد استوى عمرو على العراق ( من غير سيف ودم مهراق ( والثندية بمعنى والإكرام ) كما يقال فلان قريب من الملك وجاء ربك أى أمره. وإليه يصدع الكلم الطيب أي يرتضيه فإن الكلم عرض يمتنع عليه الانتقال ومن في السماء أي حكمه أو سلطانه أو ملك من ملائكته ( موكل بالعذاب ( للمستحقين ( وعليه فقس ) سائر والأحاديث فالعروج إليه هو العروج إلى موضع يتقرب إليه بالطاعات فيه وإتيانه في ظل إتيان عذابه والدنو هو قرب الرسول إليه بالطاعة والتقدير بقاب قوسین تصویر للمعقول بالمحسوس والنزول محمول على اللطف والرحمة وترك ما يستدعيه عظم الشأن وعلو الرتبة على سبيل التمثيل، وخص بالليل لأنه مظنة الخلوات وأنواع الخضوع والعبادات . والسؤال بأين استكشاف عما ظن أنها معتقدة له من الأينية في الإلهية، فلما أشارت إلى السماء علم أنها ليست وثنية وحمل إشارتها على أنها أرادت كونه تعالى خالق السماء فحكم بإيمانها إلى غير ذلك من التأويلات التي ذكرها العلماء لهذه الآيات والأحاديث ونظائرها، فارجع إلى الكتب المبسوطة تظفر بها .
قوله : (إتيان عذابه ) وإنما خصصه بالظلل من الغمام لأن أكثر العقوبات يتقدمها ظلل كما نقل في القصص.
قوله : ( والدنو هو قرب الرسول عليه السلام إلخ) يعني أن دنو الله تعالى من رسوله عليه السلام كناية عما ذكره فلا يظن أنه مبني على أن الضمير في دنا راجع إلى الرسول عليه السلام والأظهر أن يقال دنوه تعالى منه عليه السلام رفع مكانته
قوله : (وخص بالليل إلخ ) وخص بالسماء الدنيا مبالغة بالتلطف .
قوله : ( فلما أشارت إلى السماء علم أنها ليست وثنية وحمل إشارتها إلخ) إدعاء هذا العلم والحمل إن كان بإسنادهما إلى الوحي المقارن لإشارتها إلى السماء فلا كلام فيه وإن كان بإسنادهما إلى نفس الإشارة المذكورة كما هو الظاهر من العبارة، ففيه أنها إنما تدل على ذلك لو كان السؤال بأين الإله وأما إذا كان باين الله تعالى كما الاستدلال فالدلالة المذكورة في المذكورة محل بحث لأن الله إسم خاص لذاته تعالى لم يطلق على غيره لا في الجاهلية ولا الإسلام كما صرحوا به فمن أين يعلم من تلك الإشارة أنها ليست وثنية وإنها تريد أنه تعالى خالق السماء وليست على معتقد كفار العرب من كونه تعالى في السماء على ما ذكره القاضي وغيره في تفسير قوله تعالى : أأمنتم من في السماء ﴾ [ تبارك : ۱۷] ، من جواز كون الآية على زعم العرب فإنهم يزعمون أن الله تعالى في السماء تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً على أنه يلزم مما ذكر عدم مطابقة جواب الخرساء للسؤال بأين الله والقصد إلى خلاف ما يترقب السامع من الجارية الأمية بعيد جداً، ولا رباب المكاشفة فيه وفي أمثاله توجيه ذكر في مفتاح الغيب وغيره
والله أعلم .
The second observation - the first aim: that He, exalted, is not in a direction nor in a place. Then: "Those who are with your Lord" [41:38], "To Him ascends the good word" [35:10], "The angels and the spirit ascend to Him" [70:4], "Do they await but that Allah should come to them in canopies of clouds?" [2:210], "Are you secure from Him who is in the heaven?" [67:16], then "He drew near and came down" [53:8-9], and the hadith of descent: that He descends to the lowest heaven every night, and in a narration every Friday night, saying 'Is there any repentant so I forgive him? Is there any seeker of forgiveness so I forgive him?' And his statement, peace be upon him, to the mute slave girl: 'Where is Allah?' so she pointed to the sky, and he affirmed and did not deny and said she is a believer. So the question and affirmation mentioned indicate direction and place. And the answer is that they are presumptive apparent meanings that do not contradict certainties indicating the negation of place and direction. For whenever two proofs conflict, it is necessary to act upon both as much as possible, so the apparent meanings are interpreted either summarily, delegating the details to Allah, as is the view of those who stop at the apparent meaning, and this is the way of most of the Salaf, as related from Ahmad: 'The istiwā' is known, the modality is unknown, and inquiring about it is an innovation'; or in detail, as is the view of a group, so we say: istiwā' means domination, as in his statement: 'Amr came to dominate over Iraq without sword or shed blood'; and closeness is by honor and generosity, as it is said: 'So-and-so is close to the king'; and 'Your Lord came' means His command; and 'to Him ascends the good word' means He approves it, for the word is an accident that cannot move; and 'Who is in the heaven' means His judgment or His authority or an angel from His angels entrusted with punishment for those deserving; and analogize the rest of the verses and hadiths accordingly. So ascending to Him is ascending to a place where one draws near to Him through acts of obedience; and His coming in canopies is the coming of His punishment; and the drawing near is the Prophet's closeness to Him through obedience, and the estimation of two bow-lengths is a likening of the intellectual to the sensible; and the descent is interpreted as kindness and mercy, and leaving aside what His majesty and high rank demand, by way of representation; and it is specified at night because it is a time for seclusions and types of humility and worship. And the question with 'where' was to investigate what she thought was an attribute of direction in divinity, and when she pointed to the sky, he knew she was not polytheistic, and he interpreted her pointing as meaning that she intended that He is creator of the sky, so he judged her belief, and other such interpretations that scholars have mentioned for these verses and hadiths and their like, so refer to the detailed books to find them. His statement: ('Coming of His punishment') and He specified it with canopies of clouds because most punishments are preceded by canopies as narrated in stories. His statement: ('And the drawing near is the closeness of the Prophet, peace be upon him, etc.') means that the drawing near of Allah from His Prophet is a metaphor for what he mentioned, so one should not think it is based on the pronoun in 'dana' referring to the Prophet; rather, it is said: His drawing near to him is raising his station. His statement: ('And it is specified at night, etc.') and He specified the lowest heaven for exaggeration in kindness. His statement: ('When she pointed to the sky, he knew she was not polytheistic and interpreted her pointing, etc.') Claiming this knowledge and interpretation, if they are attributed to the revelation coinciding with her pointing to the sky, then there is no issue; but if they are attributed to the same mentioned pointing, as is apparent from the expression, then it involves that it only indicates that if the question was 'Where is the deity?' But if it was 'Where is Allah?' as in the inference, then the mentioned indication is debatable, because Allah is a specific name for His essence, not applied to other than Him, neither in pre-Islamic times nor in Islam, as they stated. So how is it known from that pointing that she was not polytheistic and that she intended that He is creator of the sky, and not upon the belief of the Arab disbelievers that He is in the sky, as al-Qāḍī and others mentioned in the interpretation of His statement: 'Are you secure from Him who is in the heaven?' [67:16], regarding the possibility that the verse is according to the belief of the Arabs, for they claimed that Allah is in the sky—exalted is He greatly above that. Moreover, what results from the mentioned is that the slave girl's answer does not match the question 'Where is Allah?', and intending contrary to what the listener expects from an illiterate slave girl is very far-fetched. There is no key to unveiling in this and its like; a direction has been mentioned in Miftāḥ al-Ghayb and elsewhere. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and interpretation of scriptural evidence that appears to attribute direction and place to Allah. The author first lists the problematic verses and hadiths: verses about being with the Lord, ascending, coming in clouds, nearness, and hadiths of descent and the slave girl. These suggest direction and place. The response is that these are ḍawāhir ẓanniyya (presumptive apparent meanings) and cannot override the qaṭʿiyyāt (certainties) that negate place and direction. When two proofs conflict, both must be reconciled as much as possible, so these texts must be interpreted. Two methods are given: (1) tafwīḍ (consignment): accept the wording but delegate the true meaning to Allah, as the Salaf did; (2) taʾwīl (detailed interpretation): provide specific interpretations, e.g., istiwāʾ means domination, closeness means honor, coming means command, ascent means approval, etc. The author then addresses the slave girl hadith: the question 'Where is Allah?' was to test her belief; her pointing to the sky indicated she was not polytheistic, and the Prophet interpreted it as her acknowledgment that Allah is creator of the sky. The commentary then discusses the plausibility of this interpretation, questioning whether such inference from a gesture is valid given that the name 'Allah' is unique. The author notes that these issues have been addressed in larger works. Finally, the author mentions various interpretative details from the commentators.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the apparent meaning of these verses is definitive and must be taken literally, overriding rational arguments.
Correction: The author clarifies that these are presumptive (ẓannī) and must be interpreted in light of certain (qaṭʿī) proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how should the apparent meanings of verses that seem to indicate direction for Allah be characterized?
A) They are certain and undeniable.
B) They are presumptive and do not contradict certain proofs.
C) They are metaphorical and meaningless.
D) They are abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Scriptural texts that seem to attribute direction or place to Allah are presumptive and must be interpreted to harmonize with certain proofs negating such attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'He is with you wherever you are' (Quran 57:4), do you find yourself imagining a comforting closeness that feels almost physical? The author says 'with' does not imply spatial presence. How will you retrain your heart to feel that closeness without visualizing a location? What specific verse will you meditate on tonight to deepen that non-physical intimacy?
?
The hadith of descent says Allah comes down to the lowest heaven every night. You have likely called upon Him in the last third of the night. Did you ever picture a movement? The author says it is a presumptive meaning. What will you do in your next night prayer to affirm that Allah hears without descending? Start your supplication with a phrase that reinforces His transcendence—what will that phrase be?
?
The slave girl pointed to the sky, and the Prophet ﷺ affirmed her faith. You too may have looked up when feeling desperate. The author says that pointing to the sky is an apparent meaning, not a proof of location. When you next raise your hands in du'a, what thought will you replace the unconscious 'He is up there' with? Write down one sentence you will repeat mentally during du'a to reinforce that Allah is beyond space.

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
8 min
Two Methods: Tafwīḍ and Taʾwīl
The author presents tafwīḍ and taʾwīl with examples and glosses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main methods scholars use to interpret anthropomorphic verses: tafwīḍ (consignment) and taʾwīl (detailed interpretation), with examples from the text.

Core Idea
Scholars either consign the meaning to Allah or provide a detailed interpretation.

After establishing the principle of reconciliation, the author presents two approaches. The first is tafwīḍ: accepting the wording but leaving the true meaning to Allah, as was the way of most Salaf. The second is taʾwīl: providing specific interpretations that fit the context and avoid anthropomorphism. The author then gives examples of taʾwīl for key verses.

Guided Path
1
Understand tafwīḍ (consignment)
Read the description of tafwīḍ: the text is accepted as true, but its detailed meaning is delegated to Allah. Note the quote from Imam Ahmad about istiwāʾ.
This method avoids speculation.
2
Learn taʾwīl (detailed interpretation)
The author then gives detailed interpretations: istiwāʾ as domination, closeness as honor, 'Your Lord came' as His command, etc. See how each interpretation avoids attributing a body or place.
These are based on linguistic and contextual evidence.
3
Apply the method to other texts
The author says 'analogize the rest' – meaning this method can be extended to similar verses and hadiths. The glosses provide further clarifications.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الأول : أنه تعالى ليس في جهة ولا في مكان
فإن استكبروا فالذين عند ربك ) [فصلت : ۳۸ ]، و إليه يصعد الكلم الطيب ﴾ [فاطر: ۱۰]، تعرج الملائكة والروح إليه ﴾ [المعارج : ٤]، وهل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم في ظلل من الغمام أمنتم من في السماء أن يخسف بكم الأرض ثم دنى فتدلى فكان قاب قوسين أو أدنى ﴾ [البقرة : ٢١٠]، وحديث النزول وهو أنه تعالى ينزل إلى السماء الدنيا في كل ليلة وفي رواية في كل ليلة جمعة فيقول هل من تائب فأتوب عليه هل من مستغفر فأغفر له ) وقوله عليه السلام للجارية الخرساء أين الله فأشارت إلى السماء (فقرر ولم ينكر وقال إنها مؤمنة ) فالسؤال والتقرير المذكوران يشعران بالجهة ) والمكان ) والجواب أنها ظواهر ظنية لا تعارض اليقينيات ( الدالة على نفي المكان والجهة كيف ) ومهما تعارض دليلان وجب العمل بهما ما أمكن فتؤول الظواهر إما اجمالاً ويفوض تفصيله إلى الله كما هو رأى من يقف على الله وعليه أكثر السلف كما روي عن أحمد الاستواء معلوم والكيفية مجهولة والبحث عنها بدعة، وإما تفصيلاً كما هو رأى طائفة فنقول الاستواء الاستيلاء نحو) قوله ( قد استوى عمرو على العراق ( من غير سيف ودم مهراق ( والثندية بمعنى والإكرام ) كما يقال فلان قريب من الملك وجاء ربك أى أمره. وإليه يصدع الكلم الطيب أي يرتضيه فإن الكلم عرض يمتنع عليه الانتقال ومن في السماء أي حكمه أو سلطانه أو ملك من ملائكته ( موكل بالعذاب ( للمستحقين ( وعليه فقس ) سائر والأحاديث فالعروج إليه هو العروج إلى موضع يتقرب إليه بالطاعات فيه وإتيانه في ظل إتيان عذابه والدنو هو قرب الرسول إليه بالطاعة والتقدير بقاب قوسین تصویر للمعقول بالمحسوس والنزول محمول على اللطف والرحمة وترك ما يستدعيه عظم الشأن وعلو الرتبة على سبيل التمثيل، وخص بالليل لأنه مظنة الخلوات وأنواع الخضوع والعبادات . والسؤال بأين استكشاف عما ظن أنها معتقدة له من الأينية في الإلهية، فلما أشارت إلى السماء علم أنها ليست وثنية وحمل إشارتها على أنها أرادت كونه تعالى خالق السماء فحكم بإيمانها إلى غير ذلك من التأويلات التي ذكرها العلماء لهذه الآيات والأحاديث ونظائرها، فارجع إلى الكتب المبسوطة تظفر بها .
قوله : (إتيان عذابه ) وإنما خصصه بالظلل من الغمام لأن أكثر العقوبات يتقدمها ظلل كما نقل في القصص.
قوله : ( والدنو هو قرب الرسول عليه السلام إلخ) يعني أن دنو الله تعالى من رسوله عليه السلام كناية عما ذكره فلا يظن أنه مبني على أن الضمير في دنا راجع إلى الرسول عليه السلام والأظهر أن يقال دنوه تعالى منه عليه السلام رفع مكانته
قوله : (وخص بالليل إلخ ) وخص بالسماء الدنيا مبالغة بالتلطف .
قوله : ( فلما أشارت إلى السماء علم أنها ليست وثنية وحمل إشارتها إلخ) إدعاء هذا العلم والحمل إن كان بإسنادهما إلى الوحي المقارن لإشارتها إلى السماء فلا كلام فيه وإن كان بإسنادهما إلى نفس الإشارة المذكورة كما هو الظاهر من العبارة، ففيه أنها إنما تدل على ذلك لو كان السؤال بأين الإله وأما إذا كان باين الله تعالى كما الاستدلال فالدلالة المذكورة في المذكورة محل بحث لأن الله إسم خاص لذاته تعالى لم يطلق على غيره لا في الجاهلية ولا الإسلام كما صرحوا به فمن أين يعلم من تلك الإشارة أنها ليست وثنية وإنها تريد أنه تعالى خالق السماء وليست على معتقد كفار العرب من كونه تعالى في السماء على ما ذكره القاضي وغيره في تفسير قوله تعالى : أأمنتم من في السماء ﴾ [ تبارك : ۱۷] ، من جواز كون الآية على زعم العرب فإنهم يزعمون أن الله تعالى في السماء تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً على أنه يلزم مما ذكر عدم مطابقة جواب الخرساء للسؤال بأين الله والقصد إلى خلاف ما يترقب السامع من الجارية الأمية بعيد جداً، ولا رباب المكاشفة فيه وفي أمثاله توجيه ذكر في مفتاح الغيب وغيره
والله أعلم .
The second observation - the first aim: that He, exalted, is not in a direction nor in a place. Then: "Those who are with your Lord" [41:38], "To Him ascends the good word" [35:10], "The angels and the spirit ascend to Him" [70:4], "Do they await but that Allah should come to them in canopies of clouds?" [2:210], "Are you secure from Him who is in the heaven?" [67:16], then "He drew near and came down" [53:8-9], and the hadith of descent: that He descends to the lowest heaven every night, and in a narration every Friday night, saying 'Is there any repentant so I forgive him? Is there any seeker of forgiveness so I forgive him?' And his statement, peace be upon him, to the mute slave girl: 'Where is Allah?' so she pointed to the sky, and he affirmed and did not deny and said she is a believer. So the question and affirmation mentioned indicate direction and place. And the answer is that they are presumptive apparent meanings that do not contradict certainties indicating the negation of place and direction. For whenever two proofs conflict, it is necessary to act upon both as much as possible, so the apparent meanings are interpreted either summarily, delegating the details to Allah, as is the view of those who stop at the apparent meaning, and this is the way of most of the Salaf, as related from Ahmad: 'The istiwā' is known, the modality is unknown, and inquiring about it is an innovation'; or in detail, as is the view of a group, so we say: istiwā' means domination, as in his statement: 'Amr came to dominate over Iraq without sword or shed blood'; and closeness is by honor and generosity, as it is said: 'So-and-so is close to the king'; and 'Your Lord came' means His command; and 'to Him ascends the good word' means He approves it, for the word is an accident that cannot move; and 'Who is in the heaven' means His judgment or His authority or an angel from His angels entrusted with punishment for those deserving; and analogize the rest of the verses and hadiths accordingly. So ascending to Him is ascending to a place where one draws near to Him through acts of obedience; and His coming in canopies is the coming of His punishment; and the drawing near is the Prophet's closeness to Him through obedience, and the estimation of two bow-lengths is a likening of the intellectual to the sensible; and the descent is interpreted as kindness and mercy, and leaving aside what His majesty and high rank demand, by way of representation; and it is specified at night because it is a time for seclusions and types of humility and worship. And the question with 'where' was to investigate what she thought was an attribute of direction in divinity, and when she pointed to the sky, he knew she was not polytheistic, and he interpreted her pointing as meaning that she intended that He is creator of the sky, so he judged her belief, and other such interpretations that scholars have mentioned for these verses and hadiths and their like, so refer to the detailed books to find them. His statement: ('Coming of His punishment') and He specified it with canopies of clouds because most punishments are preceded by canopies as narrated in stories. His statement: ('And the drawing near is the closeness of the Prophet, peace be upon him, etc.') means that the drawing near of Allah from His Prophet is a metaphor for what he mentioned, so one should not think it is based on the pronoun in 'dana' referring to the Prophet; rather, it is said: His drawing near to him is raising his station. His statement: ('And it is specified at night, etc.') and He specified the lowest heaven for exaggeration in kindness. His statement: ('When she pointed to the sky, he knew she was not polytheistic and interpreted her pointing, etc.') Claiming this knowledge and interpretation, if they are attributed to the revelation coinciding with her pointing to the sky, then there is no issue; but if they are attributed to the same mentioned pointing, as is apparent from the expression, then it involves that it only indicates that if the question was 'Where is the deity?' But if it was 'Where is Allah?' as in the inference, then the mentioned indication is debatable, because Allah is a specific name for His essence, not applied to other than Him, neither in pre-Islamic times nor in Islam, as they stated. So how is it known from that pointing that she was not polytheistic and that she intended that He is creator of the sky, and not upon the belief of the Arab disbelievers that He is in the sky, as al-Qāḍī and others mentioned in the interpretation of His statement: 'Are you secure from Him who is in the heaven?' [67:16], regarding the possibility that the verse is according to the belief of the Arabs, for they claimed that Allah is in the sky—exalted is He greatly above that. Moreover, what results from the mentioned is that the slave girl's answer does not match the question 'Where is Allah?', and intending contrary to what the listener expects from an illiterate slave girl is very far-fetched. There is no key to unveiling in this and its like; a direction has been mentioned in Miftāḥ al-Ghayb and elsewhere. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and interpretation of scriptural evidence that appears to attribute direction and place to Allah. The author first lists the problematic verses and hadiths: verses about being with the Lord, ascending, coming in clouds, nearness, and hadiths of descent and the slave girl. These suggest direction and place. The response is that these are ḍawāhir ẓanniyya (presumptive apparent meanings) and cannot override the qaṭʿiyyāt (certainties) that negate place and direction. When two proofs conflict, both must be reconciled as much as possible, so these texts must be interpreted. Two methods are given: (1) tafwīḍ (consignment): accept the wording but delegate the true meaning to Allah, as the Salaf did; (2) taʾwīl (detailed interpretation): provide specific interpretations, e.g., istiwāʾ means domination, closeness means honor, coming means command, ascent means approval, etc. The author then addresses the slave girl hadith: the question 'Where is Allah?' was to test her belief; her pointing to the sky indicated she was not polytheistic, and the Prophet interpreted it as her acknowledgment that Allah is creator of the sky. The commentary then discusses the plausibility of this interpretation, questioning whether such inference from a gesture is valid given that the name 'Allah' is unique. The author notes that these issues have been addressed in larger works. Finally, the author mentions various interpretative details from the commentators.

تأويل
الاستواء
الاستيلاء
النزول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that tafwīḍ is a form of ignorance or that taʾwīl is arbitrary.
Correction: Tafwīḍ is a pious stance of the Salaf, and taʾwīl is based on linguistic and contextual evidence, not arbitrary.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'istiwā'' according to the taʾwīl method in this passage?
A) Sitting on a throne
B) Domination
C) Being settled
D) Unknown

Key Takeaway
The two methods of interpreting anthropomorphic verses are consigning the meaning to Allah (tafwīḍ) and providing detailed interpretations (taʾwīl) based on language and context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' (20:5), do you find it easier to simply affirm and stop thinking (tafwīḍ) or to seek an interpretation (taʾwīl)? Which impulse in your heart feels more reverent, and which one might be a sign of overreliance on your own mind? Commit to learning the scholarly interpretations before settling on your own comfort.
?
The author mentions that taʾwīl is based on language and context, not imagination. Are there times when you have interpreted Allah's names or attributes based on your own feelings rather than the understanding of the scholars? How can you train yourself to defer to their method?
?
The slave girl's response 'in the heaven' was accepted because it indicated she was not a worshipper of idols. When people ask you about Allah's location, what is your immediate reaction? Do you have a response that is both correct and kind? If not, what will you learn to say?

The Slave Girl Hadith and Its Analysis
The author interprets the slave girl hadith and the commentator critiques it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the interpretation of the hadith where the Prophet asked a slave girl 'Where is Allah?' and she pointed to the sky, including the scholarly analysis of whether this indicates direction or not.

Core Idea
The slave girl's pointing was interpreted as acknowledgment of Allah's transcendence, not location.

The hadith of the slave girl is often used to argue that Allah is in a direction (the sky). The author provides an interpretation: the question was to investigate her belief, and her pointing to the sky was taken as an affirmation that Allah is the Creator of the sky, not that He is located there. However, the passage also includes a critical analysis from the commentator questioning whether such an inference is valid, given the uniqueness of the name 'Allah' and the possibility that she shared the belief of the Arab polytheists.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the author's interpretation
The Prophet asked 'Where is Allah?' and she pointed to the sky; he affirmed her faith. The author says the question was to test her belief, and her pointing was interpreted as meaning He is the Creator of the sky.
This interpretation avoids implying direction.
2
Examine the commentator's critique
The commentator raises a problem: if the question used the name 'Allah', which is unique to God, how can we be sure she wasn't thinking of a deity in the sky? The inference from pointing alone is not certain.
This shows the depth of scholarly debate.
3
Understand the resolution
The commentator suggests that perhaps the Prophet had divine knowledge (waḥy) of her belief. Otherwise, the inference is debatable. The author refers to other works for a full discussion.

Source Text

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الأول : أنه تعالى ليس في جهة ولا في مكان
فإن استكبروا فالذين عند ربك ) [فصلت : ۳۸ ]، و إليه يصعد الكلم الطيب ﴾ [فاطر: ۱۰]، تعرج الملائكة والروح إليه ﴾ [المعارج : ٤]، وهل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم في ظلل من الغمام أمنتم من في السماء أن يخسف بكم الأرض ثم دنى فتدلى فكان قاب قوسين أو أدنى ﴾ [البقرة : ٢١٠]، وحديث النزول وهو أنه تعالى ينزل إلى السماء الدنيا في كل ليلة وفي رواية في كل ليلة جمعة فيقول هل من تائب فأتوب عليه هل من مستغفر فأغفر له ) وقوله عليه السلام للجارية الخرساء أين الله فأشارت إلى السماء (فقرر ولم ينكر وقال إنها مؤمنة ) فالسؤال والتقرير المذكوران يشعران بالجهة ) والمكان ) والجواب أنها ظواهر ظنية لا تعارض اليقينيات ( الدالة على نفي المكان والجهة كيف ) ومهما تعارض دليلان وجب العمل بهما ما أمكن فتؤول الظواهر إما اجمالاً ويفوض تفصيله إلى الله كما هو رأى من يقف على الله وعليه أكثر السلف كما روي عن أحمد الاستواء معلوم والكيفية مجهولة والبحث عنها بدعة، وإما تفصيلاً كما هو رأى طائفة فنقول الاستواء الاستيلاء نحو) قوله ( قد استوى عمرو على العراق ( من غير سيف ودم مهراق ( والثندية بمعنى والإكرام ) كما يقال فلان قريب من الملك وجاء ربك أى أمره. وإليه يصدع الكلم الطيب أي يرتضيه فإن الكلم عرض يمتنع عليه الانتقال ومن في السماء أي حكمه أو سلطانه أو ملك من ملائكته ( موكل بالعذاب ( للمستحقين ( وعليه فقس ) سائر والأحاديث فالعروج إليه هو العروج إلى موضع يتقرب إليه بالطاعات فيه وإتيانه في ظل إتيان عذابه والدنو هو قرب الرسول إليه بالطاعة والتقدير بقاب قوسین تصویر للمعقول بالمحسوس والنزول محمول على اللطف والرحمة وترك ما يستدعيه عظم الشأن وعلو الرتبة على سبيل التمثيل، وخص بالليل لأنه مظنة الخلوات وأنواع الخضوع والعبادات . والسؤال بأين استكشاف عما ظن أنها معتقدة له من الأينية في الإلهية، فلما أشارت إلى السماء علم أنها ليست وثنية وحمل إشارتها على أنها أرادت كونه تعالى خالق السماء فحكم بإيمانها إلى غير ذلك من التأويلات التي ذكرها العلماء لهذه الآيات والأحاديث ونظائرها، فارجع إلى الكتب المبسوطة تظفر بها .
قوله : (إتيان عذابه ) وإنما خصصه بالظلل من الغمام لأن أكثر العقوبات يتقدمها ظلل كما نقل في القصص.
قوله : ( والدنو هو قرب الرسول عليه السلام إلخ) يعني أن دنو الله تعالى من رسوله عليه السلام كناية عما ذكره فلا يظن أنه مبني على أن الضمير في دنا راجع إلى الرسول عليه السلام والأظهر أن يقال دنوه تعالى منه عليه السلام رفع مكانته
قوله : (وخص بالليل إلخ ) وخص بالسماء الدنيا مبالغة بالتلطف .
قوله : ( فلما أشارت إلى السماء علم أنها ليست وثنية وحمل إشارتها إلخ) إدعاء هذا العلم والحمل إن كان بإسنادهما إلى الوحي المقارن لإشارتها إلى السماء فلا كلام فيه وإن كان بإسنادهما إلى نفس الإشارة المذكورة كما هو الظاهر من العبارة، ففيه أنها إنما تدل على ذلك لو كان السؤال بأين الإله وأما إذا كان باين الله تعالى كما الاستدلال فالدلالة المذكورة في المذكورة محل بحث لأن الله إسم خاص لذاته تعالى لم يطلق على غيره لا في الجاهلية ولا الإسلام كما صرحوا به فمن أين يعلم من تلك الإشارة أنها ليست وثنية وإنها تريد أنه تعالى خالق السماء وليست على معتقد كفار العرب من كونه تعالى في السماء على ما ذكره القاضي وغيره في تفسير قوله تعالى : أأمنتم من في السماء ﴾ [ تبارك : ۱۷] ، من جواز كون الآية على زعم العرب فإنهم يزعمون أن الله تعالى في السماء تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً على أنه يلزم مما ذكر عدم مطابقة جواب الخرساء للسؤال بأين الله والقصد إلى خلاف ما يترقب السامع من الجارية الأمية بعيد جداً، ولا رباب المكاشفة فيه وفي أمثاله توجيه ذكر في مفتاح الغيب وغيره
والله أعلم .
The second observation - the first aim: that He, exalted, is not in a direction nor in a place. Then: "Those who are with your Lord" [41:38], "To Him ascends the good word" [35:10], "The angels and the spirit ascend to Him" [70:4], "Do they await but that Allah should come to them in canopies of clouds?" [2:210], "Are you secure from Him who is in the heaven?" [67:16], then "He drew near and came down" [53:8-9], and the hadith of descent: that He descends to the lowest heaven every night, and in a narration every Friday night, saying 'Is there any repentant so I forgive him? Is there any seeker of forgiveness so I forgive him?' And his statement, peace be upon him, to the mute slave girl: 'Where is Allah?' so she pointed to the sky, and he affirmed and did not deny and said she is a believer. So the question and affirmation mentioned indicate direction and place. And the answer is that they are presumptive apparent meanings that do not contradict certainties indicating the negation of place and direction. For whenever two proofs conflict, it is necessary to act upon both as much as possible, so the apparent meanings are interpreted either summarily, delegating the details to Allah, as is the view of those who stop at the apparent meaning, and this is the way of most of the Salaf, as related from Ahmad: 'The istiwā' is known, the modality is unknown, and inquiring about it is an innovation'; or in detail, as is the view of a group, so we say: istiwā' means domination, as in his statement: 'Amr came to dominate over Iraq without sword or shed blood'; and closeness is by honor and generosity, as it is said: 'So-and-so is close to the king'; and 'Your Lord came' means His command; and 'to Him ascends the good word' means He approves it, for the word is an accident that cannot move; and 'Who is in the heaven' means His judgment or His authority or an angel from His angels entrusted with punishment for those deserving; and analogize the rest of the verses and hadiths accordingly. So ascending to Him is ascending to a place where one draws near to Him through acts of obedience; and His coming in canopies is the coming of His punishment; and the drawing near is the Prophet's closeness to Him through obedience, and the estimation of two bow-lengths is a likening of the intellectual to the sensible; and the descent is interpreted as kindness and mercy, and leaving aside what His majesty and high rank demand, by way of representation; and it is specified at night because it is a time for seclusions and types of humility and worship. And the question with 'where' was to investigate what she thought was an attribute of direction in divinity, and when she pointed to the sky, he knew she was not polytheistic, and he interpreted her pointing as meaning that she intended that He is creator of the sky, so he judged her belief, and other such interpretations that scholars have mentioned for these verses and hadiths and their like, so refer to the detailed books to find them. His statement: ('Coming of His punishment') and He specified it with canopies of clouds because most punishments are preceded by canopies as narrated in stories. His statement: ('And the drawing near is the closeness of the Prophet, peace be upon him, etc.') means that the drawing near of Allah from His Prophet is a metaphor for what he mentioned, so one should not think it is based on the pronoun in 'dana' referring to the Prophet; rather, it is said: His drawing near to him is raising his station. His statement: ('And it is specified at night, etc.') and He specified the lowest heaven for exaggeration in kindness. His statement: ('When she pointed to the sky, he knew she was not polytheistic and interpreted her pointing, etc.') Claiming this knowledge and interpretation, if they are attributed to the revelation coinciding with her pointing to the sky, then there is no issue; but if they are attributed to the same mentioned pointing, as is apparent from the expression, then it involves that it only indicates that if the question was 'Where is the deity?' But if it was 'Where is Allah?' as in the inference, then the mentioned indication is debatable, because Allah is a specific name for His essence, not applied to other than Him, neither in pre-Islamic times nor in Islam, as they stated. So how is it known from that pointing that she was not polytheistic and that she intended that He is creator of the sky, and not upon the belief of the Arab disbelievers that He is in the sky, as al-Qāḍī and others mentioned in the interpretation of His statement: 'Are you secure from Him who is in the heaven?' [67:16], regarding the possibility that the verse is according to the belief of the Arabs, for they claimed that Allah is in the sky—exalted is He greatly above that. Moreover, what results from the mentioned is that the slave girl's answer does not match the question 'Where is Allah?', and intending contrary to what the listener expects from an illiterate slave girl is very far-fetched. There is no key to unveiling in this and its like; a direction has been mentioned in Miftāḥ al-Ghayb and elsewhere. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and interpretation of scriptural evidence that appears to attribute direction and place to Allah. The author first lists the problematic verses and hadiths: verses about being with the Lord, ascending, coming in clouds, nearness, and hadiths of descent and the slave girl. These suggest direction and place. The response is that these are ḍawāhir ẓanniyya (presumptive apparent meanings) and cannot override the qaṭʿiyyāt (certainties) that negate place and direction. When two proofs conflict, both must be reconciled as much as possible, so these texts must be interpreted. Two methods are given: (1) tafwīḍ (consignment): accept the wording but delegate the true meaning to Allah, as the Salaf did; (2) taʾwīl (detailed interpretation): provide specific interpretations, e.g., istiwāʾ means domination, closeness means honor, coming means command, ascent means approval, etc. The author then addresses the slave girl hadith: the question 'Where is Allah?' was to test her belief; her pointing to the sky indicated she was not polytheistic, and the Prophet interpreted it as her acknowledgment that Allah is creator of the sky. The commentary then discusses the plausibility of this interpretation, questioning whether such inference from a gesture is valid given that the name 'Allah' is unique. The author notes that these issues have been addressed in larger works. Finally, the author mentions various interpretative details from the commentators.

Why Allah Cannot Be a Body
Classification of corporealist views and rational refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the authors of kalam rationally prove that Allah cannot be a body and how they categorize and refute corporealist claims.

Core Idea
If Allah were a body, He would be spatial, composite, and temporal — all impossible for the Creator.

This passage tackles a central kalam claim: Allah is not a body. It first surveys the different corporealist groups and their views, then provides the decisive rational proof. Understanding this argument is key to grasping Islamic transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Note the structure: first the classification of views, then the rational proof.
The author uses a concise, proof-based style.
2
Identify the groups and their positions
Distinguish the Karramiyya (some said 'body' meaning existent or self-subsistent, leading to a verbal dispute) from the Mujassima (who said body in reality with specific human-like attributes).
The Karramiyya disagreement may be merely verbal.
3
Understand the verbal dispute nuance
The author notes that if the dispute is only about terminology and no revealed term is involved, there is no real conflict. But the Mujassima go beyond wording to real attributes.
This shows the importance of tawqīf (revelation) in naming.
4
Analyze the rational proof
The proof has three implications: being a body entails spatiality (already refuted), composition, and temporality. Also, it would require attributes of bodies leading to contradictions.
Tracing these logical consequences is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : في أنه تعالى ليس بجسم
ثم اختلفوا ( فالكرامية ) أي بعضهم قالوا هو جسم أي موجود وقوم) جسم آخرون منهم ( قالوا هو جسم أي قائم بنفسه فلا نزاع معهم ) على التفسيرين (إلا في التسمية) أي إطلاق لفظ الجسم عليه ) ومأخذها التوقيف ولا توقيف) هاهنا ) والمجسمة قالوا هو جسم حقيقة فقيل) مركب من لحم ودم كمقاتل ابن سلیمان ) وغيره ( وقيل ) هو ( نور يتلألأ كالسبيكة البيضاء وطوله سبعة أشبار من شير نفسه ومنهم ) أي من المجسمة ( من ) يبالغ ) ويقول إنه على صورة إنسان فقيل شاب أمر دجعد قطط ( أي شديد الجعودة ( وقيل ) هو شيخ أشمط الرأس واللحية تعالى الله عن قول المبطلين والمعتمد في بطلانه أنه لو كان جسماً لكان متحيزاً واللازم قد أبطلناه ) في المقصد الأول، وأيضا يلزم تركبه وحدوثه لأن كل جسم كذلك وأيضاً فإن كان جسماً لا تصف بصفات الأجسام، أما كلها فيجتمع الضدان
قوله : ( وذهب بعض الجهال إلخ ) إذا كان النزاع مع البعض في اللفظ لا في المعنى فالحكم بالجهل مطلقاً إما على التغليب أو على إطلاق الإسم مع عدمورود الإذن ومع الإيهام بما لا يجوز حقه تعالى أصلا على أن أجراء الجسم مجرى الموجود القادم بنفسه من آثار الجهل، لأنه مخالف للعرف واللغة ولما اشتهر من الاصطلاحات .
قوله : (لو كان جسماً لكان متحيزاً) هذا الاستدلال على تقدير تمامه إنما يتأتى إذا لم يستدل على نفي التحيز بما يتوقف على نفي جسمتيه كما في بعض الأدلة السابقة .
The second aim: that He, exalted, is not a body. Then they disagreed: the Karramiyya—some of them said He is a body, meaning existent; and another group from them said He is a body, meaning self-subsistent, so there is no dispute with them except in terminology, i.e., applying the word 'body' to Him, and its source is revelation and there is no revelation here. And the Mujassima said He is a body in reality: some said composed of flesh and blood, like Muqatil ibn Sulayman and others; and some said He is a shining light like a white ingot, seven spans long in His own span; and among them—i.e., the Mujassima—some exaggerate and say He is in the form of a human: some said a young man with curly hair, and some said He is an old man with a gray head and beard. Exalted is Allah above the sayings of the false. And the relied-upon proof of its falsehood is that if He were a body, He would be spatial, and the consequent we have already refuted in the first aim; also composition and temporality would be necessary, because every body is so; also if He were a body, He would be described with attributes of bodies, and if all of them, then opposites would meet. His statement: ('And some of the ignorant went, etc.') If the dispute with some is in wording not meaning, then judging them as ignorant absolutely is either by way of predominance or by using the term without permission, and with causing misunderstanding of what does not befit His right, exalted; indeed, treating 'body' as equivalent to 'existing self-subsistent' is from the effects of ignorance, because it contradicts custom, language, and established terminologies. His statement: ('If He were a body, He would be spatial') This argument, assuming its completeness, only applies if one does not argue for the negation of spatiality based on something that depends on negating His being a body, as in some previous proofs.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of corporealist views about Allah. The author states the second aim: that Allah is not a body (jism). He then mentions the different groups: the Karramiyya, who differed among themselves—some said He is a body meaning 'existent', others said meaning 'self-subsistent'. The author notes that if the dispute is merely verbal, there is no real conflict except in terminology, and since such terms are not revealed (tawqīfī), they are not binding. Then the Mujassima (anthropomorphists) are mentioned: they assert that Allah is a body in reality, with various descriptions: some say composed of flesh and blood (like Muqatil ibn Sulayman), some say a shining light of specific dimensions, and some go further to say He is in human form. The author then denounces these views and presents the decisive proof (muʿtamad) of falsehood: if Allah were a body, He would be mutaḥayyiz (occupying space), which was already refuted in the first aim; also composition (tarkīb) and temporality (ḥudūth) would follow, since every body is composite and temporal; also He would be subject to attributes of bodies, and if all attributes were considered, opposites would meet. The commentary then addresses two marginal notes: the first discusses the ruling on those who say 'body' meaning 'existent' or 'self-subsistent'—it may be a verbal dispute, but the absolute judgment of ignorance is either by predominance or due to misleading connotations. The second note clarifies that the proof from spatiality (taḥayyuz) only works if it does not presuppose the negation of being a body itself, which would be circular.

Key Terms
جسم
متحيز
تركيب
حدوث
كرامية
مجسمة
توقيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think calling Allah a 'body' meaning 'existent' or 'self-subsistent' is harmless and does not imply spatiality or composition.
Correction: The author shows that even this verbal usage is problematic because the term 'body' carries connotations and lacks revelatory warrant; moreover, the rational proof shows that if taken literally, it leads to impossible consequences.

Checkpoint
What is the primary rational proof the author relies on to refute that Allah is a body?
A) If Allah were a body, He would be spatial, which was already refuted.
B) The Koran explicitly denies that Allah is a body.
C) There is consensus among Muslims that Allah is not a body.
D) The Karramiyya themselves deny it.

Key Takeaway
The rational proof that Allah cannot be a body is grounded in necessary entailments: spatiality, composition, and temporality, all of which are impossible for the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to think of Allah, do you unconsciously form a mental image, even if vague? This lesson proves any spatial or bodily concept is impossible. Will you actively train yourself to negate all resemblance (tanzīh) in your thoughts? What concrete step can you take today to purify your tawḥīd from subtle anthropomorphism?
?
The Mujassima claimed to honor Allah by describing Him as a body, yet they fell into falsehood. Are there any practices in your worship—such as seeking closeness through physical imagery or attributing human-like actions to Allah—that you do out of perceived devotion but actually contradict His transcendence? How can you ensure your worship is based on revelation, not your own imagination?
?
The author says the verbal dispute with the Karramiyya is 'from the effects of ignorance' because it contradicts custom, language, and established terminologies. Are you careful with the words you use about Allah? Do you ever use phrases that might imply a limitation or imperfection without realizing it? What change will you make in your speech about Allah starting today?

Allah Is Not a Body: The Sects and Rational Proof
Sanusi enumerates erroneous views on divine corporeality and gives a rational proof with four steps.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational arguments decisively reject corporeality for Allah, and why the divergent views of the Karramiyyah and Mujassimah are either merely verbal or plainly false.

Core Idea
If Allah were a body, He would need a creator — but He is the Creator of all bodies.

In this passage, Sanusi sets out the Second Purpose of the Second Observatory: affirming Allah's transcendence beyond body. He first identifies the erroneous positions of those who affirm a body — the Karramiyyah with their terminological dispute, and the Mujassimah with their crass anthropomorphism — then presents a rigorous rational proof showing that corporeality entails impossible consequences. This establishes the definitive stance of Ahl al-Haqq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the enumeration of sects: Karramiyyah vs. Mujassimah.
2
Distinguish the positions of the ignorant
Identify two groups: Karramiyyah (verbal dispute over the meaning of 'body') and Mujassimah (literal anthropomorphism with detailed descriptions). Note that the Karramiyyah's view is less severe because it is only a terminological disagreement.
The passage says 'there is no dispute on the two interpretations except in terminology' – this is key.
3
Outline the rational proof's structure
The proof has four main steps: (1) would be extended (already refuted), (2) would be composite and temporal, (3) would entail accidents leading to contradiction or need for an external preponderant, (4) would be finite requiring a specifying cause. Each step shows an impossibility that follows from corporeality.
These are interdependent but each stands on its own as an argument.
4
Understand the logical necessity
Each consequence (extension, composition, accidents, finitude) implies dependence on something else – a creator, a part, an external cause. Since Allah is the Necessary Being, He cannot depend on anything. Thus corporeality is impossible.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum: if body, then dependent; but Allah is independent; therefore not body.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Second Purpose: Concerning that He, exalted, is not a body. This is the doctrine of the people of truth. Some ignorant people went to [affirm a body], then they differed: The Karramiyyah – some of them said He is a body, i.e., an existent; another group among them said He is a body, i.e., self-subsistent; there is no dispute with them on the two interpretations except in terminology, i.e., applying the word 'body' to Him, and its basis is [divine] authorization, and there is no authorization here. And the Mujassimah said He is a body in reality; it was said [He is] composed of flesh and blood, like Muqatil ibn Sulayman and others, and it was said He is a radiant light like a white ingot, and His length is seven spans of His own span. And among them – i.e., the Mujassimah – are those who exaggerate and say He is in the form of a human, so it was said [He is] a young man with intensely curly hair, and it was said He is an old man with a gray head and beard – exalted is Allah above the saying of the false ones. The relied-upon [proof] for its falsehood is that if He were a body, He would be extended (mutahayyiz), and that consequence we have already refuted in the First Purpose. Also, composition and temporality would be necessitated, because every body is so. Also, if He were a body, He would not be free from the attributes of bodies. If all [attributes], then opposites would combine; or if some, then preference without a preponderant would be necessitated if there is no external preponderant – that being the equal relationship of His essence to all those attributes – or need, i.e., the need of His essence in being described by that part to another. Also, He would be finite, on the supposition of His being a body, so He would inevitably be specified by a particular magnitude and a particular shape. And His specification by them to the exclusion of other bodies would require a specifying agent external to His essence, to avoid preference without a preponderant; then would be necessitated need of another in being described by that shape and magnitude.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines the Second Purpose of the Second Observatory: affirming that Allah is not a body. It begins by stating that this is the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq. Then it describes the positions of the ignorant: the Karramiyyah are divided on the meaning of 'body', but the dispute is merely verbal, and the Mujassimah affirm a literal body, with various descriptions (e.g., composed of flesh, light, human form). The author then presents the relied-upon rational proof: (1) if a body, then extended (mutahayyiz), already refuted; (2) necessitating composition and temporality; (3) would entail subjection to accidents, leading to either impossible combination of opposites or need for an external preponderant; (4) would be finite, requiring a specifying cause and thus dependence. Each step follows from the nature of bodies.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Karramiyyah's view is a genuine theological dispute about Allah's nature.
Correction: The passage clarifies that their disagreement is only about the word 'body' (whether it can be applied to Allah), not about actual corporeality. Sanusi says 'there is no dispute except in terminology'.

Key Takeaway
The rational impossibility of Allah being a body is established by multiple independent arguments, each showing that corporeality entails dependence, which contradicts His nature as the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof shows that if Allah had partial attributes, He would need an external cause to choose them—making Him dependent. But in your daily life, do you sometimes treat Allah as if He needs things (like your worship, or your attention)? How can this truth that Allah is absolutely self-sufficient (al-Ghani) reshape your du'a and your reliance on Him?

Sanusi says the Karramiyyah dispute is merely verbal—they didn't mean a real body. But the Mujassimah did. Allah is Neither Substance nor Accident
Al-Jurjani defines substance and accident and proves their negation in Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot be classified as a substance (jawhar) or an accident (arad) according to both kalam and philosophical definitions.

Core Idea
Allah transcends all categories of contingent beings.

This lesson examines the third purpose of the author: proving that Allah is neither a substance nor an accident. Al-Jurjani presents two arguments against substance based on different traditions, and a single argument against accident. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping divine transcendence in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Jurjani structures his argument: first substance (with two sub-arguments), then accident.
The passage is concise but packed with technical terms.
2
Understand the two arguments against substance
Identify the mutakallim's definition of substance as 'extended by essence' and the philosopher's definition as 'quiddity not in a subject'. Notice why each definition fails to apply to Allah.
The philosopher's argument hinges on the identity of existence and quiddity in Allah.
3
Understand the argument against accident
Accidents need a substrate to exist; Allah is independent of all things. Therefore, He cannot be an accident.
This argument is simpler but equally decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : أنه تعالى ليس جوهراً ولا عرضاً أنه تعالى ليس جوهراً ولا عرضاً أما الجوهر فنقول إنه مسلوب عنه تعالى فإما عند المتكلم فلأنه المتحيز بالذات وقد أبطلناه، وأما عند الحكيم فلأنه ماهية إذا وجدت في الأعيان كانت لا في موضوع وذلك إنما يتصور فيما وجوده غير ماهيته ووجود الواجب نفس ماهيته، فلا يكون جوهراً عندهم أيضاً وأما العرض فلاحتياجه في وجوده إلى محله، والواجب تعالى مستغن عن جميع ما عداه .
The Third Purpose: That He, exalted, is neither a substance nor an accident. He, exalted, is neither a substance nor an accident. As for substance, we say it is negated from Him. For the mutakallim, it is because substance is extended by essence, and we have already refuted that. For the philosopher, it is because substance is a quiddity which, when existing in external objects, is not in a subject; and that is only conceivable when its existence is other than its quiddity. But the existence of the Necessary is identical to its quiddity, so it is not a substance according to them either. As for accident, it is because of its need, in its existence, for its substrate, while the Necessary is independent of all other than Him.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines the Third Purpose: Allah is neither a substance (jawhar) nor an accident (arad). It first addresses substance: (1) From the mutakallim's perspective, substance is defined as 'extended by essence' (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat), which has already been refuted for Allah. (2) From the philosopher's perspective, substance is a quiddity that, when existent in external things, is not in a subject; this definition presupposes a distinction between existence and quiddity, but in Allah existence and quiddity are identical, so He cannot be a substance. Then accident: accidents need a substrate (mahall) to exist, but Allah is independent of all other than Him, so He is not an accident. Thus, both categories are negated.

متحيز بالذات
ماهية
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if Allah is not a body, He could still be a substance or accident; this passage shows why even those are impossible.
Correction: Substance, whether defined as extended or as a distinct quiddity, does not apply to Allah. Accidents are also negated because of their dependency. Allah transcends both categories.

Checkpoint

D) Because substance is composite

Key Takeaway
Allah is neither a substance nor an accident because each category implies limitations or dependencies that are incompatible with His absolute transcendence.

?

God is not a substance or an accident
Sanusi negates substance and accident from God with dual definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be a substance or an accident according to both the mutakallimun and the philosophers.

Core Idea
God's existence is identical to His essence; thus He fits no category of substance or accident.

Sanusi and his commentator establish the transcendence of God by negating that He is a substance (jawhar) or an accident ('arad). They address two understandings of substance: the mutakallim's definition as what occupies space, and the philosopher's definition as a quiddity with existence added. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind both negations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once without analysing. Notice the dual definitions of substance.
The passage is concise but packed with two arguments.
2
Understand the mutakallim's definition
Substance is defined as what occupies space (mutahayyiz). Why is this refuted for God? Reflect on God's transcendence of spatial dimensions.
This is the earlier refutation of God being a body.
3
Understand the philosopher's definition
A substance is a quiddity whose existence is other than its essence. Why doesn't God fit this? Because God's existence is identical to His quiddity.
This is a key metaphysical distinction.
4
Necessity of accident negation
Accident needs a locus; God is independent. This is straightforward from the concept of divine self-sufficiency.
5
Summarise the key point
Write one sentence in your own words: why God is neither substance nor accident.
Compare with the core_idea above.
