The answer: the argument is based on the similarity of bodies, and it was mentioned in Jawahir al-Maqasid that this is a principle on which many foundations of Islam are built. So it is not permissible for the specifier to be the will, for then the discussion would shift to His, exalted, being specified by that will, given that some attributes have no role in determining His prior qualification with the will, so the will would not be a preponderant for His qualification with them. Also, what is based on will is temporal, as is well known, so it would necessitate that He, exalted, be a locus for temporal events. His statement: (the specifier is external.) This is also based on the similarity of bodies, and it is refuted by the qualification of the Necessary, exalted, with His attributes without their opposites. This is pure delusion, for the essence of the Necessary, exalted, is different from all essences, so there is no objection in its requiring specificity of attributes, unlike when it is similar to bodies. His statement: (only conceivable for that whose existence is other than its quiddity.) This statement was mentioned at the beginning of the Station of Accidents as well, and the commentator explicitly stated there that his phrase 'when it exists' indicates the addition of existence. We have previously discussed this, that the addition of absolute existence is not disputed among the philosophers, so why is that not sufficient for the applicability of this definition to the Necessary? And we have elaborated on it to some extent, so refer back to it. His statement: (so it is not a substance according to them either.) It is said: the application of 'substance' to the Creator, glory be to Him, in the sense of
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and objection-response section from the commentary on al-Mawaqif. It addresses the third purpose (al-maqsad al-thalith) of the second observatory, which is to negate that God is a substance or an accident. The author first presents the reasoning of those who assert that God is a body (presumably the anthropomorphists) and then refutes it. The proof for God's non-corporeality is given by showing that if God were a body, He would be finite, specified with a particular quantity and form, necessitating an external specifier and thus need, which is impossible for God. The author then states the correct position: God is not a substance or an accident. For the mutakallim, substance is defined as what occupies space, which has been refuted; for the philosopher, substance is a quiddity existing not in a subject, which requires that existence be additional to quiddity, but God's existence is identical to His quiddity. Accident is negated because it needs a locus, while God is independent. The author then addresses several objections (introduced by 'qala') that might arise from the argument, each with its answer. The first objection asks why preponderance cannot be attributed to God's will; the answer distinguishes that the possibility argument is based on the similarity of bodies, and that invoking the will simply shifts the problem to the specification of that will. The second objection challenges the need for an external specifier; the answer states that God's essence is unique and can necessitate particular attributes without a need for an external cause, unlike similar bodies. The third objection concerns the definition of substance for philosophers and the addition of existence; the author refers to a previous discussion. The fourth objection questions the negation of 'substance' in the sense of self-subsistent entity; this objection is cut off at the page break. The passage systematically defends the transcendence of God beyond physical categories.

متحيز
ماهية
وجود
واجب

Correction: The passage insists on the literal definitions; God transcends these categories entirely.

Key Takeaway
God is neither a substance nor an accident because these depend on composition or dependence, while God is simple and self-sufficient.

?

If God's existence is identical to His essence, then He is absolutely simple—no parts, no addition. Does your worship reflect belief in a God who cannot be analyzed or compartmentalized? Where do you treat Him as if He were composed?
?
The passage says God is independent of all other than Himself. Is your heart's deepest attachment truly to a God who needs nothing, or do you rely on something else as if it were indispensable? What would it take to make your dependence solely on Him?

The objection about God's will as preponderant
Sanusi answers the objection about God's will as a preponderant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the will cannot be invoked as a preponderant to explain God's specific attributes in the context of the similarity of bodies.

Core Idea
Invoking God's will to explain specification shifts the problem to the specification of the will itself.

An objector suggests that God's will can explain why He has certain attributes rather than others. The commentator responds by showing that this argument presupposes the similarity of bodies, a principle already refuted, and that invoking the will leads to further difficulties if it is considered temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the structure: objection, then answer with multiple points.
The answer has two main parts: similarity of bodies and temporality of will.
2
Understand the base assumption
The argument for needing a preponderant is based on the similarity of bodies. If bodies are not similar, the need for an external preponderant disappears.
This is a key principle in kalam.
3
Why will does not solve it
If will is invoked, the discussion shifts to why that will is specific. This leads to a regress unless the will is itself necessary, but it is temporal.
The temporality issue: if will is temporal, God becomes a locus of temporal events.
4
Connect to divine uniqueness
The uniqueness of God's essence is the ultimate answer: it needs no external cause for its attributes.
This links back to the previous lesson.

For the philosopher, it is because it is a quiddity which, when it exists in external entities, is not in a subject, and that is only conceivable for that whose existence is other than its quiddity, while the existence of the Necessary is identical to Its quiddity, so It is not a substance according to them either. Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and objection-response section from the commentary on al-Mawaqif. It addresses the third purpose (al-maqsad al-thalith) of the second observatory, which is to negate that God is a substance or an accident. The author first presents the reasoning of those who assert that God is a body (presumably the anthropomorphists) and then refutes it. The proof for God's non-corporeality is given by showing that if God were a body, He would be finite, specified with a particular quantity and form, necessitating an external specifier and thus need, which is impossible for God. The author then states the correct position: God is not a substance or an accident. For the mutakallim, substance is defined as what occupies space, which has been refuted; for the philosopher, substance is a quiddity existing not in a subject, which requires that existence be additional to quiddity, but God's existence is identical to His quiddity. Accident is negated because it needs a locus, while God is independent. The author then addresses several objections (introduced by 'qala') that might arise from the argument, each with its answer. The first objection asks why preponderance cannot be attributed to God's will; the answer distinguishes that the possibility argument is based on the similarity of bodies, and that invoking the will simply shifts the problem to the specification of that will. The second objection challenges the need for an external specifier; the answer states that God's essence is unique and can necessitate particular attributes without a need for an external cause, unlike similar bodies. The third objection concerns the definition of substance for philosophers and the addition of existence; the author refers to a previous discussion. The fourth objection questions the negation of 'substance' in the sense of self-subsistent entity; this objection is cut off at the page break. The passage systematically defends the transcendence of God beyond physical categories.

Key Terms
ترجيح
مرجح
إرادة
تماثل
أجسام
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's will can arbitrarily specify any attribute without further cause.
Correction: The passage shows that appealing to will simply pushes the question back to how that will itself is specified.

Checkpoint
Why does invoking God's will not solve the problem of specification?
A) Because God's will is not effective.
B) Because it shifts the problem to the specification of the will itself.
C) Because God does not have a will.
D) Because the will is temporal.

Key Takeaway
The will does not break the need for a preponderant because it itself requires specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself saying 'God wills it' as a way to avoid understanding the deeper reasons for His decrees, will you now stop and ask: what is the rational consistency behind this? How will you change your habit today?
?
If God's will is not arbitrary but follows a consistent wisdom that we are obligated to seek, what does that demand of your own intellectual effort? Can you commit to studying the arguments of kalam, even when they challenge your comfort?
?
The argument refutes the idea that will alone suffices for explanation. In your life, where have you substituted lazy appeals to 'God's will' for the hard work of understanding? What one step will you take this week to replace that habit with genuine inquiry?

Objections on external specifier and substance definition
Sanusi responds to objections about external specifier and substance definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections regarding the external specifier and the definition of substance, and the refutations based on God's unique essence.

Core Idea
God's unique essence can necessitate specific attributes without external cause or composition.

The commentator addresses two more objections. The first challenges the need for an external specifier if bodies are not similar. The second concerns the philosopher's definition of substance and whether it applies to God. Both are answered by emphasizing God's unique, simple essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections and answers
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the first objection is about external specifier, the second about the definition of substance.
The passage ends mid-sentence on a third objection.
2
First objection: external specifier
The objection claims that if bodies are not similar, there is no need for an external specifier. The answer uses analogy: God's unique essence necessitates His attributes without external cause.
This refutes the need for an external specifier altogether.
3
Second objection: definition of substance
The objector questions why the additional existence philosophers affirm for God does not make Him a substance. The commentator refers to an earlier discussion but does not give a full answer here.
This is a technical point about the addition of existence.
4
Notice the cut-off
The text begins a third objection about applying 'substance' to God in another sense but is cut off due to page break.
The answer is incomplete in this lesson.

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

God is not in time
The author denies that God exists in time and explains its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be in time according to both philosophical and theological reasoning, and how His priority over creation is not temporal but essential.

Core Idea
God is not in time; time belongs to the changing world.

This passage from al-Mawaqif establishes a core Islamic theological principle: God is not subject to time. The author presents arguments from both philosophers and theologians, and then addresses the nature of God's priority over the world. Understanding this distinction safeguards against anthropomorphic misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main thesis
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the opening statement: God is not in time. This is the central thesis.
The passage includes commentary objections, but focus first on the main argument.
2
Examine the philosophers' argument
Look for the phrase 'as for the philosophers' (أما عند الحكماء). They argue that time is the measure of motion; since God has no motion or change, He has no relation to time.
Note the distinction between 'concomitance' (muqaranah) and 'being in time' (zamani).
3
Examine the theologians' argument
Find the phrase 'as for us' (وأما عندا). The theologians argue that time is renewed and measures renewed things; the Eternal cannot be in time.
This is a simpler argument based on God's eternity.
4
Understand the distinction between temporal and essential priority
The author discusses whether the world is temporally or essentially originated. Either way, God's priority is not temporal—it is essential priority, meaning He is the cause and the world is the effect.
Temporal priority would imply God is in time, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الرابع : أنه تعالى ليس في زمان
[ المقصد الرابع : أنه تعالى ليس في زمان ]
( أنه تعالى ليس في زمان ) أي ليس وجوده وجوداً زمانياً، ومعنى كون الوجود زمانياً أنه لا يمكن حصوله إلا في زمان، كما أن معنى كونه مكانياً أنه لا يمكن حصوله إلا في مكان ( هذا مما اتفق عليه أرباب الملل ولا نعرف فيه للعقلاء خلافاً)، وإن كان مذهب المجسمة يجر إليه كما يجر إلى الجهة والمكان . ( أما عند الحكماء فلأن الزمان ( عندهم ( مقدار حركة المحدد للجهات ( فلا يتصور فيما لا تعلق له بالحركة والجهة وتوضيحه أن التعين التدريجي زماني بمعنى أنه يتقدر بالزمان وينطبق عليه ولا يتصور وجوده إلا فيه والتغير الدفعي متعلق بالآن الذي هو طرف الزمان فما لا تغير فيه أصلاً لا تعلق له بالزمان قطعاً، نعم وجوده تعالى مقارن للزمان وحاصل مع حصوله وأما أنه زماني أو آني أي واقع في أحدهما فكلا ( وأما عندا فلانه ) أي الزمان ( متجدد يقدر به متجدد فلا يتصور في القديم فأي تفسير فسر الزمان به امتنع ثبوته لله تعالى تنبيه على ما يتضمنه هذا الأصل الذي مهدناه آنفاً ) يعلم مما ذكرنا أنا سواء قلنا العالم حادث بالحدوث الزماني ) كما هو رأينا، أو كما الذاتي) هو رأي الحكيم ( فتقدم الباري سبحانه عليه ) لكونه موجداً إياه (ليس تقدماً زمانياً وإلا لزم كونه تعالى واقعاً في الزمان، بل هو تقدم ذاتي عندهم وقسم
الموجود القائم بنفسه وبمعنى الذات والحقيقة إصطلاح شائع بين الحكماء وعليه قول محمد بن كرام أن الله تعالى إحدى الذوات إحدى الجوهر.
قوله : (كما يجر إلى الجهة (والمكان لفظ يجر يدل على أنهم لا يصرحون بالجهة والمكان، وقد سبق تصريحهم بهما وكأن مراده أن هذا الجر محقق ثابت باعتبار قولهم بالجسمية سواء صرحوا بهما أم لا ويجوز أن يكون إيراد الجر للمشاكلة .
قوله : ( أو الذاتي لفظة أو بمعنى الواو والأفسد المعنى إذ التسوية إما هي بين الأمرين لا
بين أحدهما وفي مثله توجيه آخر ذكرناه في بحث الحمد من حواشينا على المطول . قوله : ( وإلا لزم كونه تعالى واقعاً في الزمان ظاهره مناقض لما فهم من كلام الشارح في مباحث الزمان من أن الثابت إذا نسب إى المتغير بالتقدم الزمني لم يحتج المتقدم إلى الزمان والظاهر التقدم الزماني عند الفلاسفة تقدم لا يجامع فيه القبل البعد ويكفي فيه مقارنة المتقدم لزمان ما لا يقارنه المتأخر، اللهم إلا أن يقال ما ذكره الشارح هاهنا متابعة للمنصف وليس بمختاره كيف وتقدم الباري عز إسمه على الحوادث لا بد أن يكون زمانياً عند الفلاسفة تقدم كما أن تقدم الأب على الابن كذلك عندهم أيضاً، إذ قد صرحوا بوجود اجتماع المتقدم والمتأخر في غير التقدم الزماني، وبذلك استدلوا على سرمدية الزمان وبهذا يعلم أن قوله بل هو تقدم ذاتي عندهم إنما يستقيم بالنسبة إلى القديم من أجزاء العالم.
The Second Observatory - The Fourth Purpose: That He, exalted, is not in time. [The Fourth Purpose: That He, exalted, is not in time.] (That He, exalted, is not in time) meaning His existence is not a temporal existence; the meaning of existence being temporal is that it cannot occur except in time, just as the meaning of being spatial is that it cannot occur except in space. (This is agreed upon by the people of the religions, and we know of no disagreement among the rational people about it), although the doctrine of the anthropomorphists leads to it as it leads to direction and place. (As for the philosophers, because time (according to them) is the measure of the motion of the sphere that determines directions, so it is inconceivable for that which has no relation to motion and direction. Its clarification is that gradual determination is temporal, meaning it is measured by time and corresponds to it, and its existence is inconceivable except in it; and instantaneous change pertains to the 'now' which is the boundary of time. So that which has no change whatsoever has absolutely no relation to time. Yes, His existence, exalted, is concomitant with time and occurs along with its occurrence, but as to being temporal or an 'instantial'—i.e., occurring in either of them—no. (And as for us, because it (i.e., time) is a renewed thing by which renewed things are measured, so it is inconceivable for the Eternal. Whatever interpretation is given to time, its attribution to God, exalted, is impossible. A note on what is implied by this principle we have laid out above). From what we have mentioned, it is known that whether we say the world is temporal in its origination (as is our view) or essential (which is the philosopher's view), the priority of the Creator, glory be to Him, over it, because He is its originator, is not temporal priority; otherwise it would necessitate His, exalted, being located in time. Rather it is essential priority according to them. And a division of the existent that is self-subsistent and in the sense of essence and reality is a common terminology among the philosophers, and upon it is the saying of Muhammad ibn Karram that God, exalted, is one of the essences, one of the substances. His statement: (as it leads to direction and place. The phrase 'it leads' indicates that they do not explicitly state direction and place, and they have previously stated them. It seems that his intent is that this leading is established and fixed based on their assertion of corporeality, whether they state them explicitly or not. And it is possible that the use of 'leads' is for rhetorical effect. His statement: (or essential.) The word 'or' is in the sense of 'and', and the meaning is corrupted, for the equalization is between the two things, not between one of them. In such cases there is another explanation we mentioned in the discussion of 'al-hamd' in our glosses on al-Mutawwal. His statement: (and otherwise He, exalted, would be located in time.) Its apparent meaning contradicts what is understood from the commentator's discussion on time, that when the fixed is related to the changing by temporal priority, the prior does not need time. The apparent view is that temporal priority for the philosophers is a priority in which the before and after do not coexist, and it suffices that the prior is concomitant with some time that the posterior does not accompany. Unless it is said that what the commentator mentions here is a following of al-Musannif and not his choice. For the priority of the Creator, may His name be exalted, over temporal events must be temporal priority according to the philosophers, just as the priority of a father over a son is according to them also, for they have explicitly stated the coexistence of the prior and posterior in non-temporal priority, and by that they argued for the eternity of time. From this it is known that his statement 'rather it is essential priority according to them' is only valid with respect to the eternal parts of the world.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof that God is not in time. The author begins by stating the fourth purpose of the second observatory: God is not in time, meaning His existence is not temporal—it cannot be confined to time. This is agreed upon by all religions and rational people, though the anthropomorphists' doctrine tends toward it. The author then gives two arguments: one from the philosophers and one from the theologians (implied by 'and as for us'). For the philosophers, time is the measure of the motion of the sphere that determines directions, so what has no motion or direction cannot be in time. Gradual determination is temporal, and instantaneous change is linked to the 'now' (the limit of time); but God has no change, so He has no relation to time. His existence is concomitant with time, not located in it. For the theologians, time is a renewed thing measuring renewed things, so the Eternal cannot be in time. The author then discusses the priority of God over the world: whether the world is temporally originated (theologians) or essentially originated (philosophers), God's priority is not temporal, otherwise He would be in time; it is essential priority. The passage also addresses several objections from a commentator (with 'qala'). The first objection deals with the phrase 'as it leads to direction and place', clarifying that anthropomorphists do not always explicitly state direction and place, but their corporealism implies it. The second objection concerns the word 'or' in 'or essential', explaining that it means 'and' here and that the equalization is between two things, not one. The third objection tackles the implication that if God's priority were temporal, He would be in time; the commentator notes that this appears to contradict a previous statement about the prior not needing time, but explains that temporal priority for philosophers means non-coexistence of before and after, and that God's priority over temporal events is indeed temporal priority for them, as they argue for the eternity of time. The author ultimately shows that the phrase 'essential priority' only applies to the eternal parts of the world, not to God's priority over temporal things. The passage carefully distinguishes different senses of priority and temporality to safeguard God's transcendence.

زماني
تقدم
تقدم ذاتي
آن
حركة
مقارن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because God's existence is concomitant with time, He is in time.
Correction: The author clarifies that God's existence is concomitant (muqaran) with time, not located in it (zamani). Being concomitant means occurring simultaneously but not being contained by time.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers deny that God is in time?
A) Because time is a created thing and God is the creator of time.
B) Because time is the measure of motion, and God has no motion or change.
C) Because time is a substance and God is not a substance.
D) Because time is eternal and God is eternal.

Key Takeaway
God's existence is not temporal; He is not located in time, and His priority over creation is essential, not temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often imagine God waiting for your prayer, as if He exists in a timeline alongside you. How does this lesson demolish that mental picture, and what must you now replace it with when you stand before Him in worship?
?
If God's knowledge of your past, present, and future is a single timeless act, does your fear of what He knows about your sins drive you to despair or to a deeper repentance? Be honest: do you live as if He is watching you in time, or as if He is the eternal ground of your very existence?
?
This lesson insists that God's priority over creation is essential, not temporal. How does this reshape your understanding of your own dependence on Him? Will you begin to see every moment—past, present, future—as equally in need of His timeless creative act, or will you slip back into thinking of Him as merely 'before' you in time?

Definition of Temporal Existence and the Consensus
Author defines temporal existence and states consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of temporal existence and the unanimous consensus among rational people that God is not temporal.

Core Idea
God’s existence is not temporal by definition and consensus.

This lesson begins by defining what it means for something to be temporal, drawing an analogy to spatial existence. The author then asserts that this is a point of agreement among all religious groups and rational thinkers, though some heretical views may implicitly lead to temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of temporal existence
Notice that the author defines temporal existence as existence that cannot occur except in time, similar to spatial existence that cannot occur except in place.
2
Note the consensus claim
The author states that this is agreed upon by all people of religions and rational people, with no known disagreement.
This is a strong claim of unanimous agreement.
3
Consider the exception of corporealists
The author mentions that the school of corporealists (mujassimah) leads to temporality just as it leads to direction and place.
This shows that the consensus is among the mainstream, not all groups.

The Second Observatory - The Fourth Purpose: That He, Exalted, is not in time. (That He, Exalted, is not in time) meaning His existence is not a temporal existence. The meaning of existence being temporal is that it cannot occur except in time, just as the meaning of it being spatial is that it cannot occur except in place. (This is something agreed upon by the people of religions, and we know of no disagreement among the rational people about it), even though the school of the corporealists leads to it as it leads to direction and place. (As for the philosophers, because time (according to them) is the measure of the motion of the sphere that determines directions, so it is inconceivable for something that has no relation to motion and direction. Its clarification: gradual determination is temporal, meaning it is measured by time and corresponds to it, and its existence is inconceivable except in it. Instantaneous change is related to the 'now' which is the extremity of time. So what has no change at all has no relation to time at all. Yes, His existence, Exalted, is concomitant with time and occurs with its occurrence, but as for being temporal or instantaneous, i.e., occurring in one of them, no.) (And as for the [theologians], because it (i.e., time) is something renewed by which what is renewed is measured, so it is inconceivable for the Eternal. Any interpretation by which time is interpreted, its attribution to God is impossible.)
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines the temporal existence and states the consensus that God is not in time. The author first explains that 'not in time' means His existence is not temporal, i.e., it is not such that it can only occur in time, analogous to spatial existence. He then notes that this is agreed upon by all religious groups and rational people, although the anthropomorphists' position inevitably leads to affirming time for God just as it leads to affirming direction and place. The author then presents two proofs. The first is from the philosophers: time is the measure of the motion of the sphere that determines directions; therefore, anything unrelated to motion and direction cannot be temporal. God has no change or motion, so He has no relation to time. His existence is concomitant with time but not located in it. The second proof is from the theologians: time is a continuous succession that measures change; it cannot be conceived for the Eternal, who is unchanging. Thus, any definition of time makes its attribution to God impossible.

Key Takeaway
Temporal existence means being confined to time, and God is unanimously agreed to be beyond that.

?

?

?
The passage says this truth is agreed upon by all religions and rational people. Yet how often does your daily anxiety act as if God is caught in the same rush of minutes you are? What practical step can you take this hour to honor the fact that your Lord is not temporal?

Philosophical and Theological Proofs That God Is Not Temporal
Author gives two proofs: from philosophers and theologians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs—philosophical and theological—that establish God’s non-temporality.

Core Idea
Time is tied to motion and change; God, being eternal and unchanging, cannot be temporal.

After establishing the consensus, the author now provides two rational arguments. The philosophers argue that time is the measure of motion, so what has no motion cannot be temporal. The theologians argue that time is a succession measuring change, so the Eternal cannot be subject to it.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Note how they define time as the measure of the motion of the sphere that determines directions.
God has no relation to motion or change, so He cannot be temporal.
2
Read the theologians' argument
They define time as a renewed succession that measures what is renewed, so it cannot apply to the Eternal.
Both definitions preclude time from applying to God.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author notes that even if time is defined differently, any definition makes its attribution to God impossible.
This is a strong a priori argument.

God Does Not Unite or Indwell: The Argument from Essential Necessity
Author proves that God cannot unite with another nor dwell in anything, based on essential necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot indwell anything based on the definition of indwelling as dependence and its contradiction with essential necessity.

Core Idea
Indwelling implies dependence, but God's necessary existence requires absolute independence.

This lesson continues the discussion of divine transcendence by addressing two potential misconceptions: that God could unite with another being or indwell creation. The author builds on earlier proofs about union and now defines indwelling in a way that clearly shows its impossibility for God. You'll see how a precise definition of a term can resolve a theological question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the key terms: اتحاد, حلول, وجوب ذاتي.
The passage includes a clarifying gloss from the commentator.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage makes two claims: God does not unite with another, and God does not indwell. Find the proof for each.
3
Understand the definition of indwelling
Indwelling (حلول) is defined as existence in a manner of dependence (تبعية). Reflect on why this negates essential necessity.
Dependence here is not spatial but existential.
4
Connect to absolute independence
Essential necessity (الوجوب الذاتي) implies absolute independence (الاستغناء المطلق). How does indwelling contradict that?
5
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding with the multiple-choice question. It directly checks the lesson's main point.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس : أنه تعالى لا يتحد بغيره
[ المقصد الخامس : في أنه تعالى لا يتحد بغيره ]
( في أنه تعالى لا يتحد بغيره لما علمت فيما تقدم) أي في الموقف الثاني ( من امتناع إتحاد الإثنين مطلقاً، و) في ( أنه تعالى لا يجوز أن يحل في غيره ) وذلك ( لأن الحلول هو الحصول على سبيل التبعية وأنه ينفي الوجوب الذاتي ( وأيضاً لو
قوله : ( لأن الحلول هو الحصول على سبيل التبعية لم يرد بها التبعية في التحيز حتى يرد أن الحلول هو الاختصاص الناعت، بل أن الحال تابع للمحل في الجملة وذلك ضروري ومناف للوجوب الذاتي الذي هو منشأ الاستغناء المطلق واستدلالهم على انتفاء الوجوب الذاتي في الصفات بغير هذا لا يقدح فيما ذكرنان إذ تعدد العلل لا ينفيه.
The Second Observatory - The Fifth Purpose: That He, Exalted, does not unite with another. [The Fifth Purpose: That He does not unite with another] (That He does not unite with another because you have known in what preceded) i.e., in the Second Station (the impossibility of the union of two things absolutely, and) (that He, Exalted, is not permitted to dwell in another) and that (because indwelling is existing in a manner of dependence, and that negates essential necessity. And also if... His saying: (Because indwelling is existing in a manner of dependence) - by it is not meant dependence in location such that it would be objected that indwelling is specifying attribute; rather, the indweller is dependent on the locus in general, and that is necessary and contradicts essential necessity which is the source of absolute independence. And their argument for the denial of essential necessity in the attributes by other than this does not invalidate what we mentioned, for multiplicity of causes does not negate it.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage begins the fifth purpose, stating that God does not unite (yatahhad) with another and does not indwell (yahullu) in another. The proof is based on the earlier established impossibility of the union of two entities absolutely (from the Second Station). Additionally, indwelling (hulul) is defined as existing in a mode of dependency (taba'iyya), which negates the essential necessity (wujub dhati) of God, because necessary existence entails absolute independence (istighna' mutlaq). The author adds a gloss clarifying that 'dependency' here does not mean spatial dependency, which would be the special characteristic of a locus; rather, the indweller is dependent on the locus in a general sense, and that inevitably contradicts the essential necessity that is the basis of absolute self-sufficiency. The gloss also notes that proving necessary existence in attributes by other means does not invalidate this argument, as multiple lines of causation can converge.

Key Terms
الاتحاد
الحلول
الوجوب الذاتي
التبعية
الاستغناء المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Indwelling (hulul) merely means being inside something, like water in a container.
Correction: The passage defines hulul as existential dependence, not mere spatial containment. Even if spatial, the dependence is what matters: the indweller relies on the locus, which negates God's self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that God cannot dwell (yahullu) in anything?
A) Because dwelling requires a spatial location, which is impossible for God.
B) Because dwelling implies dependence on something else, negating necessary existence.
C) Because dwelling is a form of union, which is impossible.
D) Because the Quran explicitly denies it.

Key Takeaway
God cannot indwell anything because indwelling implies dependence, and essential necessity entails absolute independence from all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any created being or thing that you functionally treat as necessary for your existence—something you feel you cannot live without? How does God's essential necessity and absolute independence expose that attachment as a form of dependence on other than Him? Will you repent and re-center your trust on Him alone?
?
The passage defines indwelling as dependence. Have you ever imagined that God 'needs' your worship or obedience to be complete? If He is absolutely independent, then your acts of devotion cannot add to Him. How does that truth free you from the burden of performing for God, and how does it call you to worship Him purely out of love and gratitude?
?
Consider something you rely on for your identity, security, or worth—like relationships, wealth, or status. The fact that God cannot be dependent shows His perfection. In what ways have you been seeking perfection through dependence on these things? Today, will you confess that dependence and ask God to make you depend on Him with the absolute dependence that reflects His absolute independence?

Refutation of Incarnation and Union
Iji provides several arguments against union and incarnation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proofs that establish the impossibility of Allah incarnating in or uniting with any created thing.

Core Idea
Allah's absolute independence and necessity preclude any form of incarnation or union.

In this passage, Iji presents a series of rigorous arguments demonstrating that the doctrines of incarnation (ḥulūl) and union (ittiḥād) are impossible for the Necessary Being. The arguments draw on core principles of Islamic theology: divine independence, necessity, and transcendence. Understanding these proofs is essential for grasping how kalam defends tawḥīd against anthropomorphic and pantheistic ideas.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation carefully. Let the flow of the arguments come through.
Notice how Iji moves from union to incarnation.
2
Identify the two main topics
The passage starts with the impossibility of union (ittiḥād) and then focuses on incarnation (ḥulūl). Distinguish these two concepts.
3
Analyze the independence argument
Focus on the argument that if Allah is independent by essence, He cannot need a locus. This is a key premise.
4
Examine the divisibility arguments
Iji considers two cases: if the locus is divisible, the indweller becomes divisible; if indivisible, Allah is in the smallest thing. Both lead to absurdities.
5
Consider the objection and response
The opponent might redefine incarnation as special attribution without spatiality. Iji refutes this by pointing to divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : في أنه تعالى لا يتحد بغيره

في أنه تعالى لا يتحد بغيره لما علمت فيما تقدم من امتناع إتحاد الإثنين مطلقاً، و في أنه تعالى لا يجوز أن يحل في غيره وذلك لأن الحلول هو الحصول على سبيل التبعية وأنه ينفي الوجوب الذاتي وأيضاً لو استغني عن المحل لذاته لم يحل فيه، إذ لا بد في الحلول من حاجة ويستحيل أن يعرض للغني بالذات ما يحوجه إلى المحل، لأن ما بالذات لا يزول بالغير وإلا لاحتياج إليه أي إلى المحل لذاته فإن الاستغناء عدم الاحتياج فلا واسطة بينهما، ولزم حينئذ مع حاجة الواجب قدم المحل فيلزم محالان معاً وأيضاً إذا حل في شيء فإن المحل إن قبل الانقسام لزم انقسامه وتركبه واحتياجه إلى أجزائه وهو باطل وإلا أي إن لم يقبل الانقسام كالجوهر الفرد كان الواجب أحقر الأشياء لحلوله فيه وأيضا فلو حل في جسم فذاته قابلة للحلول في الجسم والأجسام متساوية في القبول لتركبها من الجواهر الأفراد المتماثلة، وإنما التخصيص ببعض الأجسام دون بعض للفاعل المختار فلا يمكن الجزم بعدم حلوله في البقة والنواة وإنه ضروري البطلان والخصم معترف به وربما يحتج عليه بأن معنى حلوله في الغير كون تحيزه تبعاً لتحيز المحل فيلزم كونه متحيزاً في جهة، وقد أبطلناه وقد عرفت ضعفه أن الحلول مفسر بالاختصاص الناعت دون التبعية في التحيز كيف وإنه ينتقض بصفاته تعالى فإنها قائمة بذاته ولا تحيز
Fifth topic: That He, exalted, does not unite with another. That He, exalted, does not unite with another, because you have known from what preceded of the impossibility of the union of two things absolutely, and that He, exalted, does not permit that He should indwell in another. And that is because indwelling is occurrence in a dependent manner, and it negates essential necessity. And also, if He were independent of the locus by His essence, He would not indwell in it, for there must be need in indwelling, and it is impossible for the essentially independent to be subject to what makes it need the locus, because what is essential does not cease by another; otherwise He would need it, i.e., the locus, by essence. For independence is the absence of need, so there is no intermediary between them. And then, with the need of the Necessary Being, the pre-eternity of the locus would follow, so two impossibilities would follow together. And also, if He indwells in something, then if the locus accepts division, the division of the Indweller, its composition, and its need for its parts would follow—which is false. And if not, i.e., if it does not accept division like the indivisible atom, then the Necessary would be the most contemptible of things due to His indwelling in it. And also, if He indwells in a body, then His essence is capable of indwelling in the body; and bodies are equal in acceptance due to their composition from similar indivisible atoms. And specification to some bodies rather than others is by the free agent, so it is not possible to be certain of His not indwelling in a flea or a date pit. And that is necessarily false, and the opponent acknowledges it. And perhaps it is argued against it that the meaning of His indwelling in another is that His spatiality is dependent on the spatiality of the locus, so it follows that He is spatially located in a direction, and we have refuted that, and you know its weakness. The indwelling is interpreted as special attribution without dependency in spatiality. How so? And it is also refuted by His attributes, for they subsist in His essence and have no spatiality.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a series of proofs against the doctrines of incarnation (حلول) and union (اتحاد). The author first reminds the reader that union of two distinct things is impossible, and then focuses on the impossibility of incarnation. The first argument: incarnation means the dependent existence of one thing in another, which negates essential necessity (الوجوب الذاتي) because a necessary being must be absolutely independent. Second: if Allah is independent by essence, He cannot need a locus for indwelling; need would contradict independence. Third: if He needed a locus, that locus would have to be pre-eternal (since the necessary being is pre-eternal), leading to two absurdities: the locus being eternal and the necessary being being dependent. Fourth: if the locus is divisible (like a body), then the indweller would also be divisible, which implies composition and need—impossible for Allah. If the locus is indivisible (like an atom), then Allah would be contained in the smallest thing, making Him the most contemptible—absurd. Fifth: if He indwells in one body, then since all bodies are similar in composition, He could indwell in any body, even a flea or a date pit—which is necessarily false and admitted by the opponent. The author then addresses an objection: some define incarnation as spatial dependency, which would imply Allah is spatially limited. The answer is that incarnation is not spatial dependency but 'special attribution' (اختصاص ناعت) without spatiality, and divine attributes themselves (which subsist in His essence without spatiality) serve as a refutation of spatial interpretation.

Key Terms
اتحاد
حلول
وجوب ذاتي
استغناء
تحيز
اختصاص ناعت
الجوهر الفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Incarnation could be possible if defined as a special relation without spatial dependency.
Correction: Even if defined as special attribution, it still implies need and contradicts divine independence, as Iji shows through his arguments.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among the arguments given against incarnation?
A) Incarnation implies need, contradicting essential independence.
B) If the locus is divisible, Allah would be divisible.
C) If the locus is indivisible, Allah would be the most contemptible.
D) Incarnation is impossible because Allah cannot be known.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being's essential independence makes incarnation and union rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah is absolutely independent of everything, including your own acts of worship? How does this belief challenge the way you rely on your own efforts rather than His mercy?
?
If Allah cannot indwell in any created thing, then He is not 'closer' to you by being in a mosque or a holy place. Does this shake your comfort zones? Where do you truly seek His presence?
?
The passage shows that incarnation would make Allah contemptible if He were contained in a tiny atom. How does this refutation humble you when you try to confine Allah to your own limited understanding? Repent of any mental image you have of Him.

Proof from Self-Sufficiency Against Hulul
al-Jurjani presents the first proof against hulul from God's self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against hulul (divine indwelling) based on God's essential self-sufficiency and why it would entail need and the pre-eternity of the locus.

Core Idea
God’s independence makes indwelling impossible because need and independence cannot coexist.

al-Jurjani begins his refutation of hulul by appealing to God's absolute self-sufficiency. If God is independent by essence, He cannot require a locus for His existence. Indwelling would introduce need, contradicting His nature. Moreover, it would force the pre-eternity of the locus, an absurd consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core premise
Read the passage and find the premise that essential independence precludes need.
The argument uses a strict dichotomy.
2
Understand the implication of need
Understand that indwelling requires a relationship of dependence on a locus.
3
Follow the consequence of pre-eternity
Recognize that if God indwells, the locus must be co-eternal with God, leading to two absurdities.
The absurdities are need in God and pre-eternity of a contingent locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استغني عن المحل لذاته لم يحل فيه، إذ لا بد في الحلول من حاجة ويستحيل أن يعرض للغني بالذات ما يحوجه إلى المحل، لأن ما بالذات ما يحوجه إلى المحل لأن ما بالذات لا يزول بالغير ) وإلا لاحتياج إليه، أي إلى المحل ( لذاته ) فإن الاستغناء عدم الاحتياج فلا واسطة بينهما ، ( ولزم) حينئذ مع حاجة الواجب ( قدم المحل ) فيلزم محالان معاً ( وأيضاً ) إذا حل في شيء ( فإن المحل إن قبل الانقسام لزم انقسامه وتركبه واحتياجه إلى أجزائه وهو باطل ( وإلا ) أي إن لم يقبل الانقسام كالجوهر الفرد ( كان ) الواجب ( أحقر الأشياء ) لحلوله فيه ( وأيضا فلو حل في جسم فذاته قابلة للحلول في الجسم ) والأجسام متساوية في القبول لتركبها من الجواهر الأفراد المتماثلة ، ( وإنما التخصيص ) ببعض الأجسام دون بعض ) للفاعل المختار فلا يمكن الجزم بعدم حلوله في البقة ،والنواة وإنه ضروري البطلان
If He were independent of the locus by essence, He would not indwell in it, for indwelling necessarily requires need, and it is impossible for that which is rich by essence to acquire something that would make it need the locus, because what is by essence does not cease by another. Otherwise, it would need it, i.e., the locus (by essence). For independence is lack of need, so there is no intermediary between them. And then, along with the need of the Necessary, the pre-eternity of the locus would be necessary, so two absurdities follow together. Also, if He indwells in something, then if the locus is divisible, His division, composition, and need for its parts would follow, which is false. If not divisible, like the atomic substance, then the Necessary would be the most contemptible of things for indwelling in it. Also, if He indwells in a body, then His essence would be capable of indwelling in bodies, and bodies are equal in capacity due to their composition from similar atomic substances. Specifying some bodies over others is up to the free agent, so one cannot be certain that He does not indwell in a gnat or a date-stone, and that is necessarily false.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a proof against divine indwelling (hulul). It presents two main arguments: (1) from God's self-sufficiency, and (2) from the dilemma of the divisibility of the locus. The first argument: If God were independent by essence, He cannot be in need of a locus for indwelling, because need is incompatible with essential independence. Independence is defined as lack of need, so there is no middle ground. If He were to indwell, it would imply need, which is impossible. Additionally, if He indwells, the locus would have to be pre-eternal (since God is eternal), leading to two absurdities: need in God and the pre-eternity of a contingency. The second argument: If God indwells in something, that locus is either divisible (like a body) or indivisible (like an atom). If divisible, then God would be divided, composed, and need parts. If indivisible, then God would be contained in something small, making Him contemptible. Further, if He indwells in a body, then His essence is capable of indwelling all similar bodies arbitrarily, leading to absurdities like indwelling in a gnat or date-stone. Thus it is necessarily false.

Key Terms
حلول
استغناء
المحل
الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the self-sufficiency argument conclude about God's indwelling?
A) It is possible if the locus is eternal.
B) It is impossible because it would require need.
C) It is possible if the locus is indivisible.
D) It is impossible because God cannot be contained.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute independence precludes any need for a locus, making hulul impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even subconsciously, felt that God 'needs' your worship or presence to be complete? How does this proof's assertion of absolute self-sufficiency challenge that feeling, and what does it mean for the sincerity of your worship?
?
This passage shows that any notion of God 'dwelling' in a place or person leads to absurdity. In what aspects of your daily life do you tend to confine God to a place (like a mosque, a prayer mat, or even your heart) as if He needs that container? How will you actively remind yourself of His transcendence beyond all loci?
?
If God is completely self-sufficient, your sins cannot harm Him nor can your good deeds benefit Him. How does this truth reshape your motivation for obedience—are you doing it for Him or for your own need? Commit to one concrete way you will realign your intention today.

Proof from Divisibility Against Hulul
al-Jurjani presents the divisibility argument against hulul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against hulul, which shows that any form of indwelling leads to absurd consequences regarding God's nature or His objects.

Core Idea
Indwelling forces God into either divisibility or contemptibility, both impossible.

al-Jurjani continues with a powerful dilemma. If God indwells, the locus is either divisible or indivisible. If divisible, God would be divided and composed. If indivisible, He would be contained in a tiny atom, making Him contemptible. Moreover, if He indwells any body, He could indwell any similar body, leading to absurdities like indwelling in a gnat or date-stone.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Identify the two possibilities: divisible locus and indivisible atom.
2
Analyze the divisible case
If divisible, God would be divisible and composite, contradicting His simplicity.
3
Analyze the indivisible case
If indivisible, God would be contained in the smallest atom, making Him contemptible.
4
Consider the broader implication for bodies
If indwelling a body, then God could indwell all similar bodies, leading to absurdities like indwelling a gnat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If He were independent of the locus by essence, He would not indwell in it, for indwelling necessarily requires need, and it is impossible for that which is rich by essence to acquire something that would make it need the locus, because what is by essence does not cease by another. Otherwise, it would need it, i.e., the locus (by essence). For independence is lack of need, so there is no intermediary between them. This passage is a proof against divine indwelling (hulul). It presents two main arguments: (1) from God's self-sufficiency, and (2) from the dilemma of the divisibility of the locus. The first argument: If God were independent by essence, He cannot be in need of a locus for indwelling, because need is incompatible with essential independence. Refutation of the Objection on Hulul as Distinctive Attachment
Al-Jurjani refutes the redefinition of hulul as distinctive attachment using attributes as a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of hulul as distinctive attachment (ikhtisas na'it) fails because it would apply to God's attributes, which cannot indwell.

Core Idea
Defining hulul as mere attachment is refuted by the very nature of God's attributes.

Al-Jurjani addresses a subtle objection: perhaps hulul (indwelling) is simply a distinctive attachment, not spatial dependency. He shows that even this refined definition collapses when applied to God's attributes, which subsist without spatial location. The lesson unpacks this argument and the clarifying note on attributes and transfer.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the flow from objection to refutation and the concluding note.
The passage uses technical terms like 'حلول', 'اختصاص ناعت', and 'انتقال'.
2
Identify the opponent's redefinition of hulul
The opponent attempts to avoid the earlier refutation by defining hulul as a distinctive attachment (الاختصاص الناعت) rather than spatial dependency. Highlight this shift.
This redefinition is subtle—it tries to strip hulul of any spatial implication.
3
Understand the counterexample using divine attributes
Al-Jurjani argues that even this redefinition is contradicted by God's attributes. They subsist in His essence by a distinctive attachment, yet they are not considered 'indwelling' in another. Why?
If hulul were mere attachment, then attributes would be hulul, which is false.
4
Analyze the note on transfer (intiqal)
The final note clarifies that transfer is impossible for attributes; it is a property only of bodies. This reinforces why attributes cannot indwell.
This distinction between essences and attributes is crucial for divine transcendence.

And the opponent might object that the meaning of His indwelling in another is that His spatial occupancy is dependent on the locus's spatial occupancy, so He would be spatially located in a direction, and we have already refuted that and you know its weakness. That indwelling is interpreted as distinctive attachment, not dependency in spatial occupation. Moreover, it is contradicted by His attributes, for they subsist in His essence and are not spatially located. Note: Just as His essence does not indwell in another, His attribute does not indwell in another, because transfer is inconceivable for attributes; rather it is a property of essences, not absolutely, but of bodies.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a note. The opponent might define hulul as the dependency of God's spatial location on that of the locus, implying God is in a direction. The author reminds that this has been refuted. Instead, hulul is defined as a distinctive attachment (ikhtisas na'it) that does not entail spatial occupation. The author then points out that even this definition is contradicted by God's attributes, because they subsist in His essence without being spatially located—if hulul were mere attachment, attributes would be hulul, which is false. The following note (tanbih) reinforces that attributes do not indwell in another because transfer (intiqal) is not applicable to attributes; it is a property of bodies, not of essences generally.

Misconception: One might think that a non-spatial definition of hulul (distinctive attachment) avoids the problem, but it would still apply to attributes, which are agreed not to indwell.
Correction: Attributes are attached to God but do not indwell; hulul must involve more than mere attachment, otherwise attributes would be hulul, which is false.

Checkpoint

C) Because it is not scriptural.
D) Because it implies directionality.

Key Takeaway
Hulul cannot be reduced to mere attachment because even divine attributes, which are attached to God, do not 'indwell' in Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely caught yourself thinking that Allah’s mercy 'fills your heart' or that His knowledge 'enters your mind.' But according to this lesson, His attributes do not indwell in anything—they subsist in His essence alone. Where did you get the notion that divine attributes can enter creation? What does that reveal about your understanding of tawhid?
?
The opponent tried to save hulul by calling it mere attachment, but the author refuted it using God’s own attributes. Have you ever, in your worship or du‘a, imagined that by calling upon Allah’s names you are bringing those attributes into your own being—making you wise, powerful, or merciful in a way that attaches His attributes to you? How does this lesson correct that subtle shirk?
?
The note says that transfer (intiqal) is a property of bodies, not of essences or attributes. Reflect on your own breath, your thoughts, your feelings—do you treat them as if they 'move' from your essence to your actions? Now consider Allah’s attributes: they do not move or transfer. How does this silence the whispers that try to anthropomorphize Allah? Will you let this knowledge change how you describe Allah in your daily speech? What will you do differently tomorrow?

The Two Principles: Refuting Union and Indwelling
Al-Jurjani states two principles and enumerates eight Christian positions, refuting all but one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two principles of divine transcendence (denial of union and indwelling) and how Al-Jurjani refutes eight Christian positions based on them.

Core Idea
God does not unite with or indwell in any creation—neither His essence nor His attributes.

Al-Jurjani establishes two foundational principles: that Allah neither unites with nor indwells in any created being. He then applies this to Christian claims about Jesus, enumerating eight possible positions and showing that all but one are false. This lesson examines those principles and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author's own structure come through before analyzing.
The passage is an observation under the fifth purpose.
2
Identify the two principles
Notice that the first principle is denial of union (الاتحاد), and the second is denial of indwelling (الحلول). Both are stated at the beginning.
The note extends indwelling from essence to attributes.
3
Understand the note on attributes
The note explains that just as the essence does not indwell, neither does an attribute, because transition is not conceivable for attributes.
This blocks any claim that God's attribute could reside in creation.
4
Examine the eight Christian positions
The passage lists six possibilities of union/indwelling (essence or attribute, in body or soul) and two additional: granting creative power or honorific sonship.
All are false except the last (honorific sonship).
5
Review the refutation and note
The first six are refuted by the impossibility of union/indwelling; the seventh by the principle that only God creates; the eighth is not refuted but is not Islamic doctrine.
The author also notes he will not detail other sects due to incompleteness.

And since their speech is confused, and therefore their transmission differs, he indicated what suffices for the purpose and said: The summary of their doctrine is that either they say: union of God's essence with the Messiah, or indwelling of His essence in him, or indwelling of His attribute in him - all of that either in his body, i.e., the body of Jesus, or in his soul. Commentary

All except the last are invalid. The first six are invalid due to the impossibility of union and indwelling. The seventh is invalid because only God can create. The eighth (honorific sonship) is acceptable in principle but not in Islamic law. The author also notes that he will not detail the sects further due to the book's incompleteness.

The Third Proof: God Cannot Be Affected by Another
Third argument: God cannot be affected by another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against temporal events in God: that He cannot be affected by another, and how the objection from the 'renewed' is answered.

Core Idea
God's essence cannot be influenced by any external cause.

Sanusi presents the third argument against temporal events in God: if a temporal event subsisted in Him, He would be affected and changed by another, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. He then addresses an objection concerning the 'renewed' (al-mutajaddid), clarifying that being affected by a non-existent is merely conceptual. This lesson unpacks the logic of the proof and its defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how compact the argument is.
The passage includes an objection and a reference to earlier discussion.
2
Identify the main claim and entailment
The main claim is that God cannot be affected by another (lā yataʾaththaru ʿan ghayrihī). The entailment: if a temporal event subsisted in Him, His essence would be affected and changed.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objection says this argument is invalidated by the 'renewed' (al-mutajaddid). The response: being affected by a non-existent is purely conceptual (iʿtibārī maḥḍ), not real.
The 'renewed' refers to states or relations that come into existence without a cause.
4
Connect to prior knowledge
The passage references a previous discussion about the eternity of possibility and its entailment. This is part of the universal matters (al-umūr al-ʿāmma) covered elsewhere.
This shows how arguments build on earlier foundational concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث منها أنه تعالى لا يتأثر عن غيره ولو قام به حادث لكانت ذاته متأثرة عن الغير متغيرة به. قوله: (الثالث منها أنه تعالى إلخ) فإن قلت هذا الوجه أيضاً منقوض بالمتجدد قلت ممنوع فإن التأثر عن غير الموجود اعتباري محض. قوله : ( وهذا ليس بلازم لأن أزلية الإمكان إلخ ) قد سبق في الأمور العامة اعتراض الشارح هذا الكلام وبيان الاستلزام بلا محذور فلذا لم يتعرض له هاهنا.
The third of them: He, exalted, cannot be affected by another (lā yataʾaththaru ʿan ghayrihī). If a temporal event subsisted in Him, His essence would be affected by another and changed by it. His saying: (The third of them, may He be exalted) — then if you say that this aspect also is invalidated by the renewed (al-mutajaddid), I say: it is denied, for being affected by a non-existent thing is purely conceptual (iʿtibārī maḥḍ). His saying: (And this is not necessary because the eternity of possibility...) — it has been mentioned before in the universal matters (al-umūr al-ʿāmma) the objector's objection to this statement and the demonstration of the entailment without consequence, so he did not address it here.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This short passage presents the third argument against temporal events in God. The argument is that God cannot be affected by another (lā yataʾaththaru ʿan ghayrihī). If a temporal event subsisted in His essence, that would mean His essence is affected (mutaʾaththira) and changed (mutaghayyira) by an external cause. Since God is independent and not subject to external influences, this is impossible. A potential objection is raised: this argument is invalidated by the 'renewed' (al-mutajaddid) because the renewed also seems to involve being affected. The author dismisses this: being affected by a non-existent (like a future event before it occurs) is merely conceptual (iʿtibārī), not real. The passage then references a previous discussion about the eternity of possibility and its entailment, saying it has been covered elsewhere in the universal matters (al-umūr al-ʿāmma), so there is no need to repeat it.

Key Terms
التأثر (al-taʾaththur: being affected)
الغير (al-ghayr: the other)
المتجدد (al-mutajaddid: the renewed)
اعتباري محض (iʿtibārī maḥḍ: purely conceptual)
الأمور العامة (al-umūr al-ʿāmma: the universal matters)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that God can have any relational attributes.
Correction: The argument only denies that temporal events can cause real change in God's essence; it does not deny relational attributes like 'Creator' which are eternal.

Checkpoint

A) God cannot be described by any attribute.
B) God cannot be affected by another.
C) Temporal events cannot exist at all.
D) God changes with time.

Key Takeaway
God cannot be affected by another because that would entail change in His essence, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof says that being affected by another is a sign of contingency. Think of an area in your life where you are easily swayed by what others think or do. How might seeing God's absolute independence inspire you to become more steadfast and less affected?
?
If God cannot be affected by any cause, then your prayers and actions do not 'change' God. How does that challenge your motivation in worship? Are you seeking to change God, or to change yourself in alignment with His will?
?
The objection about the 'renewed' shows that some 'changes' are merely conceptual. Consider your own relationship with God: have you mistaken a conceptual shift in your understanding for a real change in God? How can you seek to know God as He truly is, beyond your own perceptions?

God's Transcendence and the Distinction in Possibility
Sanusi presents the third argument and refutes the first objection about eternal possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical distinction between the eternity of possibility and the possibility of eternity, and why God's transcendence precludes being affected by accidents.

Core Idea
Eternity of possibility does not imply possibility of eternity—a key distinction in divine transcendence.

In this passage, Sanusi defends God's transcendence by arguing that accidents cannot subsist in Him. He then addresses a key objection: that if God has eternal possibility for accidents, that would imply the possibility of the accident being eternal. Sanusi's answer hinges on a subtle but crucial distinction between 'eternity of possibility' and 'possibility of eternity'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation to grasp the overall argument and flow.
Pay attention to the terms 'أزلية الإمكان' and 'إمكان الأزلية'.
2
Identify the third argument
Find the statement: 'أنه تعالى لا يتأثر عن غيره'. Understand that if an accident subsisted in God, He would be affected and changed by others.
This argument is based on divine transcendence and immutability.
3
Trace the objection and answer
Locate the objection that eternal possibility for accidents would imply the possibility of the accident being eternal. Then find Sanusi's answer distinguishing 'eternity of possibility' from 'possibility of eternity'.
The distinction is subtle but crucial: the former is eternal validity, the latter is impossible.
4
Understand the analogy with creation
Sanusi refutes by analogy: if eternal possibility entailed possibility of eternity for the accident, then God's eternal agency would entail the possibility of the world being eternal (which is false).
This shows the objection leads to an absurdity.
5
Reflect on the rebuttal about attributes
Consider the distinction between essential possibility (necessary) and actual agency (not necessary). Sanusi argues that the relevant possibility is that of action and influence, which is eternal.
This reinforces why the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : ( منها ( أنه تعالى لا يتأثر عن غيره ) ولو قام به حادث لكانت ذاته متأثرة عن الغير متغيرة به ( ويمكن الجواب عن الوجه ( الأول بأن اللازم) مما ذكرتموه من لزوم القابلية للذات هو ( أزلية الصحة ) أي أزلية صحة وجود الحادث، وهذا اللازم ليس بمحال فإن صحة وجود الحادث أزلية بلا شبهة ) والمحال) هو (صحة الأزلية ) أي صحة أزلية وجود الحادث وهذا ليس بلازم لأن أزلية الإمكان تغاير إمكان الأزلية ولا تستلزمه كما في الحوادث اليومية على ما مر تحقيقة . ( فأين أحدهما من الآخر، وأيضاً ما ذكرتموه منقوض ( إذ لو لزم) وصح (لزم) مثله ( في وجود العالم وإيجاده ، فإنه تعالى موصوف في الأزل بصحة إيجاد العالم فيصح في الأزل وجوده فيصح أن يكون العالم أزليا وهو محال فلو لزم من القابلية الأزلية إمكان أزلية الحادث للزم من الفاعلية الأزلية إمكان أزلية العالم ( لايقال القابلية صفة ( ذاتية ) لازمة للذات فيلزم إمكان أزلية المقبول ) دون الفاعلية ) فإنها صفة غير لازمة فلا يلزم إمكان أزلية المفعول ، ( لأنا نقول الكلام في قابلية (الفعل والتأثير فإنها أزلية كما أشرنا إليه فيلزم إمكان أزلية المفعول لا في الفاعلية الحاصلة ( بالفعل)
The third: from them: that He, exalted, is not affected by others. And if an accident were to subsist in His essence, His essence would be affected by others and changed by it. And it is possible to answer the first face: that the necessary consequence from what you mentioned of the necessity of possibility for the essence is the eternity of validity, i.e., the eternity of the validity of the existence of the accident. And this necessary consequence is not impossible, for the validity of the existence of the accident is eternal without doubt. And the impossible is the validity of eternity, i.e., the validity of the eternity of the existence of the accident, and this is not necessary because the eternity of possibility is different from the possibility of eternity and does not entail it, as in daily accidents according to what has been verified. So where is one from the other? Also, what you mentioned is refuted: for if it were necessary and valid, then the like would be necessary in the existence of the world and its origination. For He, exalted, is described in eternity as having the validity of originating the world, so its existence is valid in eternity, so it would be valid that the world be eternal, which is impossible. So if from eternal possibility the possibility of the eternity of the accident were necessary, then from eternal agency the possibility of the eternity of the world would be necessary. It cannot be said that possibility is an essential attribute necessary for the essence, so the possibility of the eternity of the recipient follows, unlike agency, for it is not a necessary attribute, so the possibility of the eternity of the effect does not follow. For we say: the discussion is about the possibility of action and influence, for it is eternal as we indicated, so the possibility of the eternity of the effect follows, not in actual agency.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the possibility of accidents subsisting in God, namely that God cannot be affected by others. The author then provides an answer to the first objection raised against this argument. The objection is that if God has eternal possibility for accidents, that would imply the possibility of the accident being eternal, which is impossible. The answer distinguishes between 'eternity of possibility' (azaliyyat al-imkan) and 'possibility of eternity' (imkan al-azaliyya). The former is the possibility for an accident to exist in time, which is eternal and unobjectionable. The latter is the possibility for the accident to be eternal, which would imply a contradiction. The answer uses an analogy: if eternal possibility entailed possibility of eternity for the accident, then by the same token God's eternal agency would entail possibility of the world being eternal, which is false. A further rebuttal distinguishes between essential possibility (which is necessary for the essence) and actual agency (which is not), but the author replies that the relevant possibility here is the possibility of action and influence, which is eternal.

إمكان الأزلية
القابلية
الفاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God has eternal possibility for an accident, then the accident could be eternal (i.e., exist without beginning).
Correction: Eternal possibility means the validity of the accident's existence at some time is eternal, not that the accident itself could be eternal. These are distinct.

Checkpoint

A) Between essential and accidental attributes
B) Between eternal possibility and possibility of eternity
C) Between possibility in God and possibility in creatures
D) Between necessary and contingent being

Key Takeaway
The eternity of possibility for an accident does not entail the possibility of that accident being eternal, preserving God's transcendence from change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate God's transcendence, do you truly accept that He is completely free from change, or do you secretly expect Him to react to your actions as humans do? How would your worship change if you fully internalized that God is not affected by anything?
?
The distinction between 'eternity of possibility' and 'possibility of eternity' shows how subtle errors in reasoning can distort your understanding of God. Where in your own beliefs might you be blurring boundaries between what is possible for God and what is impossible?
?
If God's eternal agency does not force the world to be eternal, then your existence is a free act of His will—not a necessity. How does that truth confront your sense of entitlement or your tendency to take your own existence for granted?

Answer to third objection: God's intrinsic causation of accidents
Sanusi distinguishes external affecting from intrinsic causation in answering the third objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi distinguishes between being affected by an external cause and having an attribute arise intrinsically from God's essence, thereby defending divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God's essence can be the cause of accidents without being affected by an external agent.

Sanusi addresses the third objection against the possibility of accidents in God. The objection assumes that any new attribute implies being affected by an external cause. Sanusi carefully distinguishes between two interpretations of 'being affected,' showing that only one leads to circular reasoning, while the other allows for intrinsic causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure 'if you mean... then...'.
The passage uses a dialectical style typical of kalam.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find the two meanings of 'being affected' that Sanusi considers. The first is acquisition of an attribute after not having it; the second is the attribute being caused by an external agent.
3
Understand the first interpretation
The first interpretation is exactly the point under debate; rejecting it would be circular (begging the question). Sanusi points out that this is not a valid objection.
This shows the objection fails if taken this way.
4
Understand the second interpretation and the alternative
The second interpretation denies that the attribute is caused by an external agent. Sanusi argues that it could instead be caused by God's own essence, either necessarily (through ordered succession) or by choice (like creating other things).
This preserves divine transcendence while allowing the attribute to be new.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن الجواب عن الوجه الثالث وهو أنك إن أردت بتأثره عن غيره حصول الصفة له بعد إن لم يكن فهو أول المسألة إذ لا معنى لقيام الحادث بذاته تعالى سوى هذا فيكون قولك إنه لا يتأثر عن غيره عين مدعاك فيكون مصادرة على المطلوب، وإن أردت أن هذه الصفة الحادثة تحصيل في ذاته من فاعل غيره فممنوع، أن ذلك لازم من قيام الصفة الحادثة به لجواز أن يكون حصوله في ذاته مقتضى لذاته، أما على سبيل الإيجاب لما ذكرنا من الترتب والتلاحق . وأما على سبيل الاختيار فكما أوجد سائر المحدثات في أوقات مخصوصة يوجد الحادث في ذاته.
And it is possible to answer the third face, which is: if you mean by 'being affected by others' the acquisition of an attribute after it was not, then that is precisely the matter at hand, for there is no meaning to the subsistence of an accident in His essence, exalted, except this. So your statement that He is not affected by others is exactly your claim, and it would be begging the question. And if you mean that this accidental attribute is brought about in His essence by an agent other than Him, then it is denied that this is necessary from the subsistence of the accidental attribute in Him, because it is permissible that its occurrence in His essence be a requisite of His essence, either by way of necessity due to the ordered succession we mentioned, or by way of choice, just as He originated other contingent things in specific times, so He originates the accident in His essence.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage answers the third objection by carefully defining what 'being affected by others' means. If it means simply that an attribute occurs after not having been, then this is exactly what is being debated, and denying it would be circular. If it means that the attribute is caused by an external agent, then it is denied that this follows from the subsistence of the accident, because the accident could be caused by God's own essence, either necessarily through the ordered succession or voluntarily, just as God creates other things in time.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any new attribute in God must come from an external cause.
Correction: The text shows that the accident can be caused by God's own essence, either necessarily or by choice, without external affecting.

Checkpoint
What two possible ways are mentioned by which an accident could be caused by God's essence itself?
A) By necessity and by chance
B) By obligation and by permission
C) By necessity (via ordered succession) and by choice
D) By emanation and by creation

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defends the possibility of accidents in God by showing that they can be intrinsic to His essence, either necessarily or by choice, without implying external affecting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
