The Subject of Vision: Absolute Existence
Objection and response on the cause of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection regarding the cause of vision and the response that the subject of vision is absolute existence, not a specific attribute.

Core Idea
The subject of vision is absolute existence, not a particular attribute.

This passage presents a key objection in the debate on whether God can be seen. The objector argues that if the cause of vision exists in God, then vision should be valid, but the response clarifies that the cause is not a specific attribute but absolute existence, which is common and therefore not subject to conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Begin by reading the objection carefully. The objector argues that if the cause of vision is present in God (the Eternal), then vision should be valid for Him just as it is for contingent beings.
Notice the objection hinges on the idea that the cause is something present in the object.
2
Understand the answer's key concept
The answer reinterprets 'cause' as 'subject' (muta'alliq), which is absolute existence—not a specific attribute or particular existence. This is the core of the response.
Absolute existence means having an identity, not any particular characteristics.
3
Reflect on the example
The example of a distant phantom seen without discerning its particularity illustrates that what makes vision possible is the sheer existence of the object, not its detailed attributes.
This example helps show that absolute existence suffices for vision.
4
Note the conclusion
Since the subject is absolute shared identity, no additional condition or removal of impediment is needed. Thus the objection fails.
This conclusion directly refutes the objector's conditional argument.

Rather, existence is the cause of the validity of a specific reality being visible. (Commentary: 'And in this promotion are other affectations, etc.') We have indicated them in the midst of the discourse. Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section concerning the cause of the validity of vision (ru'ya) of God. It begins by stating a seventh objection: that if the cause of vision's validity exists in the Eternal (i.e., God), then vision should be valid for Him, as it is for contingent beings. The objector suggests that the particularity of the original (the Eternal) could be a condition or the particularity of the branch (the contingent) an impediment. The answer, already presented, clarifies that the 'cause' of vision's validity is actually its 'subject' (muta'alliq), which is absolute existence—i.e., being something with an identity, not specific identities. This is likened to seeing a distant phantom without discerning its particularity. Since the subject is absolute shared identity, no additional conditions or impediments apply. The author then criticizes the rational approach used to prove vision, noting that the concept of absolute identity is a mental consideration (i'tibari) like quiddity, so vision cannot attach to it. What is perceived is the existing particularity, albeit indistinctly. Indistinctness varies, so not every such perception leads to detail. The author concludes that reliance on rational proof is difficult, so one should follow Abu Mansur al-Maturidi in adhering to transmitted texts. The marginal notes comment on specific phrases: the first explains that common properties like opposition are caused by material existence, not existence alone; the second quotes al-Razi rejecting the view that only existence is seen; the third indicates that other affectations have been alluded to. Finally, a refutation is raised using the validity of being-created (sihhat al-makhlooqiyya): since being-created is common between substances and accidents and only existence is common, it would imply the validity of God's being-created, which is impossible. The answer from the author of al-Maqasid is that being-created is purely relational and does not require cause, unlike vision's validity which is realized and negated. Another response grants but says temporality (huduth) serves as cause here because the impediment to vision is the impossibility of seeing what has no external reality. The passage ends with a truncated sentence about 'deficiency' and then the statement that al-Maturidi's reliance on transmitted texts has been mentioned.

The Vision of Allah: Consensus and the Third Opinion
Refutes al-Razi's consensus argument for vision with logical categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's consensus argument for the occurrence of vision and the logical refutation by al-Amidi.

Core Idea
Not every new opinion breaks consensus—only those that contradict both sides.

This passage presents a key theological debate on whether believers will see Allah on the Day of Resurrection. Imam al-Razi uses a consensus argument to support his view, but the critique by al-Amidi reveals a more nuanced understanding of what actually constitutes breaking consensus. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage from al-Razi's presentation to the refutation. Follow the translation closely.
Pay attention to the logical categories: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
2
Identify al-Razi's argument
Al-Razi claims the community has only two opinions. If he says vision is valid but does not occur, that would be a third opinion breaking consensus because validity and occurrence are inseparable.
3
Understand the refutation's core point
Al-Amidi explains that breaking consensus only occurs when you affirm what both sides negated or negate what both sides affirmed. Generating a partial opinion that agrees with each side in one part does not break consensus.
The passage uses examples from logic and fiqh to illustrate.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The example of killing a Muslim for a dhimmi and a free person for a slave shows that a detailed opinion (tafsil) does not break consensus. Think about how this applies to the vision debate.

(The Second Station: On the Occurrence of Vision: That the believers will see their Lord on the Day of Resurrection) i.e., in the hereafter. (Imam al-Razi said), using as evidence for the occurrence of vision: (The community on this matter is on two opinions) only: the first (it is valid and occurs), and the second (it does not occur and is not valid). And we have established that it is valid. So if we say, together with the statement of validity, that (it does not occur), that would be a third opinion breaking the consensus on the inseparability between validity and occurrence in negation and affirmation; rather both are affirmed together or negated together. (This) i.e., this argument (is not correct) as al-Amidi mentioned (because breaking consensus is affirming what they negated) as when some of those who reached consensus went to the universal negative and others to the particular negative, then one generates the statement of the universal affirmative; (or negating what they affirmed) as when some went to the universal affirmative and others to the particular affirmative, then one generates the statement of the universal negative. But if some went to the universal affirmative and others to the universal negative, then generating the statement of the particular affirmative and the particular negative together is not a breaking of consensus because there is no common ground between the two statements; rather it is a detail and an agreement with one group in one of the two issues and with the other in another, as in our case. And to this he alluded by his statement: (And this third opinion is nothing but the detail, which is the statement of permissibility and the statement of non-occurrence, and neither of them contradicts consensus) nor breaks it; (rather each one) of the two statements of the detail (is something said by a group) from the two groups of those who reached consensus, even if it breaks what was said by the other group. (And that) which we mentioned in this our issue (is like the issue of killing a Muslim for a dhimmi and a free person for a slave: for there are two speakers, one affirming both together like the Hanafis, and one negating both together like the Shafi'is; and the detail) between them is something not said by anyone of the community, but if it were said, (no
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents the second station on the occurrence of vision (ru'ya) and examines an argument by Imam al-Razi. Al-Razi claims that the community has only two opinions: either vision is valid and occurs, or it is invalid and does not occur. Since al-Razi has established validity, if he were to say it does not occur, that would be a third opinion breaking consensus because validity and occurrence are inseparable. The passage then refutes this by citing al-Amidi: breaking consensus means either affirming what they negated or negating what they affirmed. For example, if some hold a universal negative and others a particular negative, introducing a universal affirmative would break consensus. Similarly, if some hold a universal affirmative and others a particular affirmative, introducing a universal negative would break consensus. But if some hold a universal affirmative and others a universal negative, introducing a particular affirmative and a particular negative together does not break consensus because there is no common ground—it is a detail that agrees with each group in one part. This is exactly the situation: the third opinion (that vision is possible but does not occur) is a detail: the claim of permissibility agrees with those who affirm validity, and the claim of non-occurrence agrees with those who negate occurrence. Therefore, it does not break consensus. The passage uses an analogy: in the issue of killing a Muslim for a dhimmi and a free person for a slave, the Hanafis affirm both, the Shafi'is negate both, and a detailed opinion (affirming one and negating the other) has not been said by anyone; if it were said, it would not break consensus for the same reason. The text ends abruptly with 'لا' (no), likely the beginning of a conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any new opinion between two opposing views necessarily breaks consensus.
Correction: Only opinions that contradict both sides break consensus; a detailed opinion agreeing with each side in part does not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the third opinion (permissibility without occurrence) not break consensus?
A) Because it agrees with both previous opinions in part
B) Because it is a completely new opinion
C) Because consensus only applies to universal statements
D) Because the community never reached consensus on this issue

Key Takeaway
The third opinion (vision is possible but does not occur) does not break consensus because it is a detail that agrees with each side in part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a disagreement between two groups of Muslims, do you automatically dismiss any third view as a bid‘ah or 'breaking the consensus'? How can the principle of tafsīl help you examine whether it truly opposes the core of both positions before judging?
?
Think of a time you strongly held a position on a religious issue. Did you ever reject a nuanced middle opinion without fair consideration because it seemed 'new'? What would it cost you to sincerely revisit that issue with the logical precision shown here?
?
The lesson uses a logical tool—the square of opposition—to clarify a theological dispute. Are you willing to learn such tools to avoid unjustly accusing others of violating consensus? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to improve your understanding of valid scholarly disagreement.

First Response: The Validity of Being-Created Requires a Cause
First response on causality of validity of being-created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the validity of being-created, though relational, still requires a cause, and how its cause is the essence itself.

Core Idea
Even relational properties require a real basis and a cause.

The first response addresses the objection that the validity of being-created is purely relational and thus does not require a cause. The author argues that despite its relational nature, it is still a real property of the created being and must have a cause—namely, the essence itself, which is potentiality. This establishes that the refutation using the validity of tangibility is not automatically negated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Note the opening statement and the first response.
The passage begins by acknowledging the strength of the tangibility argument.
2
Identify the key concepts
Understand that 'validity of being-created' (sihhat al-makhlooqiyya) is described as relational (i'tibari) but still real. Note the distinction between created and impossible.
Think about why something relational can still be real.
3
Analyze the causal reasoning
The passage argues that this validity must have a cause, and that cause is the essence itself. Reflect on why potentiality (al-imkan) is identified with the essence.
The cause is not external; it is the very nature of the created being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بصحة الملموسية فقوي، وفيه بحث أما أولاً فلأن صحة المخلوقية وإن كانت اعتبارية بمعنى كونها معدومة في الخارج لكنها ثابتة للمخلوق في الواقع دون الممتنع فلا بد لثبوتها له واختصاصها به من علة فلا وجه لنفي اقتضائها للعلة فعلتها الإمكان بل نفس الذات لأنها نفس الإمكان فلا يعلل إلا بالذات. وأما ثانياً : فلان تعلق الرؤية بشيء بمعنى كونه مرئياً كما يقتضي كونه من الأمور العينية لا من الاعتبارية المحضة كذلك تعلق الخلق بشيء بمعنى كونه مخلوقا يقتضي كونه مما له تحقق في الأعيان فإن الأمور الاعتبارية المحضة لا تكون مخلوقة، وبالجملة الفرق بين صحة المخلوقية وصحة الملموسية في قوة النقض بأحدهما دون الآخر مما لا وجه له . وأما ثالثاً : فلان الحدوث متأخر عن صحة المخلوقية لأنه وصف الوجود المتأخر عنها فكيف يكون علة لها وأما رابعاً فلان اشتراك الملموسية بين الجوهر والعرض محل تأمل فإن الأعراض لا يمس بها إلا تبعاً لمحالها ويمكن أن يقال : النقض بالملموسية المطلقة سواء كان بالذات أو بالتبع، فتأمل.
By the validity of tangibility, it is strong, but there is discussion in it. As for firstly: because the validity of being-created, even if it is relational in the sense of being non-existent externally, is still established for the created in reality as opposed to the impossible, so there must be a cause for its establishment and specification; so there is no justification for negating its requiring a cause. Its cause is potentiality (al-imkan), but rather the essence itself because it is the very potentiality, so it is only caused by the essence. As for secondly: because vision's attachment to something, in the sense of its being visible, just as it requires it to be among real things, not purely relational, similarly, creation's attachment to something, in the sense of its being created, requires it to be something that has existence in external entities; for purely relational matters are not created. In summary, the distinction between the validity of being-created and the validity of tangibility in the strength of refutation by one without the other has no basis. As for thirdly: because temporality (al-huduth) is posterior to the validity of being-created, since it is a description of existence which is posterior to it, so how can it be its cause? As for fourthly: because the sharing of tangibility between substance and accident is a matter for contemplation; for accidents are not touched except as a consequence of their loci. And it can be said: the refutation by absolute tangibility, whether essential or consequential, so reflect.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage discusses a refutation of the earlier proof for vision using the validity of being-created (sihhat al-makhlooqiyya). It begins by stating that the validity of tangibility (sihhat al-malmoosiyya) is strong but subject to discussion. Four points are raised. First: although the validity of being-created is relational (i'tibari) in being non-existent externally, it is still a real property of the created being as opposed to the impossible, so it requires a cause. The cause is not non-causality; it is either potentiality (al-imkan) or the essence itself, since the essence is potentiality. Second: just as vision's attachment requires the object to be real and not purely relational, so creation's attachment requires the object to have external existence; purely relational matters cannot be created. Therefore, distinguishing between the validity of being-created and the validity of tangibility in terms of the strength of refutation is unfounded. Third: temporality (huduth) is posterior to the validity of being-created because it is a description of existence, which is posterior to validity; thus temporality cannot be its cause. Fourth: the claim that tangibility is shared between substance and accident is questionable because accidents are only touched derivatively through their loci. The passage concludes by suggesting that a refutation by absolute tangibility, whether essential or consequential, is possible, and invites reflection.

Key Terms
صحة المخلوقية
أمر اعتباري
الإمكان
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the validity of being-created is purely conceptual with no real basis and thus does not require a cause.
Correction: The passage explains that even though it is relational, it is a real property that distinguishes created from impossible, so it must have a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the first response, what is the cause of the validity of being-created?
A) The essence itself
B) Non-existence
C) Temporality
D) Accidents

Key Takeaway
The validity of being-created, though relational, has a real basis and requires a cause, which is the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even the abstract 'validity of your being created' requires a cause, where does that leave your own existence? Can you honestly claim that your life is self-sufficient, or does every moment depend on a cause beyond yourself?
?
The passage says the validity of being-created is 'established for the created in reality.' When you consider your own existence, do you truly live as though every aspect of your being is contingent and caused by God? Or do you sometimes act as if your own plans and efforts are the ultimate cause?
?
The cause of your very possibility is the essence of the Necessary Being. How does this truth change the way you approach your own limitations and failures? Will you let this lesson humble you into recognizing that your existence is held by God at every moment?

Second Response: Vision and Creation Both Require Real Objects
Second response: parallel between vision and creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the parallel between vision and creation: both require their objects to be real, not merely relational.

Core Idea
Creation necessitates real existence as much as vision does.

The second response draws an analogy between vision and creation. Just as vision's attachment to something requires that the object be a real, external thing (not purely conceptual), similarly, creation's attachment to something requires that the object have existence in external reality. Therefore, the distinction between the validity of being-created and the validity of tangibility is unfounded.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second response. Pay attention to the analogy.
The passage uses the phrase 'as for secondly' to introduce this point.
2
Identify the analogy
The author compares vision (ru'ya) and creation (khalq) in terms of what they require of their objects. Both require the object to be a real, external entity.
Think about why vision requires realness: you cannot see a purely conceptual thing.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that distinguishing between the validity of being-created and the validity of tangibility for refutation has no basis. Reflect on why the two are parallel.
The key is that creation cannot be attributed to purely relational matters.

Third Response: Temporality is Posterior to Validity of Being-Created
Third response: huduth is posterior to validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporality (huduth) cannot be the cause of the validity of being-created because it is posterior to it.

Core Idea
Temporality comes after validity; cause cannot be after effect.

The third response addresses a potential confusion: that temporality (huduth) might be the cause of the validity of being-created. The author argues that temporality is a description of existence, which itself is posterior to the validity of being-created. Therefore, temporality cannot be the cause, as it comes after.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that it is a concise argument.
The passage begins with 'as for thirdly'.
2
Understand the temporal order
The author states that temporality (huduth) is a description of existence, and existence is posterior to the validity of being-created. Therefore, temporality is posterior to that validity.
Think about the order: first the thing is possible (valid), then it exists, then it is described as temporal.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since a cause must be prior to or simultaneous with its effect, temporality cannot cause the validity of being-created. Reflect on the rhetorical question in the text.
The passage ends with 'how can it be its cause?' to emphasize impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Fourth Response: Tangibility of Accidents is Derivative
Fourth response: tangibility of accidents is derivative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the challenge to the claim that tangibility is shared between substance and accident, and the proposal of absolute tangibility.

Core Idea
Accidents are not directly tangible; tangibility is derivative.

The fourth response questions the assumption that tangibility applies equally to substances and accidents. The author points out that accidents are only touched derivatively through their loci. He then suggests that a refutation by absolute tangibility, whether essential or consequential, might still be possible, inviting further reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth response
Read the Arabic and translation. This is the final point in the passage.
The passage begins with 'as for fourthly'.
2
Analyze the critique
The author states that the claim of shared tangibility between substance and accident is questionable because accidents are only touched derivatively (taba'an) through their loci.
Consider what it means for an accident to be touched 'through its locus'—e.g., the whiteness of a wall is touched indirectly through the wall.
3
Reflect on the proposed solution
The author then says a refutation by absolute tangibility (whether essential or consequential) could be made. The phrase 'so reflect' suggests this is open for thought.
Absolute tangibility covers both essential (substance) and consequential (accident) touch.

Tangibility of accidents is derivative; absolute tangibility may still be used for refutation.

The Third Response: Temporal Scope of Negation
Commentator's third response distinguishing temporal scope of negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator restricts the negation in the verse to this world, using the distinction between absolute and permanent negation.

Core Idea
The verse negates sight only in this world, not forever.

The Mu'tazilites argued that the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' absolutely denies the possibility of seeing God. The commentator offers a precise linguistic analysis: the negation is general in persons but not in time. This lesson unpacks the third response, which shows that the verse only negates vision in the temporal scope of this world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the third response
Read the Arabic sentence describing the third response. Notice the terms 'saliba mutlaqa' and 'saliba da'ima'.
2
Understand the distinction
The commentator says the negation is absolute (saliba mutlaqa) but not permanent (da'ima). In Arabic logic, an absolute negation denies the predicate at some unspecified time, while a permanent negation denies it at all times. So the verse only negates vision in the present context (this world).
This is a technical distinction from logic.
3
Connect to the Ahl al-Sunna position
The commentator says 'we say it is in accordance with it where He is not seen in this world.' This shows that the verse supports the Sunni view that vision is possible in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث ) من تلك الوجوه ( أنها ) أي الآية ( وإن عمت في الأشخاص ) باستغراق اللام ) فإنها لا تعم في الأزمان ) فإنها سالبة مطلقة لا دائمة ) ونحن نقول : بموجبه حيث لا يرى في الدنيا . ( الرابع : ( منها ( أن الآية تدل على أن الأبصار لا تراه ولا يلزم . منه أن المبصرين لا يرونه لجواز أن يكون ذلك) النفي المذكور في الآية ( نفياً للرؤية بالجارحة مواجهة وانطباعاً كما هو العادة فلا يلزم نفي الرؤية بالجارحة مطلقاً . ( وأما عن الوجه الثاني : ) أي وأما الجواب عن الوجه الثاني من وجهي الاستدلال بالآية ( وهو قوله : تمدح ) الباري ( بأنه لا يرى فنقول : هذا مدعاكم فأين الدليل عليه ( وإذا ثبت أن سياق الكلام يقتضي أنه تمدح لم يكن لكم فيه دليل على امتناع رؤيته ( بل لنافيه الحجة على صحة الرؤية لأنه لو امتنعت رؤيته لما حصل المدح ) بنفيها عنه ( إذ لا مدح للمعدوم بأنه لا يرى حيث لم يكن له ذلك وإنما المدح فيه ) أي في عدم الرؤية ( للممتنع المتعزز بحجاب الكبرياء كما في الشاهد .
The third of those aspects: that the verse, although general with respect to persons due to the inclusive lam, it is not general with respect to times, for it is an absolute negative, not a permanent one. And we say: it is in accordance with it where He is not seen in this world. The fourth: that the verse indicates that sights do not perceive Him, and it does not necessitate that beholders do not see Him, because it is possible that the negation mentioned in the verse is a negation of vision with the organ face-to-face and impression as is customary, so the absolute negation of vision with the organ is not necessary. As for the second aspect: i.e., the answer to the second aspect of the two aspects of inference from the verse, which is his statement: the Creator praises Himself by not being seen, we say: this is your claim, so where is the proof of it? And if it is established that the context of speech implies that it is praise, you have no proof in it for the impossibility of seeing Him. Rather, for the denier of vision, there is evidence for the validity of vision, because if vision were impossible, praise would not occur by negating it from Him, since there is no praise for a non-existent that it is not seen, as it does not have that. Rather, praise lies in negation for the impossible one who is honoured by the veil of majesty, as in the witnessed case.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents three counter-arguments against the Mu'tazilite interpretation of the Qur'anic verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' (Sight does not perceive Him). The commentator addresses the claim that this verse absolutely denies the possibility of seeing God. First, he argues that the negation is not general in time: it is an absolute negative, not a permanent one, so it only negates vision in this world, leaving open the possibility in the afterlife. Second, he argues that the negation may be of a specific type of vision—vision with the bodily organ in a face-to-face manner with impression—and does not negate all forms of vision. Third, he responds to the argument that the verse is praise of God by negating vision, which would imply vision is impossible. The commentator states that even if the context indicates praise, it does not prove impossibility; rather, it proves possibility because praising God for not being seen only makes sense if He could be seen but chooses to veil Himself. Thus, the verse actually supports the possibility of vision.

السالبة المطلقة
السالبة الدائمة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves God cannot be seen at all.
Correction: The verse only negates vision in this world, not in the afterlife.

B) General in persons but not in times
C) Specific to a type of vision
D) Permanent negation

Key Takeaway
The verse only negates vision in this world, leaving open the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When you struggle to trust God's promises for the future, is it because you are treating an 'absolute' statement in this life as if it were 'permanent'? Commit to holding onto what He has promised beyond this world.

The Fourth Response: Type of Vision Negated
Commentator's fourth response restricting negation to physical vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator restricts the negation to a specific mode of vision—vision with the bodily organ in a face-to-face manner—rather than all vision.

Core Idea
The verse negates only a particular type of sight, not all sight.

The Mu'tazilites took the negation in the verse as absolute, but the commentator argues that it may be restricted to a specific kind of vision—the ordinary human experience of seeing with the eye, involving facing the object and receiving an impression. This lesson examines the fourth response, which shows that the verse does not preclude a metaphysical vision of God beyond our physical experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the fourth response
Read the Arabic for the fourth response. Note the phrase 'nafyan lil-ru’ya bi al-jariha muwajahatan wa’n-tiba‘an'.
2
Understand the restriction
The commentator says the negation is of vision with the bodily organ (bil-jariha) in a face-to-face manner (muwajahatan) and with impression (an-tiba‘an). This is the ordinary way we see physical objects. So the verse only negates that kind of vision for God.
This implies that a non-physical vision, like the beatific vision in the afterlife, is not negated.
3
Connect to Sunni doctrine
This response aligns with the Sunni belief that God will be seen in the afterlife without the physical limitations of bodily eyes, perhaps with a vision that is beyond our comprehension.

The Praise Argument Rebutted: Negation Does Not Entail Impossibility
Commentator's rebuttal to the Mu'tazilite praise argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that even if the verse is praise, it does not prove the impossibility of seeing God, but rather supports its possibility.

Core Idea
Praise for not being seen only makes sense if being seen is possible.

The Mu'tazilites argued that the verse is praise of God for not being seen, which would be meaningless if vision were possible. The commentator turns this argument around: praise is only meaningful when applied to one who could be seen but chooses to veil Himself. This lesson unpacks the rebuttal to the second aspect of the Mu'tazilite inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the response to the second aspect
Read the Arabic passage addressing the second aspect. Pay attention to the phrase 'la madha li-ma‘dum' and 'al-mumtani‘ al-muta‘azziz'.
2
Understand the logic of praise
The commentator argues that praising God for not being seen requires that vision is possible; otherwise, it would be like praising a non-existent thing for not being seen, which is meaningless. Praise is meaningful only for an entity that could be seen but is prevented by its majesty.
This is a key theological argument from common sense.
3
Apply to the verse
Thus, the verse's praise (if it is praise) actually supports the Sunni claim that God is capable of being seen, but He veils Himself out of majesty.

Refuting the Mu'tazili Interpretation of Khatm and Tab'
Refutes Mu'tazili view that God's sealing prevents belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mere description of God's sealing hearts does not necessitate the impossibility of belief, and how this refutes the Mu'tazili position.

Core Idea
A descriptive seal does not cause unbelief; the Qur'anic verses indicate a consequence, not a direct prevention.

The Mu'tazili claim that God cannot prevent belief because that would be ugly. They interpret the Qur'anic verses on khatm and tab' as metaphorical or as withholding favors. In this passage, our author argues that the mere description of sealing does not actually prevent belief, and therefore the Mu'tazili interpretation fails to explain the verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the author's argument.
Pay attention to the Qur'anic quotation and the structure of the refutation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili principle
The passage begins by stating the Mu'tazili principle that preventing belief and creating misguidance is ugly, so it cannot be attributed to God. Understand this as the basis for their reinterpretation.
This principle drives their entire approach to these verses.
3
Follow the author's argument
The author quotes Qur'an 2:6-7 to show that God mentions sealing in the context of explaining why they do not believe. Then he argues that the Mu'tazili's proposed explanations (like branding or withholding favors) are not sufficient to be the cause of this impossibility.
The key phrase is 'mere description of sealing... does not prevent belief.'
4
Consider the concluding objection
The author notes a potential objection: perhaps the sealing is an obstructing attribute in the hearts. Reflect on why this might not salvage the Mu'tazili position.
The author signals this as something to ponder ('تأمل').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثالث : أمور صرح بها القرآن
لأن الفعل بلا إخلاص كلا فعل وهو أي ما ذكروه من التأويل ( مع الابتناء على أصلهم أن منع الإيمان وخلق الضلال قبيح فلا يجوز إسناده إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى سيأتيك بيان فساده يبطله ذكر الله تعالى هذه الأشياء في معرض امتناع الإيمان منهم لأجل ذلك حيث قال : سواء عليهم أأنذرتهم أم لم تنذرهم يؤمنون ختم الله على قلوبهم ﴾ [البقرة : ٧٦]، أي لا يؤمنون لأجل الختم وذلك لأن قوله : ختم استئناف لبيان السبب ( وشيء مما ذكرتم لا يصلح لذلك ) أي لكونه سبباً لامتناع الإيمان فإن مجرد الوصف بالختم والطبع وجعل الأكنة والأقفال على قلوبهم لا يمنع من الإيمان وكذا الوسم الإيمان وكذا الوسم بعلامة مميزة، ومنع اللطف والإخلاص لا يقتضي امتناع الإيمان فلا يصح الحمل عليها .
قوله : (لا يقتضي امتناع (الإيمان وإلا كان قبيحاً لا يسند إليه تعالى عندهم وقد يناقش في ذلك بأن ما ذكر يصلح سبباً للامتناع باعتبار أن مبدأ الوسم والمنع صفة في قلوبهم مانعة فتأمل.
The sixth observation - the third aim: Matters explicitly mentioned in the Qur'an. Because the act without sincerity is like no act, and it is what they mentioned of interpretation (based on their principle that preventing belief and creating misguidance is ugly, so it is not permissible to attribute it to God, Exalted and High. The proof of its corruption will come to you. He refutes it by mentioning that God, Exalted, mentioned these things in the context of the impossibility of belief from them on account of that, where He said: 'It is the same for them whether you warn them or do not warn them: they will not believe. God has sealed their hearts.' (Qur'an 2:6-7) That is, they do not believe because of the sealing. And that is because His saying 'sealed' is an independent statement explaining the reason. (And something of what you mentioned is not suitable for that) i.e., for being a cause of the impossibility of belief. For mere description of sealing and stamping and placing coverings and locks on their hearts does not prevent belief, and likewise branding with a distinguishing mark, and the withholding of subtle favors and sincerity does not necessitate impossibility of belief, so it is not correct to attribute (the impossibility) to them. His saying: '(does not necessitate impossibility) of belief, and were it otherwise it would be ugly and not attributed to Him, Exalted, according to them. And one may object to that by saying that what is mentioned is suitable as a cause for impossibility considering that the origin of branding and prevention is an obstructing attribute in their hearts. So consider.'
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazili interpretation of Qur'anic verses about God sealing hearts. The author begins by stating the Mu'tazili principle that preventing belief is ugly and cannot be attributed to God. Then he counters by quoting the Qur'an (2:6-7) where God says He sealed their hearts and they do not believe. The Mu'tazili interpret this as God directly preventing belief, but the author argues that mere description of sealing does not actually prevent belief; rather, the sealing is a consequence or sign. The phrase 'something of what you mentioned is not suitable for that' means that the Mu'tazili's cited reason does not validly explain why they do not believe. The author then adds a marginal comment noting that one could object that the sealing itself might be an obstructing attribute in the hearts, but the main point stands. The passage guards against the misunderstanding that God directly and unjustly prevents belief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili think that their metaphorical interpretations of khatm (e.g., as a brand or withholding favors) adequately explain why the disbelievers do not believe, because they see any direct prevention of belief as ugly and thus not attributable to God.
Correction: The author argues that these interpretations do not provide a real cause for the impossibility of belief. The Qur'anic context presents the sealing as the reason for their disbelief, and mere description or metaphor does not make that link. Therefore, the Mu'tazili interpretation fails to properly explain the verses.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the Mu'tazili interpretation of khatm (sealing)?
A) The Qur'an does not mention sealing at all.
B) Mere description of sealing does not prevent belief.
C) God cannot seal hearts because it is ugly.
D) The Mu'tazili agree with the Ash'ari interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic description of sealing hearts does not itself prevent belief; the Mu'tazili interpretation fails to account for the causal link implied by the verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Qur'an 2:6-7, do you truly feel your own faith is a gift from God, or do you secretly pride yourself on your belief? How does the verse's assertion that God seals hearts challenge your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
The Mu'tazili were troubled by the idea of God preventing belief—are there any aspects of God's actions that trouble you? What does your discomfort reveal about your expectations of God, and will you submit to His decree even when it seems harsh?
?
If God can seal hearts, then your own faith is entirely by His grace. Do you live with the urgent awareness that you could be sealed at any moment, or do you take your belief for granted? What will you do today to seek His mercy before it is too late?

The Definition of Ajal and the Ash'ari View on Death's Timing
Defines ajal and defends the Ash'ari view that every death occurs at its preordained term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that ajal is the divinely decreed time of death, and that even a murdered person dies exactly at their appointed term.

Core Idea
Every death occurs at its preordained moment, not a second earlier or later.

The concept of ajal (term of life) is central to Islamic theology, especially the Ash'ari understanding of divine decree. This lesson defines ajal and establishes that no act, including murder, can alter God's decree. The author begins with a clear definition and supports it with the Quranic verse stating that nations cannot advance or delay their term.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of ajal
Read the Arabic definition: 'al-ajal huwa al-zaman alladhi 'alima Allah annahu yamutu fihi'. Notice it is defined in relation to God's knowledge.
This definition ties ajal directly to God's omniscience.
2
Understand the ruling on al-maqtul
The passage states that the killed person dies by his term. Consider why this matters: it denies that murder shortens life.
This is a distinct Ash'ari position against the Mu'tazila.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Read Quran 15:5 quoted here: 'No nation can advance its term nor delay it.' See how it supports the fixity of the term.
The verse is used to argue that the term cannot be changed.
4
Reflect on the grammatical note
The author clarifies the conjunctive structure of the verse to resolve a potential absurdity. This is a subtle scholarly move.
This shows how careful exegesis supports theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثالث: ) من الأمور ( الأجل وهو ) في الحيوان (الزمان الذي علم الله أنه يموت فيه فالمقتول عند أهل الحق ميت بأجله ) الذي قدره الله له وعلم أنه يموت فيه، ( وموته بفعله تعالى ) ولا يتصور تغير هذه المقدر بتقديم ولا تأخير قال تعالى ما تسبق من أمة أجلها وما يستأخرون فإذا جاء أجلهم لا يستأخرون ساعة ولا يستقدمون ﴾ [الحجر: ٥]، قوله : ( ولا يستقدمون) معطوف على مجموع الشرط والجزاء لا على الجزاء وحده، إذ لا يتصور استقدام شيء عند مجيئه فلا وجه لتقييد نفيه بالشرط، هذا هو المشهور وقد ذكرنا في حواشي المطول أنه يجوز عطفه على الجزاء أيضاً بناء على أن يكون معنى قوله تعالى : ولا يستأخرون ساعة ولا يستقدمون ﴾ [ يونس : ٤٩] ، لا يستطيعون تغييره على نمط قوله تعالى : ولا رطب ولا يابس إلا في كتاب مبين ﴾ [الأنعام: ٥٩] ، ومن هذا الباب قولهم : كلمته فارد على سوداء ولا بيضاء وقد يجاب عن الاستدلال بالآيتين لجواز أن يراد بالأجل فيهما الأجل الثابت فلا يعارض قسمة الأجل إلى الثابت والمعلق وأنت خبير بأنه تخصيص بلا دليل وضرورة يسمع فإن قلت : قوله تعالى : ثم قضى أجلاً وأجل مسمى ﴾ [الأنعام : ٢ ]، عنده يدل على تعدد الأجل لأن النكرة إذا أعيدت نكرة كانت الثانية غير الأولى قلت: ممنوع لقوله تعالى : وهو الذي في السماء إله وفي الأرض إله ﴾ [الزخرف : ٨٤] ، وقوله تعالى : وقالوا لولا أنزل عليه آية من ربه قل إن الله قادر على أن ينزل آية ﴾ [الأنعام : ۳۷] ، ومثله أكثر من أن يحصى ولو سلم فلعل المراد بهما أجل الدنيا وأجل الآخرة .
(The third) of the matters (is al-ajal, and it is) in animals (the time which God knows that it will die. So the killed person, according to the people of truth, dies by his term) which God decreed for him and knows that he dies in it, (and his death is by His act), and it is inconceivable that this decreed measure be changed by advancement or delay. He said: 'No nation can advance its term nor delay it. When their term comes, they cannot delay it by an hour nor advance it' (Qur'an 15:5). His saying: (nor advance it) is conjoined to the whole condition and result, not to the result alone, since it is inconceivable to advance something when it comes, so there is no point in restricting its negation by the condition. This is the well-known view, and we have mentioned in the glosses on al-Mutawwal that it is also permissible to conjoin it to the result alone, based on the meaning of His saying 'they will not delay it by an hour nor advance it' (Qur'an 10:49) – they cannot change it, in the manner of His saying 'neither moist nor dry but in a clear book' (Qur'an 6:59). And of this kind is their saying: 'I spoke to him and he replied neither black nor white.' And one may answer the argument from the two verses by saying that it is possible that the term intended in both is the fixed term, so it does not contradict the division of the term into fixed and contingent. And you are aware that this is a specification without evidence and a necessity that is listened to. For if you say: His saying 'Then He decreed a term and a named term' (Qur'an 6:2) indicates a multiplicity of terms, because if an indefinite noun is repeated indefinitely, the second is other than the first. I say: It is denied, by His saying 'It is He who is God in heaven and God on earth' (Qur'an 43:84), and His saying 'And they said: Why is not a sign sent down to him from his Lord? Say: Indeed God is able to send down a sign' (Qur'an 6:37) – and the like of this is more than can be counted. And even if it is granted, perhaps what is intended by them is the term of this world and the term of the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines ajal (term of life) and defends the Ash'ari position that every death, including murder, occurs exactly at the time God decreed. The author defines ajal as the time known to God for death, and states that the killed person dies at his appointed term, and death is by God's act. He quotes Qur'an 15:5 to show that terms cannot be changed. Then a grammatical discussion follows: the word 'nor advance it' in the verse is normally conjoined to the whole clause, but another view allows it to be conjoined to the result only. An objection is raised about the division of term into fixed and contingent; the author answers that the verses cited might refer only to the fixed term, so they do not negate the contingent term. Further, the objection from Qur'an 6:2 about two terms is refuted by showing that Arabic allows repeated indefinite nouns to refer to the same thing, or by interpreting them as the term of this world and the Hereafter. The passage guards against the idea that murder can cut short a person's life.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A murderer ends the victim's life before its decreed term, thus changing the term.
Correction: The passage affirms that the killed person dies exactly at his appointed term; the death is by God's act, and the decreed term is fixed and cannot be advanced or delayed.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the status of the killed person's term of life?
A) The killer cuts the term short.
B) The killed person dies at his appointed term exactly.
C) The killed person had a longer term that was cut short.
D) The term is unknown and not fixed.

Key Takeaway
Ajal is the unchangeable time God knows for death; even murder occurs at that exact moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that your death is fixed at a moment God knows, how does that affect the way you live today? What would change if you woke up tomorrow knowing that your ajal is exactly one month away?
?
The passage says even a murdered person dies at his term. Do you live with the fear that someone else could cut your life short, or do you trust that your ajal is secure in God's hand? What practical steps can you take to strengthen that trust?
?
Consider your habits: do you delay good deeds with the assumption that you have more time? If you knew the exact hour of your death, would you be satisfied with how you are living right now?

Addressing Objections: Fixed vs Contingent Terms and Quranic Evidence
Responds to objections regarding the division of ajal into fixed and contingent and defends the Ash'ari stance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari scholars respond to objections about the division of ajal into fixed and contingent, and how Quranic verses are interpreted to support the unity of ajal.

Core Idea
The division of ajal into fixed and contingent lacks textual evidence and is refuted by Quranic consistency.

After establishing the basic definition, the author anticipates objections. Some scholars divided ajal into a fixed term and a contingent term that could change. This lesson presents the objection and the Ash'ari response, including a detailed refutation of the argument from Quran 6:2. The author uses linguistic and contextual arguments to defend the view that all deaths occur at one fixed term.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about fixed and contingent terms
The objection claims that the verses about the term not being advanced refer only to the fixed term, not the contingent one.
This would allow a term to change in certain circumstances.
2
Examine the author's counterargument
The author calls this a 'specification without evidence' and a 'necessity that is listened to'—meaning it is baseless.
The author rejects the division as unwarranted.
3
Understand the argument from Quran 6:2
The opponent claims that 'two terms' in the verse imply multiple terms. The author refutes using Arabic linguistic examples.
Examples show that repeated indefinite nouns can refer to the same entity.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
Even if the two terms are distinct, they could refer to this world and the Hereafter.
This preserves the unity of ajal for each life.

The Consensus That Allah Does Not Do Evil and the Dialectical Exchange
The consensus on Allah's avoidance of evil and the Ash'ari-Mu'tazila difference with dialectical exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the composite consensus that Allah does not do evil, the difference between Ash'ari and Mu'tazila approaches, and the dialectical response to objections.

Core Idea
Allah does no evil; the debate is over whether that is by definition or by choice.

This lesson examines the sixth objective: Allah's transcendence from evil. Scholars agree on the conclusion but differ on its rational foundation. The Ash'aris argue that evil cannot be ascribed to Allah at all, while the Mu'tazila argue that He refrains from evil. We will also see how objections regarding Allah creating evil actions are answered.

Guided Path
1
Understand the composite consensus
Read the opening statement about the ijmā' murakkab and how it establishes that Allah does no evil and omits no obligation. Note that the conclusion is unanimous.
The consensus is 'composite' because it has a shared conclusion but different underlying rationales.
2
Distinguish the two positions
Identify the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions. The Ash'aris say 'no evil from Him' by definition; the Mu'tazila say He avoids evil and does what is obligatory. This difference stems from the foundational principle of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih.
The contrast shows how the same conclusion can be reached via different epistemological paths.
3
Examine the first objection and response
Read the objection that Allah creates evil actions like unbelief and injustice, and the response that creating evil is not itself evil—He is the originator (mūjid) but not the doer (fā'il).
This distinction is crucial for Ash'ari theodicy.
4
Examine the second objection and response
Study the second objection: if Allah does no evil, then He also does no good because there is no command or prohibition upon Him. The response uses revelation's praise of His actions to establish goodness, or the absence of blame as a criterion.
This shows how Ash'aris rely on revealed criteria for moral evaluation of divine acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد السادس : إن الله لا يفعل القبيح اعلم أن الأمة قد أجمعت إجماعاً مركباً على أن الله لا يفعل القبيح ولا يترك واجباً عليه فلا يتصور فعل قبيح من الله ولا ترك واجب، فالأشاعرة من جهة أنه لا قبيح منه؟ ولا ترك واجب ( وأما المعتزلة فمن جهة أن ما هو قبيح منه يتركه وما يجب يفعله (وهذا الخلاف في مبنى الحكم المتفق عليه ) فرع المسألة المتقدم أعني قاعدة التحسين والتقبيح ( إذ لا حاكم بقبح القبيح منه ووجوب الواجب عليه قوله : ( على أن الله تعالى لا يفعل القبيح فإن قيل : الكفر والظلم والمعاصي كلها قبائح وقد خلقها الله تعالى . قلت : نعم إلا أن خلق القبيح ليس بقبيح فهو موجد للقبيح لا فاعل له، فإن قيل : فلا يفعل الحسن أيضاً، لأنه لا حكم عليه أمراً كما لا حكم عليه نهياً والإجماع على خلافه . قلنا : قد ورد الشرع بالثناء في أفعاله فكانت حسنة لكونها متعلقة للمدح والثناء عند الله تعالى وأما إذا اكتفى في الحسن بعدم استحقاق الذم في حكم الله تعالى فالأمر أظهر .
The Sixth Observatory - The Sixth Objective: That Allah Does Not Do Evil. Know that the community has agreed—by a composite consensus—that Allah does not do evil and does not omit what is obligatory upon Him; thus it is inconceivable that Allah should do evil or omit an obligation. The Ash'aris hold that there is no evil from Him and no omission of obligation; whereas the Mu'tazila hold that what is evil from Him He omits, and what is obligatory He does. And this disagreement concerns the foundation of the agreed-upon ruling, and it is a branch of the preceding issue, namely the principle of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih, since there is no judge of the evil of what is evil from Him or the obligation of what is obligatory upon Him. His saying: (That Allah does not do evil. If it is said: unbelief, injustice, and sins are all evils, and Allah has created them. I say: Yes, but creating evil is not evil; He is the originator of evil, not the doer of it. If it is said: Then He does not do good either, because there is no command upon Him just as there is no prohibition upon Him, and consensus is contrary to that. We say: Revelation has come with praise for His actions, so they are good because they are associated with praise and commendation from Allah; but if one is satisfied in good by the absence of deserving blame according to Allah's ruling, then the matter is even clearer.)
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objective: Allah does not do evil. The author states that there is a composite consensus (ijmā' murakkab) that Allah neither does evil nor omits an obligation. The Ash'aris base this on the fact that evil is not even definable from Allah, while the Mu'tazila base it on the principle that Allah avoids evil and performs the obligatory. The disagreement is rooted in the earlier issue of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih (the rational basis of good and evil). Then a dialectical exchange is given: Objection: Allah creates evil actions like unbelief and injustice; therefore He does evil. Answer: Creating evil is not itself evil; He is the originator (mūjid) of evil, not the doer (fā'il) of it. Second objection: Then He does not do good either, since there is no command or prohibition upon Him. Reply: Revelation praises His actions, making them good; alternatively, if we define good as the absence of blame, then it is even clearer that His actions are good.

Key Terms
لا يفعل القبيح
الإجماع المركب
الأشاعرة
المعتزلة
خلق القبيح
التحسين والتقبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari position denies any moral evaluation of God's actions, leaving them arbitrary.
Correction: The text shows that Ash'aris agree with Mu'tazila on the conclusion that Allah does no evil, but they differ on the rational basis. Ash'aris do not deny that Allah's actions are good; they ground the goodness in revelation rather than unaided reason.

Checkpoint
In the dialectical exchange, how do the Ash'aris respond to the claim that Allah creates evil actions?
A) They deny that Allah creates evil.
B) They say creating evil is not evil; He is the originator, not the doer.
C) They distinguish between moral and physical evil.
D) They admit evil but claim it is just.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position that creating evil is not evil because He is the originator, not the doer, preserves Allah's transcendence while acknowledging His creation of all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness evil in the world—injustice, suffering, sin—do you secretly harbor resentment toward Allah, as if He is the 'doer' of that evil? How can the distinction between originator and doer transform your trust in Him right now?
?
The Ash'aris say Allah's actions are good because revelation praises them, not because they fit your moral checklist. Where in your life are you tempted to judge Allah's decrees by your own sense of fairness? Will you submit to His praise as the sole measure of goodness, even when it hurts?
?
If creating evil is not evil, then every trial and calamity in your life is from Him yet not a wrong done to you. Can you honestly say you believe that about the hardest thing you are facing right now? What would it cost you to truly embrace that?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
5 min
Futility and the Purpose of God's Actions
Defines futility and distinguishes purpose from end in God's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while God's actions are full of benefits, these benefits are not motivating purposes but rather ends consequent upon His actions, and why this distinction preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God's actions are not motivated by purposes—they are self-sufficient and wise.

This passage addresses a common objection: if God's actions are full of benefits, does that mean He acts for purposes like humans? The answer lies in distinguishing between a purpose (which motivates) and an end (which is a result). God's actions are not futile, but their benefits are effects, not motives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice the careful wording used to distinguish between different types of causes.
Pay special attention to terms like 'فائدة', 'غرض', and 'غاية'.
2
Identify the definition of futility
Find how the author defines 'futility' (العبث) and contrast it with the description of God's actions as 'firm and precise' (محكمة متقنة).
This sets up the problem: how can God's actions be beneficial but not purposeful in the human sense?
3
Understand the key distinction
The author separates benefits into those that are motivating causes (أسباب باعثة) and those that are mere ends or effects (غايات ومنافع). Ask yourself: why can't benefits be motives for God?
The key is God's self-sufficiency (الاستغناء الذاتي).
4
Interpret apparent verses
The passage acknowledges that some Quranic verses seem to attribute causes to God's actions. Note how the author reinterprets these as indicating ends, not purposes.
This shields divine transcendence from anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال في الجواب : إن العبث ما كان خالياً عن الفوائد والمنافع وأفعاله تعالى محكمة متقنة مشتملة على حكم ومصالح لا تحصى راجعة إلى مخلوقاته تعالى، لكنها ليست أسباباً باعثة على إقدامه وعللاً مقتضية لفاعليته فلا تكون أغراضاً له ولا عللاً غائية لأفعاله حتى يلزم استكماله بها، بل تكون غايات ومنافع لأفعاله وآثاراً مترتبة عليها فلا يلزم أن يكون شيء من أفعاله عبثاً خالياً عن الفوائد، وما ورد من الظواهر الدالة على تعليل أفعاله تعالى فهو محمول على الغاية والمنفعة دون الغرض والعلة الغائية . قوله : ( لكنها ليست أسباباً باعثة إلخ) لأن الباعث ما يكون مقصوداً بالقصد الأولي ويكون القصد إلى الفعل لأجل تحصيله وأفعال الله تعالى ليست كذلك بل كلها مقصود بالقصد الأول لاستغنائه الذاتي .
It may be said in response: Futility is that which is devoid of benefits and advantages, and His actions, exalted, are firm and precise, comprising wisdoms and interests that cannot be counted, returning to His creatures, exalted. But they are not motivating causes for His undertaking nor efficient causes necessitating His agency, so they are not purposes for Him nor final causes for His actions such that He would be completed by them. Rather, they are ends and benefits for His actions and effects consequent upon them. So it does not necessitate that any of His actions be futile, devoid of benefits. And what has come of apparent verses indicating a cause for His actions, exalted, is interpreted as [indicating] the end and benefit, not the purpose and final cause. His statement: 'but they are not motivating causes etc.' because the motive is what is intended with the primary intention, and the intention toward the action is for its attainment, and the actions of God, exalted, are not like that; rather, all of them are intended with the primary intention due to His essential self-sufficiency.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a definition and distinction. It begins by defining futility (العبث) as that which is devoid of benefits. God's actions are not futile because they are full of wisdom and benefits for creatures. However, these benefits are not motivating causes (أسباب باعثة) that prompt God to act, nor are they efficient causes (علل مقتضية) for His agency. Therefore, they are not purposes (أغراض) or final causes (علل غائية) for His actions, as that would imply God seeks completion through them. Instead, they are ends (غايات) and benefits (منافع) that are effects of His actions. The passage then explains the term 'باعث' (motive): it is something intended with the primary intention, and the action is done to attain it. God's actions are not like that because He is entirely self-sufficient and acts without need, so His actions are intended primarily without being motivated by external benefits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That benefits in creation imply that God has purposes like human purposes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that benefits in God's actions are ends and effects, not motivating purposes, because God is self-sufficient and does not act to gain anything.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the benefits in God's actions?
A) They are the ultimate purpose of His actions
B) They are ends and benefits, not purposes
C) They are the motive for His action
D) They are unnecessary

Key Takeaway
God's actions are full of benefits, but these are ends and effects, not motivating purposes, thereby preserving His transcendence and self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think God does things for you because He needs your worship or love. If He is self-sufficient, what then is the real reason you should worship Him? How does that change your motivation?
?
Look at your own actions—how many are done to gain something for yourself? How does the distinction between God's actions and your own reveal your dependence and His independence? What would it mean to act without seeking personal benefit, even for a moment?
?
If every benefit in your life is an effect of God's action, not a reason for it, then what does that say about your entitlement to those benefits? Are you ready to receive them as pure gifts, not as payments you are owed?

Reward as Favor and Its Independence from Hardship
Counter-objection denying ugliness and proportionality of reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponent responds to the claim that reward without merit is ugly, and why reward is not proportional to hardship.

Core Idea
Reward is a divine favor, not compensation for effort.

In this passage, the author presents a counter-objection to the earlier argument that requiring reward without merit would be ugly. The response has two prongs: first, ugliness does not apply to God; second, reward is not tied to hardship but to benefit. This lesson unpacks both parts and examines the examples given.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the use of 'لا نسلم' (we do not agree) as a typical rebuttal.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Look for the two distinct points: the denial of ugliness in bestowing reward, and the claim that reward can be given without great hardship.
The second part begins with 'وإن سلم' (even if granted).
3
Examine the examples
The shahada example and the word that saves a prophet show that reward is not proportional to effort.
These examples demonstrate that benefit matters more than difficulty.
4
Understand the hadith interpretation
The hadith 'the best deeds are the most strenuous' is qualified to apply only when benefits are equal.
This preserves the principle that ease can have more reward if more beneficial.
5
Reflect on the overall aim
The passage defends divine wisdom in giving reward easily and shows that obligations are not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال لهم: لا نسلم أن التفضل بالثواب (قبيح بل لا قبح هناك أصلا ولو سلم قبحه فإنما يقبح ممن يجوز عليه الانتفاع والتضرر لا من الله تعالى فإنه يجوز أن يتفضل به ) كما تفضل ) على عباده ( بما لا يحصى من النعم في الدنيا ) . وأنت خبير بأن المستقبح عندهم هو التفضل بالتعظيم الموعود دون النعم كما صورناه لكنه سند للمنع فلا يجدي دفعه ( وإن سلم تعالى أيضاً فيمكن التعريض (له أي للثواب ( بدون هذه المشاق ) العظيمة (إذ ليس الثواب على قدر المشقة وعوضاً مساوياً لها، (ألا ترى في أن التلفظ بكلمة الشهادة من الثواب ما ليس في كثير من العبادات الشاقة ) كالصلاة والصيام ) وكذا الكلمة المتضمنة لإنجاء نبي من ظالم يريد إهلاكه (أو تمهيد قاعدة خير أو دفع شر عام)، إذ يستحق بهذه الكلمة من الثواب مايزيد على ثواب كثير من العبادات وإن كانت أشق منها . ( وما يروى ) من ( أن أفضل العبادات أحمزها ) أي أشقها ( فذلك عند التساوي في المصالح ) فلا ينافي أن يكون الأخف الأسهل أكثر ثواباً إذا كان أكثر مصلحة وأعظم فائدة، وإذا أمكن التعريض المذكور بدون تلك المشاق كان التكليف بها عاريا عن الغرض .
So it is said to them: We do not agree that bestowing reward is ugly; rather, there is no ugliness there at all. And even if its ugliness were granted, it would only be ugly from one to whom benefit and harm apply, not from God, exalted, for He may bestow it just as He has bestowed on His servants countless blessings in this world. And you are aware that what they consider ugly is the bestowal of the promised glorification, not blessings, as we have illustrated. But it is a point for denial, so its repelling is useless. And even if we grant it, exalted, it is also possible to expose [the servant] to reward without these great hardships, since reward is not proportional to hardship nor an equivalent compensation for it. Do you not see that uttering the word of testimony has a reward that is not found in many difficult acts of worship like prayer and fasting. Likewise, a word that saves a prophet from an oppressor who wants to destroy him, or the establishment of a principle of good or the repelling of general evil—through this word, one merits a reward that exceeds the reward of many acts of worship even if they are more difficult. And what is narrated, that the best acts of worship are the most strenuous, that is when the benefits are equal. So it does not contradict that the lighter and easier act may have more reward if it has more benefit and greater advantage. And if the aforementioned exposure is possible without those hardships, then the obligation of them is devoid of purpose.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a counter-objection. The opponent responds to the earlier answer by saying 'we do not agree' (لا نسلم) that bestowing reward without prior merit is ugly. They argue that ugliness only applies to beings that can benefit or be harmed, not to God. God can bestow reward as a favor just as He bestows worldly blessings. The perceived ugliness is only about the specific honor (التعظيم), not blessings in general, but that argument does not salvage the opponent's position. Next, even if it were ugly, God could still expose the servant to reward without these heavy hardships, because reward is not proportional to hardship. Examples: uttering the shahada yields more reward than many difficult acts like prayer and fasting; a word that saves a prophet or establishes a good principle yields great reward. The hadith that 'the best deeds are the most strenuous' is interpreted as applying when the benefits are equal. Thus, if reward can be attained easily, then imposing difficult obligations is purposeless.

Key Terms
التفضل بالثواب
قبح
المشقة
كلمة الشهادة
أفضل العبادات أحمزها
المصالح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reward must be proportional to the amount of hardship endured.
Correction: The author shows that reward depends on benefit and divine favor, not on effort. A small act like uttering shahada can have immense reward.

Checkpoint
What example does the author give to show that reward is not proportional to hardship?
A) The reward for prayer is greater than fasting
B) Uttering the shahada has more reward than many difficult acts
C) The reward for Hajj is the greatest
D) Difficult acts always have more reward

Key Takeaway
Reward is a divine favor that can be granted without hardship, as reward is based on benefit, not effort.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you avoid easy acts of worship (like a good word) thinking them less valuable, while exhausting yourself with heavy rituals? If a single sincere word outweighs years of prayer, what small deed will you commit to today that you have been neglecting?
?
If reward is purely a favor from God, not a wage for your effort, then where in your heart do you feel entitled to complain about your struggles in worship? Will you repent of that entitlement and instead ask for the grace to be grateful for the privilege of worship?
?
The passage says God can grant reward without any hardship—you could have been created in paradise instantly. Yet you are here, with trials. If you truly grasped that your hardships are not the currency of reward, would you still resent the difficulties in your life, or would you see them as opportunities for a different kind of generosity from God?

Sins Before Prophethood: The Three Views
Sanusi enumerates three positions on prophetic sin before prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main positions on whether prophets can commit sins before receiving revelation, and the reasoning each school uses to support its view.

Core Idea
Sins before prophethood are debated based on miracle, reason, and social effect.

We have already discussed the infallibility of prophets after prophethood. But what about before they became prophets? Scholars differ on whether a future prophet could commit sins, especially major ones, before being sent. This passage from Sanusi presents the three main positions: the Jumhur (majority Ash'aris and some Mu'tazila), most Mu'tazila, and the Rawafid (Shi'a). Each has a distinct argument, as we will see.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The passage begins by stating that the previous discussion applies after revelation and prophethood. Now it turns to the period before prophethood.
2
Identify the Jumhur's view
The Jumhur holds that major sins are not impossible before prophethood because there is no evidence from the miracle, reason, or revelation to forbid them.
This does not mean they condone sin, only that it is not impossible.
3
Identify the Mu'tazila's view
Most Mu'tazila argue that major sins are impossible because they would cause aversion, preventing people from following the prophet and defeating the purpose of the mission. Some extend this to any repulsive quality, even if not a sin.
4
Identify the Rawafid's view
The Rawafid hold that prophets are innocent of all sins, major and minor, before and after prophethood, even unintentionally or by error.
This is the most stringent position.
5
Reflect on the diversity
Consider how each view uses different principles: absence of evidence, social utility, and absolute transcendence.
These differences reflect broader theological commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( هذا كله بعد الوحي والاتصاف بالنبوة ( وأما قبله فقال الجمهور : ) أي أكثر أصحابنا وجمع من المعتزلة ( لا يمتنع أن يصدر عنهم كبيرة إذ لا دلالة للمعجزة عليه ) أي على امتناع الكبيرة قبل البعثة ) ولا حكم للعقل بامتناعها ولا دلالة سمعية عليه أيضاً ) وقال أكثر المعتزلة : تمتنع الكبيرة وإن تاب منها لأنه أي الكبيرة ( يوجب النفرة ) عمن ارتكبها ( وهي تمنع عن اتباعه فتفوت مصلحة البعثة ومنهم من منع عما ينفر الطباع عن متابعتهم ( مطلقاً ) أي سواء لم يكن ذنباً لهم أو كان (كعهر الأمهات إي كونها زانيات ) والفجور في الآباء ) ودناءتهم واسترذالهم ) والصغائر الخسيسة دون غيرها من الصغائر . ( وقالت الروافض : لا يجوز عليهم صغيرة ولا كبيرة ) لا عمداً ولا سهواً ولا خطأ في التأويل بل هم مبرؤون عنها قبل الوحي ( فكيف بعد الوحي .
(All this is after revelation and being described as prophethood. As for before it, the majority said – meaning most of our companions and a group of the Mu'tazila – it is not impossible that a major sin occurs from them, since the miracle does not indicate its impossibility before the mission, and there is no rational judgment of its impossibility, nor is there any transmitted evidence for it either. And most of the Mu'tazila said: a major sin is impossible, even if they repent from it, because it causes aversion from the one who committed it, and it prevents following him, thus the benefit of the mission is lost. And among them is one who prohibited whatever repels natural dispositions from following them absolutely – meaning whether it was not a sin for them or was such as the fornication of mothers (i.e., their being adulteresses) and immorality in fathers, their lowliness and vileness – and base minor sins without other minor sins. And the Rawafid said: no minor or major sin is permissible for them, neither intentionally nor forgetfully nor by error in interpretation; rather, they are innocent of them before revelation, so how about after revelation?)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage discusses whether prophets can sin before receiving revelation and entering prophethood. The author first states that the previous discussion applied after revelation. For the period before, the majority (Jumhur, consisting of most Ash'aris and some Mu'tazila) hold that major sins are not impossible, because the miracle confirming prophethood does not cover that period, and there is no rational or textual proof of impossibility. Most Mu'tazila, however, consider major sins impossible even before prophethood, because a major sin would cause people to shun the prophet, defeating the purpose of sending him. Some go further and forbid anything that naturally repels people, even if not a sin, such as having immoral parents or being of low birth. Base minor sins are also prohibited, but not other minor sins. The Rawafid (Shi'a) hold that prophets are immune from all sins, major and minor, intentionally or forgetfully, before and after prophethood.

Key Terms
قبل النبوة
كبيرة
نفرة
بعثة
روافض
جمهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jumhur's view that major sins are possible before prophethood means they deny prophetic infallibility entirely.
Correction: The Jumhur's view is based on absence of evidence for impossibility, not a denial of infallibility after prophethood. They affirm infallibility during prophethood.

Checkpoint
Why does the Jumhur (majority) allow major sins before prophethood?
A) Because there is no miracle to prevent them
B) Because prophets are not yet obligated
C) Because all sins are forgiven before prophethood
D) Because reason requires it

Key Takeaway
The possibility of sin before prophethood is a matter of debate, with the Jumhur allowing it due to lack of evidence, the Mu'tazila prohibiting it due to social consequences, and the Rawafid extending infallibility to before revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that a major sin would cause aversion and prevent people from following a prophet. Is there any habit or sin in your own life that, if known, would cause others to reject the truth you carry? What does this lesson teach you about the importance of personal integrity in inviting others to Islam?
?
The Jumhur view allows that a prophet could have sinned before prophethood, yet he was still chosen. Have you ever dismissed a person's message because of their past mistakes? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to judge based on someone's history?
?
The Rawafid extend infallibility to before revelation, asserting complete purity. If you truly believed that Allah preserves His messengers from all sin, how would that change your trust in the Quran and Sunnah? Does your heart fully accept that the Prophet ﷺ never sinned, even before his mission, or do you harbor doubts?

Proof from the Prostration of Angels to Adam
First argument for preferring prophets over angels using the prostration story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the superiority of prophets over angels, grounded in the prostration of angels to Adam, and how the objections are refuted using Quranic context.

Core Idea
The prostration of angels to Adam establishes his superiority as the highest service, confirmed by God's silence on Iblis's objection.

This lesson presents the first of several arguments for the preference of prophets over angels. The author uses the well-known story of the angels prostrating to Adam to argue that Adam—and by extension, all prophets—are superior to angels. However, the argument faces several objections, which are meticulously answered. By studying this passage, you will see how rational and textual evidence combine in kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the full argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: claim, objection, answer.
Pay attention to the parentheses in the original text; they indicate the author's own commentary.
2
Identify the core claim
Focus on the sentence starting with 'لأن السجود أعظم أنوع الخدمة...' This encapsulates the rational premise: prostration is the highest service and using a superior for an inferior is rationally unacceptable.
The term 'إخدام' (use) is key; it implies commanding someone to serve.
3
Understand the objections
The passage lists three possibilities that could undermine the argument: prostration as worship to God with Adam as qibla, prostration as a customary greeting, and prostration as a test. Consider why each is raised.
These objections rely on cultural or contextual interpretations.
4
Examine the refutation
The author answers by citing Iblis's words (Quran 17:62) and the principle that God's narration without rebuke validates the interpretation. See how this refutes the objections.
The principle used is a well-known method in tafsir and usul.
