Adam is better than them. The First Observatory - the Eighth Aim: Preference of Prophets over Angels. Because prostration is the greatest type of service, and using the better for the lesser is not accepted by intellects. And if it were so, other prophets would be likewise since there is no one who says [they are] better. It cannot be said that prostration occurs in various ways; perhaps it was not a prostration of veneration for him, for it is possible that their prostration was to God and Adam was like a direction of prayer for them. And on the supposition that it was for Adam, it is possible that they knew in the prostration that it was standing in place of greeting, so they honored him, so it would not be an extreme in humility and service because this is a customary matter that may differ with different times. Also, it is possible that He commanded them to prostrate as a test to distinguish the obedient from the disobedient, so it does not indicate his preference over them in any of these possibilities. We say: His statement: 'Do you see this one whom You have honored above me? I am better than him; You created me from fire and him from clay' [Al-Isra 17:62] indicates that it was a prostration of honor and preference, and negates all other possibilities, since there was nothing there to direct the honor except the command to prostrate. His statement: 'And the use of the better for the lesser is not accepted by intellects' - and as for his saying, peace be upon him, 'The leader of a people is their servant,' it is metaphorical and figurative, for the servant is like the leader in managing affairs and interests. And it may be answered that service from oneself is not the command to serve, which is the meaning of 'use', and therein lies the discussion. The truth is that obligation differs according to different interests and times, and therefore laws differ; perhaps the interest at the time of Adam's caliphate was the angels' prostration to him, and at another time the opposite. And this may be supported by the fact that the wisdom in commanding them to prostrate, as narrated in commentaries, is to manifest what is in their hearts of submission and surrender, and that only appears by commanding the better or equal. His statement: 'And also it is possible, etc.' - And also it is possible that there is another wisdom we do not know. His statement: 'We say' - it is said: This only indicates that Iblis (may God curse him) understood this honor, so perhaps He commanded what is understood as honor as a test, not as honor. And if it were valid, the evidence would be this statement, not the command to prostrate. Its answer is what I heard from the master investigator: that God's custom is that when He relates speech from a people and does not rebuke them, it indicates its correspondence with reality. And with this he refuted those who say that Lot's people were requesting him to marry his daughters and he was abstaining, due to God's narration of their words without rebuke: 'You know we have no right to your daughters.' And when it is established by this that the command to prostrate was for honor, then the very command to do so is evidence for the desired conclusion, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the superiority of prophets over angels, based on the story of the prostration of angels to Adam. It begins by stating the argument: prostration is the highest form of service, and using a superior being for an inferior one is intellectually unacceptable. Then it addresses objections: that the prostration might not have been one of veneration, or that it was a test. The answer refutes these by citing Iblis's words in the Quran, indicating that he understood it as an honor, and God did not deny it, establishing it as such. The passage then provides commentary clarifying that the Prophet's saying 'the leader is the servant' is metaphorical, and that obligations vary with times. It also notes that the test explanation is not sufficient because there could be other hidden wisdoms. Finally, it reinforces that God's narration of Iblis's words without rebuke proves the correct understanding.

Key Terms
سجود
تكريم
إخدام
ابتلاء
قضية عرفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that prostration can be interpreted in various ways, but the Quranic context shows it was specifically for honor.
Correction: The author argues that Iblis's understanding of the prostration as an honor, and God's silence on that understanding, proves that the prostration was indeed an act of honor, not a test or other form.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the prostration of angels to Adam proves Adam's superiority?
A) Because prostration is always an act of worship.
B) Because Iblis's objection shows he understood it as honor, and God did not rebuke him.
C) Because the angels were forced to prostrate.
D) Because Adam was the first human.

Key Takeaway
The prostration of angels to Adam, understood through Iblis's acknowledgment and God's silent endorsement, proves the superiority of prophets over angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that it is irrational for a superior to serve an inferior. In your daily interactions, do you ever treat those you consider 'lesser' with condescension? How can you emulate the angels' humility in prostrating to Adam, despite your own perceived status?
?
Iblis understood the command as honor but refused out of pride. When you receive a command from Allah that challenges your pride—such as apologizing to someone you look down on—do you obey or do you rationalize like Iblis? What specific step can you take today to submit despite your ego?
?
The passage says obligations vary with time and interest. Are there any practices you have dismissed as 'not relevant today' that you need to reconsider? If a command came directly to you, as it did to the angels, would you obey without question?

The Third Proof: Identity Requires Differentiation and Its Refutation
Al-Iji presents the third proof requiring differentiation in non-existence and gives two answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof against bodily resurrection that requires differentiation in non-existence, and the two theological responses (Mu'tazili and Ash'ari) that refute it.

Core Idea
Resurrection does not require real distinction in non-existence; distinction is imaginary.

This lesson presents the third argument against the possibility of returning the same body: that identity would require differentiation in non-existence, which is impossible. Al-Iji then gives two answers, one based on Mu'tazili principles and one on Ash'ari principles, showing how the argument fails. Understanding this debate clarifies the Ash'ari position on the nature of non-existence and possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
The third proof states that for the resurrected body to be identical to the original, the original must have been distinguished in non-existence. But non-existence is pure negation with no distinctions. Therefore the identity judgment is impossible.
Notice that the argument relies on the impossibility of differentiation in non-existence.
2
Examine the conditional premise
The soundness of the identity judgment requires that the non-existent be qualified with the possibility of return, which entails differentiation; otherwise the qualification would apply equally to all non-existents.
This conditional shows why the Mu'tazila might accept the antecedent but reject the impossibility.
3
Understand the Mu'tazili answer
On Mu'tazili principles (non-existent is a thing), the impossibility is not conceded because non-existents can have real attributes and distinctions.
This answer is considered apparent from their perspective.
4
Understand the Ash'ari answer
On Ash'ari principles, the conditional is denied; the judgment does not require external differentiation because the possibility of return is a mental attribute. Real distinction occurs only at the time of re-creation.
The distinction in non-existence is imaginary, like that of possibles that never existed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث الحكم الصحيح ( بأن هذا الذي وجد الآن ( عين الأول يستدعي تميزه حال العدم، وأنه ) أي التميز حال العدم ( محال ) لأن النفي الصرف لا تميز وأما الشرطية فلأن صحة ذلك الحكم تستدعي اتصاف ذلك المعدوم حال عدمه بصحة العود إذ لو لم يتصف بصحة العود، لما أمكن عوده فلا يصح ذلك الحكم عليه واتصافه بصحة العود يقتضي امتيازه وإلا لم يكن ذلك الاتصاف أولى به من غيره، (الجواب : على أصل المعتزلة وهو كون المعدوم شيئاً ) أي أمراً ثابتاً متقرراً حال العدم (ظاهر)، لأن إبطال التالي حينئذ ممنوع وما ذكر في بيانه مردود (و) الجواب على أصلنا) منع الشرطية (لأنا) نمنع استدعاءه ) أي استدعاء ذلك الحكم وصحته (للتميز) في الخارج فإن صحة العود صفة اعتبارية هي امكان الوجود بعد زواله فلا يكون الاتصاف بها مقتضياً للامتياز الخارجي، بل التميز في الخارج (إنما يحصل حال الإعادة أعني زمان الوجود الثاني (وهو) أي التميز الحاصل للمعدوم حال عدمه، واتصافه بصحة العود ) أمر وهمي لا حقيقة (له بحسب الخارج كالتميز الحاصل في الممكنات التي لم توجد بعد فإن قيل: نحن ندعي لزوم هذا التميز قلنا فبطلانه ممنوع حينئذ لأن مثل هذا التميز حاصل للمعدومات الصرفة كالممتنعات .
The third (is) the correct judgment that this which exists now is identical to the first requires its differentiation in the state of non-existence, and that (i.e., differentiation in the state of non-existence) is impossible because pure negation has no differentiation. As for the conditional, because the soundness of that judgment requires that that non-existent in the state of its non-existence be qualified by the possibility of return, for if it were not qualified by the possibility of return, its return would not be possible, so that judgment would not be sound concerning it. And its being qualified by the possibility of return entails its differentiation, otherwise that qualification would not be more appropriate to it than to another. The answer: according to the principle of the Mu'tazila, which is that the non-existent is a thing, i.e., a fixed, established matter in the state of non-existence, it is apparent, because refuting the consequent then is not admitted, and what is mentioned in its explanation is rejected. And the answer according to our principle (Ash'ari) is to deny the conditional, because we deny that it (i.e., that judgment and its soundness) requires differentiation in external existence. For the possibility of return is a relational attribute which is the possibility of existence after its removal, so being qualified by it does not necessitate external differentiation. Rather, external differentiation only occurs in the state of re-creation, i.e., the time of the second existence. And the differentiation that occurs for the non-existent in the state of non-existence and its being qualified by the possibility of return is an imaginary thing with no reality in terms of external existence, like the differentiation that occurs for possibles that have not yet existed. If it is said: we claim the necessity of this differentiation, we say: its falsehood is then not admitted because such differentiation exists for pure non-existents like impossibles.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the possibility of resurrection based on the need for differentiation in non-existence, and then gives two responses. The argument: If the resurrected body is identical to the original, then that identity judgment requires that the non-existent original be distinguished in non-existence. But non-existence is pure negation and has no distinctions. Therefore the judgment is impossible. The conditional premise is that the identity judgment requires the non-existent to be qualified with the possibility of return. That qualification would necessitate differentiation, otherwise it would apply equally to all. The first answer (on Mu'tazili principles) accepts that the non-existent is a thing, so it can have distinction, thus the impossibility is not conceded. The second answer (on Ash'ari principles) denies the conditional; the judgment does not require external differentiation because the possibility of return is a mental attribute, and real distinction occurs only at the time of second existence. The distinction in non-existence is merely imaginary, like the distinction of possibles that never existed. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
الحكم الصحيح
التميز
صحة العود
المعدوم
المعتزلة
الأشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument requires actual distinction in non-existence, and that the Ash'ari response concedes some real distinction.
Correction: The Ash'ari response clarifies that the differentiation is purely imaginary and has no external reality; real distinction occurs only at the time of re-creation.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response to the third proof, what is the nature of the differentiation of the non-existent before re-creation?
A) It is a real external distinction.
B) It is an imaginary distinction with no external reality.
C) It is impossible.
D) It is a necessary attribute.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response shows that the differentiation needed for identity is merely imaginary, not real external distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you believe that your body, now decaying or scattered, will be recreated as the same 'you'? What doubts about Allah's power over non-existence linger in your heart that this argument and its refutation must uproot?
?
If the Ash'ari say that the possibility of return is merely a mental attribute, does that make the resurrection less real for you? How does your trust in Allah's promise anchor your certainty beyond philosophical distinctions?
?
When you consider the concept of 'pure negation' for things that have perished, does it frighten you? How can you actively cultivate a hope that does not depend on imagining a continued existence in non-existence, but rather on the sheer power of the One who brings forth from nothing?

Defending the Denial of the Beatific Vision
Refutes the claim that denying the beatific vision is disbelief based on Quran 32:10.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila defended themselves against the charge of disbelief by interpreting 'liqa'' in Quran 32:10 as metaphorical, referring to reward rather than a literal vision of God.

Core Idea
Denying the beatific vision is not disbelief because the Quranic term 'meeting' is metaphorical.

In this passage, the author responds to the accusation that the Mu'tazila are disbelievers for denying the beatific vision. He examines the key Quranic verse cited against them and shows that the word 'liqa'' does not literally mean seeing God, but rather reaching the reward. This lesson demonstrates the importance of precise linguistic analysis in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the author structures his argument: accusation, Quranic evidence, counter-argument, and conclusion.
Pay attention to the key term 'liqa'' and its interpretation.
2
Identify the accusation and the Quranic proof
The accusation is that denying the vision (ru'ya) is kufr. The proof is Quran 32:10 which condemns those who disbelieve in the 'meeting with their Lord'. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the metaphorical interpretation
The author argues that 'liqa'' literally means physical contact, which is impossible for God, so it must be metaphorical. The consensus of exegetes is that it means reaching the abode of reward. See how this refutes the accusation.
This step is crucial for grasping the lesson's main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إنكارهم الرؤية وقد دل القرآن على أن منكرها كافر لأنه قال تعالى : { بل هم بلقاء ربهم كافرون } [السجدة : ١٠]، قلنا : اللقاء حقيقة في الالتقاء والوصول إلى مماسة الشيء وذلك محال في حقه تعالى فتعين أنه في الآية مجاز، فلعل المراد به لقاء ثواب الله لا رؤيته فإن المفسرين كلهم قالوا : المراد به الوصول إلى دار الثواب.
The sixth: their denial of the vision (ar-ru'yah), and the Quran has indicated that its denier is a disbeliever because He, exalted, said: 'But they are disbelievers in the meeting with their Lord' [Sajda 32:10]. We said: Meeting (liqa') in its literal sense means encountering and reaching physical contact, and that is impossible with respect to God, so it must be metaphorical in the verse. Perhaps what is meant by it is meeting the reward of God, not seeing Him, for all the interpreters said: what is meant is reaching the abode of reward.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage addresses the sixth accusation: that the Mu'tazila are disbelievers because they deny the beatific vision (ru'ya) of God in the afterlife. The accuser cites verse 32:10 which mentions 'liqa' rabbihim' (meeting with their Lord). The response argues that 'liqa'' literally means physical meeting and contact, which is impossible for God due to His transcendence. Therefore, the term must be metaphorical. The consensus of the exegetes is that it refers to reaching the abode of reward (dar al-thawab), not seeing God's essence. Thus, denying the literal vision does not make one a disbeliever.

Key Terms
رؤية
لقاء
مجاز
دار الثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people believe that the Quranic term 'liqa' in 32:10 literally means seeing God, and thus denying the beatific vision is kufr.
Correction: The author shows that 'liqa' literally means physical meeting, which is impossible for God, so it must be metaphorical. The consensus of exegetes is that it means reaching the reward, not seeing God.

Checkpoint
How does the author interpret the word 'liqa'' in Quran 32:10 to avoid declaring the Mu'tazila disbelievers?
A) As a literal vision
B) As meeting the reward of God
C) As spiritual union
D) As physical approach

Key Takeaway
The denial of the beatific vision does not constitute disbelief because the Quranic term 'meeting' is metaphorical for reaching reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain that someone's belief was false because of a Quranic verse you took literally, only to later learn it was metaphorical? This lesson forces you to confront that our own certainty can be misplaced. Can you honestly say you have never judged another Muslim's faith based on your own limited understanding of the Quran? What will you do to ensure you do not repeat that error?
?
The author here defends the Mu'tazila from the charge of kufr over a theological disagreement. Think about your own life: Are there any Muslims you have secretly considered less faithful due to differences in secondary matters? This lesson calls you to humility. Are you willing to reconsider your judgments and learn the deeper interpretations before condemning? What specific step will you take today to cultivate that humility?

Reasons for Declaring Anthropomorphists Disbelievers and Their Refutations
Sanusi refutes three grounds for takfir of the Mujassima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments used to declare anthropomorphists (Mujassima) disbelievers and the refutations of those arguments based on the distinction between erroneous belief about God and outright polytheism.

Core Idea
Erroneous beliefs about God do not automatically make one a polytheist.

Sanusi presents three grounds on which some scholars declared the Mujassima (anthropomorphists) disbelievers. Each is carefully refuted, showing that while they hold incorrect beliefs about God's attributes, they do not worship another deity. This distinction is crucial in the science of takfir.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to get an overview of the three arguments and their refutations.
Notice that the first argument is only mentioned and its response is referenced from earlier.
2
List the three arguments for takfir
Identify each of the three grounds: ignorance, worship of other-than-God (like idol worshippers), and making a body a god (like Christians).
3
Examine the refutation for each
For each argument, see how Sanusi responds. The second argument's refutation is detailed: the Mujassim worships God, not a body. The third refutation follows the same principle.
4
Distinguish between Mujassima and polytheists
Understand that the Mujassima believe in God as Creator, Provider, etc., but attribute to Him what is not permissible. They do not set up another god, so takfir does not apply.
This is the core lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : من أبحاث التكفير قد كفر المجسمة بوجوه : الأول : إن تجسمه جهل به وقد مر جوابه . الثاني : أنه عابد لغير الله فيكون كافراً كعابد الصنم، قلنا : ليس المجسم عابداً لغير الله بل هو معتقد في الله الخالق الرازق العالم القادر ما لا يجوز عليه مما قد جاء به الشرع على تأويل ولم يؤله فلا يلزم كفره، بخلاف عابد الصنم فإنه عابد لغير الله حقيقة . الثالث : لقد كفر الذين قالوا إن هو المسيح ابن مريم وما ذلك الكفر إلا لأنهم جعلوا غير الله إلها فلزم الشرط وهؤلاء المجسمة كذلك لأنهم جعلوا الجسم الذي هو غير الله إلها، قلنا: ما ذكرتموه ممنوع والمستند ما تقدم من أنه اعتقد في الله ما لا يجوز عليه فلم يجعل غير الله إلهاً حتى يكون مشركاً.
The third: From the discussions of takfir: they declared the Mujassima (anthropomorphists) disbelievers on several grounds: The first: Their embodying God is ignorance of Him, and its answer has already passed. The second: That he worships other than God, so he is a disbeliever like a worshipper of an idol. We say: The Mujassim is not worshipping other than God, but rather he believes in God, the Creator, the Provider, the All-Knowing, the All-Powerful, with what is not permissible for Him according to what has come from the Shari'ah through interpretation, and he does not take a god besides Him, so disbelief does not apply to him, unlike the worshipper of an idol who worships other than God in reality. The third: Indeed, those who said that the Messiah is the son of Mary were disbelievers, and that disbelief is only because they made other than God a god, so shirk applies. And these [Mujassima] likewise, because they make the body, which is other than God, a god. We say: What you have mentioned is denied, and the basis is that which preceded: he believes in God what is not permissible for Him, so he does not make other than God a god, such that he would be a polytheist.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage covers three arguments used to declare the Mujassima disbelievers, each with a refutation. First, that they are ignorant of God—but ignorance alone does not warrant takfir. Second, that they are like idol worshippers—the response distinguishes: the Mujassim worships God, albeit with erroneous conception, while idol worshippers worship something else. Third, they are compared to Christians who deify Jesus—the response says they do not regard the body as a god; they merely attribute to God what is not permissible, which does not make them polytheists.

Key Terms
التجسيم
المجسمة
عابد الصنم
إلها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anthropomorphists are polytheists because they describe God with bodily attributes.
Correction: They are not polytheists because they still worship God alone, even if their description is erroneous.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the Mujassima not like idol worshippers?
A) They are ignorant and excused
B) They worship God and do not take another deity
C) They follow the Shari'ah exactly
D) They are not aware of their error

Key Takeaway
The Mujassima are not declared disbelievers because their error is in describing God, not in worshipping another deity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed another Muslim's faith because they had a flawed understanding of God? How does this lesson challenge that impulse, and what will you do the next time you encounter such a person?
?
This lesson teaches that the Mujassima were not declared disbelievers because their error was in describing God, not in worshipping something else. Where in your own life might you be quick to judge another's beliefs or actions as disbelief when their real mistake is something less severe? Commit to one concrete change in how you assess others.
?
The refutations in this passage rest on distinguishing between believing in God with wrong attributes versus worshipping another being. How often do you examine your own heart to ensure that your worship is directed solely to the One God, even if your understanding of Him is incomplete? What step will you take this week to deepen both your knowledge and your sincerity?
